Official links:
- NCVO: Writing a budget
- NCVO: Direct costs and support costs
50. Q: What is the difference between input-based and output-based budgeting?
A: Input-based budgeting focuses on the resources the charity will buy, such as salaries, supplies, travel, rent, or services. Output-based budgeting starts with what those resources are meant to produce, such as people served, workshops delivered, cases closed, or facilities maintained. The first method is useful for control and accounting detail, while the second helps leadership see whether spending is creating the intended result. Strong charities often use both together: inputs for control, outputs for management, and the link between them for accountability.
Send to the team:
- “Please show both what we spend and what that spending is meant to deliver.”
Key point:
- Output-based budgeting links cost to results, while input-based budgeting tracks the resources used.
Official links:
- NCVO: Writing a budget
- NCVO: Project budgeting and full cost recovery
51. Q: What is the purpose of budget review meetings?
A: Budget review meetings are the regular control point where management compares the approved plan with actual performance and asks whether the organization is still on course. They are not just reporting sessions. They are decision sessions where leaders examine whether the assumptions behind the budget still hold, whether spending is staying within limits, whether activities are moving at the expected pace, and whether any corrective action is needed. In a nonprofit, the value of the meeting is that it links money, delivery, and accountability in one conversation, so staff can see whether the organization is using resources in the way the board, donor, and management expected. Good budget reviews also improve discipline because they force timely action instead of waiting until year-end to explain problems.
Send to the team:
- “Please review the budget against actuals and note any action items.”
Key point:
- Budget reviews turn financial data into management action.
Official links:
- 2 CFR 200.302 Financial management
- 2 CFR 200.329 Monitoring and reporting program performance
- GAO Green Book
52. Q: What should be checked during budget review?
A: A proper budget review should check more than one number on a page. It should look at income received or confirmed, spending to date, commitments that are already approved but not yet paid, the current cash position, and the delivery status of the program itself. That matters because a budget can appear acceptable while hidden commitments or delayed receipts are creating pressure underneath. The review should also test whether the spending pattern matches the activity plan. For example, if half the budget period has passed but only a small share of planned work has been completed, the issue may not be finance alone; it may be implementation delay, procurement blockage, staffing gaps, or donor timing. The purpose is to see the whole picture early enough to respond intelligently.
Send to the team:
- “Please check income, spend, commitments, and cash before the review meeting.”
Key point:
- Review both financial results and delivery progress.
Official links:
- 2 CFR 200.302 Financial management
- 2 CFR 200.303 Internal controls
- 2 CFR 200.329 Monitoring and reporting program performance
53. Q: What is a budget variance?
A: A budget variance is the difference between what the organization planned and what actually happened, either in income, expense, or both. It is one of the main tools for understanding whether the budget is still reliable. A variance on its own is not automatically a problem; it is simply a signal that reality has moved away from the original assumption. Some variances are expected, such as timing shifts or seasonal spending. Others reveal deeper issues, such as under-collection of donor income, uncontrolled cost growth, or poor planning. In nonprofit management, the variance becomes useful only when it is explained in context, because leadership needs to know not just that a line is off, but what it means for the project, the cash position, and the remaining work.
Send to the team:
- “Please calculate the variance for each major line item.”
Key point:
- Variance shows the gap between plan and reality.
Official links:
- 2 CFR 200.328 Financial reporting
- 2 CFR 200.329 Monitoring and reporting program performance
- GAO Green Book
54. Q: Why do budget variances happen?
A: Budget variances happen because the assumptions built into the plan no longer match what is actually happening. This can happen for many reasons: timing differences between when costs were expected and when they were incurred, inflation or price changes, staff vacancies or replacements, donor disbursement delays, implementation changes, scope revisions, or external shocks such as exchange-rate movement. In practice, the real cause is often a combination of factors rather than a single issue. For that reason, the review should not stop at the number. It should identify whether the variance is caused by timing, volume, price, or scope, because each driver suggests a different response. Timing variances may self-correct; structural variances may require revised plans, reforecasting, or donor consultation.
Send to the team:
- “Please explain whether the variance is timing, volume, price, or scope related.”
Key point:
- Variances usually come from changed assumptions or timing.
Official links:
- 2 CFR 200.328 Financial reporting
- 2 CFR 200.329 Monitoring and reporting program performance
- 2 CFR 200.305 Federal payment
55. Q: How should a nonprofit explain budget variances?
A: A nonprofit should explain a budget variance in a way that tells the full story, not just the arithmetic. A useful explanation states the size of the variance, the reason it occurred, the operational or financial impact, and the action being taken next. That means the commentary should connect the finance result to the reality behind it. For example, if travel is under budget because a field mission was postponed, the report should say that the delay affected implementation timing and may shift spending into a later period. If salaries are under budget because positions remain vacant, the report should show the hiring impact and the effect on delivery. Strong variance commentary helps managers make decisions and helps donors and boards understand whether the organization is merely under-spending or actually under-delivering.
Send to the team:
- “Please include the cause, impact, and corrective action for each material variance.”
Key point:
- Good variance explanations are specific and actionable.
Official links:
- 2 CFR 200.329 Monitoring and reporting program performance
- GAO Green Book
56. Q: What is favorable variance in NGO budgeting?
A: Favorable variance means the result is better than the budget expected, such as spending less than planned or receiving more income than anticipated. In a nonprofit setting, however, “better” should never be judged only by the accounting number. A lower expense figure is only favorable if the organization still delivered the planned service, met donor conditions, and maintained quality. If the organization spent less because activities were delayed, staff were absent, or output was reduced, then the variance may look good on paper while masking an operational problem. The real test is whether the financial result and the program result both remain acceptable. Favorable variance therefore needs a second question: did the organization achieve the intended mission outcome, or did it simply spend less?
Send to the team:
- “Please confirm that the favorable variance did not hide underdelivery.”
Key point:
- Favorable variance is good only when performance stays on target.
Official links:
- 2 CFR 200.329 Monitoring and reporting program performance
- 2 CFR 200.301 Performance measurement
57. Q: What is unfavorable variance in NGO budgeting?
A: Unfavorable variance means actual performance is worse than budget, such as spending more than planned or collecting less income than expected. In NGO finance, that can create immediate pressure on cash, delivery, or compliance. The key is not to panic at the number but to determine whether the problem is temporary or structural. A temporary unfavorable variance might come from an invoice arriving early or a donor payment arriving late. A structural unfavorable variance might show that the budget was unrealistic, costs were underestimated, or activity levels are higher than planned. Unfavorable variance should trigger prompt review, because the longer it sits unexplained, the more likely it is to become a funding or control problem.
Send to the team:
- “Please identify whether the unfavorable variance is temporary or structural.”
Key point:
- Unfavorable variance needs a prompt explanation and response.
Official links:
- 2 CFR 200.303 Internal controls
- 2 CFR 200.329 Monitoring and reporting program performance
58. Q: How should budget variance analysis be used by management?
A: Management should use variance analysis as a decision tool, not as a purely historical report. The analysis should help leaders spot trends, detect emerging problems, update forecasts, and decide whether to adjust spending, delay hiring, change implementation pace, or seek additional funding. It should also show whether the same issue is repeating across several months, because a recurring pattern is more important than a one-off month. In a healthy organization, variance analysis becomes part of a management rhythm: identify the difference, understand the cause, decide the response, and track the outcome at the next review. That makes the budget a living control tool rather than a static document filed after approval.
Send to the team:
- “Please use variance analysis to update the forecast and action plan.”
Key point:
- Variance analysis should drive management decisions.
Official links:
- 2 CFR 200.303 Internal controls
- GAO Green Book
59. Q: What is budget burn rate?
A: Budget burn rate is the speed at which an organization is using up its budget over time. It is usually measured monthly or quarterly and shows whether spending is moving at a pace that matches the approved plan. In NGO management, burn rate matters because a fast burn can create early exhaustion of funds, while a slow burn can signal under-implementation or delayed procurement. Burn rate is especially useful when viewed alongside the grant period and planned activity milestones. It tells management whether the organization is consuming resources in line with the life of the project. Burn rate is therefore both a financial and operational indicator, because a healthy burn rate should support delivery rather than simply drain cash quickly.
Send to the team:
- “Please show the monthly burn rate alongside the budget.”
Key point:
- Burn rate shows how fast funds are being used.
Official links:
- 2 CFR 200.329 Monitoring and reporting program performance
- 2 CFR 200.302 Financial management
60. Q: Why is budget burn rate important for grant management?
A: Burn rate is important in grant management because it shows whether the grant funds are being consumed at the right pace for the award period and planned outputs. If the burn rate is too slow, the organization may face underspend, delayed delivery, or pressure to reprogram funds near the end of the award. If it is too fast, the organization may run short before the grant period ends. Good grant managers watch burn rate because it helps them protect both compliance and implementation. It also helps leadership decide when to request budget revisions, whether to accelerate activity, or whether to slow spending until donor timing becomes clearer. In that sense, burn rate is a practical bridge between money, timetable, and accountability.
Send to the team:
- “Please monitor burn rate against the grant period and deliverables.”
Key point:
- Burn rate helps prevent underspend and overspend.
Official links:
- 2 CFR 200.308 Revision of budget and program plans
- 2 CFR 200.328 Financial reporting
- 2 CFR 200.329 Monitoring and reporting program performance
61. Q: How should spending pace be compared to the budget?
A: Spending pace should be compared on the same time basis as the budget, so that the analysis is fair and meaningful. That usually means comparing month-to-date against month-to-date, or year-to-date against year-to-date, rather than comparing a full-year budget to a single month of actuals. The comparison should also be linked to delivery progress, because spending at the right pace is only useful if the work itself is progressing at the right pace. If spending is ahead of budget but activities are also ahead, that may be acceptable. If spending is ahead while delivery is behind, the organization needs to investigate immediately. The point of the comparison is to answer whether resources are being used in step with the approved plan.
Send to the team:
- “Please compare cumulative actual spend with cumulative budget and activity progress.”
Key point:
- Compare both time and delivery progress.
Official links:
- 2 CFR 200.329 Monitoring and reporting program performance
- GAO Green Book
62. Q: What is cash planning and how does it relate to the budget?
A: Cash planning is the process of estimating when cash will arrive and when it will need to leave the organization so obligations can be paid on time. It is related to the budget, but it is not the same thing. A budget tells the organization what it expects to earn and spend; a cash plan tells the organization when the money will actually move. That distinction matters because a nonprofit can be budget-positive and still face a cash shortage if donor receipts are delayed or if a major expense comes due before payment arrives. Cash planning therefore turns the budget into a liquidity tool. It helps management see whether the organization can meet payroll, vendor payments, and program commitments without disruption.
Send to the team:
- “Please prepare a cash plan from the budget and expected payment dates.”
Key point:
- Budget and cash plan are related but not identical.
Official links:
- 2 CFR 200.305 Federal payment
- 2 CFR 200.302 Financial management
63. Q: Why should an NGO budget for cash timing as well as expense timing?
A: An NGO should budget for cash timing because the date an expense is recognized is not always the date the cash is needed. A program may approve an expense in one month but pay it in another, and a donor may recognize a commitment well before the funds arrive in the bank. If management focuses only on accounting expense timing, it may miss the actual cash pressure that affects salaries, suppliers, and field operations. Budgeting for cash timing helps the organization avoid payment delays, emergency borrowing, and awkward interruptions in service delivery. It also improves forecasting because it shows whether the organization has enough liquidity to bridge the gap between promise and payment.
Send to the team:
- “Please include expected cash receipts and payment dates in the plan.”
Key point:
- Cash timing protects liquidity.
Official links:
- 2 CFR 200.305 Federal payment
- 2 CFR 200.302 Financial management
64. Q: What is the difference between annual budget and cash forecast?
A: An annual budget is the organization’s approved plan for income and expense across the year. A cash forecast is a timing tool that shows when cash is expected to move in and out during that same period. The budget can be stable while the cash forecast changes frequently, because donor disbursements, vendor payments, payroll dates, and grant reimbursements may shift. In practice, the budget answers, “What do we plan to do?” while the cash forecast answers, “Can we afford to do it when payment is due?” A nonprofit needs both. One supports performance control, and the other supports liquidity control. Together they help management avoid the mistake of confusing accounting results with actual cash availability.
Send to the team:
- “Please keep the annual budget and cash forecast aligned.”
Key point:
- Budget tracks performance; forecast tracks liquidity.
Official links:
- 2 CFR 200.302 Financial management
- 2 CFR 200.305 Federal payment
65. Q: How should grant income be timed in the budget?
A: Grant income should be timed according to the award terms, the expected payment schedule, and any eligibility or condition rules that affect when the funds can actually be drawn or recognized. The budget should not assume that the full grant value arrives on day one unless the award clearly says so. Instead, it should reflect practical timing: advance payment, reimbursement-based payment, milestone-based disbursement, or installment release. This is important because grant income timing affects hiring, procurement, and field delivery. If the organization budgets grant income too early, it may plan spending it cannot yet support. If it budgets too late, it may underuse available resources. Accurate timing makes the budget a realistic management tool rather than an optimistic forecast.
Send to the team:
- “Please schedule grant income based on the award and payment rules.”
Key point:
- Grant timing must follow donor and award rules.
Official links:
- 2 CFR 200.305 Federal payment
- 2 CFR 200.308 Revision of budget and program plans
- 2 CFR 200.403 Factors affecting allowability of costs
66. Q: How should restricted grants be treated in planning?
A: Restricted grants should be planned separately so the organization can see exactly what money is tied to a particular purpose, period, or project. That means restricted income should not be merged loosely into general budget pools where it becomes hard to monitor. The plan should show the permitted use, the expected timing, the specific cost lines it can support, and any reporting obligations connected to it. This separate treatment protects compliance and makes it easier to detect whether spending is still within donor expectations. It also helps management avoid using restricted resources to cover unrelated pressures. In a strong budget process, restricted money is visible from the start rather than being discovered only during donor reporting or audit review.
Send to the team:
- “Please keep restricted grant spending separate from unrestricted activity.”
Key point:
- Restriction controls how the money can be planned and used.
Official links:
- 2 CFR 200.303 Internal controls
- 2 CFR 200.329 Monitoring and reporting program performance
67. Q: How should donor reporting requirements affect budget design?
A: Donor reporting requirements should shape the budget from the beginning, not be added as an afterthought. If a donor wants reporting by activity, location, cost category, or funding period, the budget should be built in that same structure so reporting can be produced without manual rework or unreliable mapping. The reporting deadline should also be reflected in the budget calendar so staff know when data must be ready. This matters because many reporting problems are really budgeting design problems. A budget that does not match the donor report format creates unnecessary reconciliation work and increases the chance of inconsistency. When the budget is designed around the report structure, the finance team can monitor compliance more cleanly and management can respond faster.
Send to the team:
- “Please design the budget in the same structure used for donor reporting.”
Key point:
- Budget structure should support reporting obligations.
Official links:
- 2 CFR 200.328 Financial reporting
- 2 CFR 200.329 Monitoring and reporting program performance
- 2 CFR 200.308 Revision of budget and program plans
68. Q: How should funding gaps be identified during planning?
A: Funding gaps should be identified by comparing the full cost of planned work against confirmed resources by period, not just by looking at the annual total. That means the analysis should show whether enough money is available in the months when costs will actually fall due. A project may look fully funded across twelve months, but still have a serious gap in the first quarter if receipts arrive late. The planning process should therefore check timing, not only totals. It should also distinguish between a real funding gap and a temporary timing gap. That distinction matters because a timing gap might be bridged by short-term cash planning, while a real funding gap may require cost reduction, fundraising, or scope adjustment.
Send to the team:
- “Please compare the plan with confirmed funding month by month.”
Key point:
- Funding gaps are often timing gaps as well as total gaps.
Official links:
- 2 CFR 200.305 Federal payment
- 2 CFR 200.329 Monitoring and reporting program performance
69. Q: What is a funding gap analysis?
A: A funding gap analysis is a planning review that shows where expected costs exceed confirmed resources and how large that difference is. It should not stop at identifying the shortfall. It should also show when the gap occurs, which activities are affected, and what options exist to cover it. Those options may include additional fundraising, delaying nonessential spending, rephasing activity, or seeking donor approval for reprogramming. The analysis is useful because it turns uncertainty into a specific management discussion. Instead of saying “we may have a shortfall,” it says, “We have a shortfall in this period, for this amount, affecting these activities, with these possible responses.” That level of clarity is what makes the analysis actionable.
Send to the team:
- “Please identify the gap, the timing of the gap, and the bridge options.”
Key point:
- Gap analysis turns shortage into an action plan.
Official links:
- 2 CFR 200.305 Federal payment
- GAO Green Book
70. Q: How can an NGO use forecasting to manage funding gaps?
A: An NGO can use forecasting as an early warning tool that helps leadership see a funding gap before it becomes a crisis. Once a gap appears in the forecast, management can update income assumptions, slow or pause nonessential spending, prioritize core deliverables, and intensify fundraising or donor discussion. Forecasting is useful because it creates time for choices. Without it, the organization notices the problem only when cash is already tight or bills are overdue. A good forecast therefore supports resilience as well as planning. It helps management test different scenarios and see which one keeps the organization viable without harming the most important program commitments. In practice, forecast discipline is one of the strongest defenses against avoidable financial stress.
Send to the team:
- “Please update the forecast and show the gap response plan.”
Key point:
- Forecasting gives early warning and time to respond.
Official links:
- 2 CFR 200.301 Performance measurement
- 2 CFR 200.329 Monitoring and reporting program performance
- GAO Green Book
71. Q: What is a staffing plan in the budget process?
A: A staffing plan is the budget’s workforce map. It shows which positions are needed, when they will be filled, what they will cost, and which costs belong to which program, grant, or cost center. It should also show whether a role is permanent, temporary, vacant, or contingent on funding. That level of detail matters because staff costs are often the largest recurring expense in a nonprofit and can quickly distort the budget if the assumptions are weak. A staffing plan gives management a clear view of the organization’s capacity and its financial commitment. It also supports donor reporting because salary costs often need to be allocated and justified by activity. In short, the staffing plan turns headcount decisions into financial visibility.
Send to the team:
- “Please include timing, grade, and funding source for each position.”
Key point:
- Staffing plans make salary budgets realistic.
Official links:
- 2 CFR 200.430 Compensation—personal services
- 2 CFR 200.302 Financial management
- 2 CFR 200.329 Monitoring and reporting program performance
72. Q: How should vacant positions be treated in a budget?
A: Vacant positions should not be budgeted as if they are filled from the first day of the year unless that is truly expected. Instead, the budget should show realistic fill dates and vacancy periods so the financial plan reflects how staffing usually works in practice. This prevents the budget from becoming artificially tight or artificially optimistic. Vacancy assumptions also matter for donor reporting and management review, because they explain why salary spending may come in under plan without suggesting that the budget is wrong. Treating vacancies honestly gives a clearer picture of available headroom and helps leadership decide whether under-spend on salaries should be used for delivery catch-up, cost reallocation, or reserve building.
Send to the team:
- “Please budget vacant roles with realistic start dates.”
Key point:
- Vacancy timing should be visible in the budget.
Official links:
- 2 CFR 200.430 Compensation—personal services
- 2 CFR 200.302 Financial management
73. Q: How should a nonprofit budget for recruitment delays?
A: A nonprofit should budget for recruitment delays by assuming that hiring rarely happens instantly and by timing salary and onboarding costs realistically. That means the budget should show the expected delay between approval of the position and the actual start date. It should also account for related items such as advertisement costs, interview time, onboarding, and temporary coverage if needed. This approach is useful because recruitment delays often produce apparent savings in one line while creating pressure elsewhere in the organization. A realistic budget gives management a better view of when capacity will actually arrive and how much temporary strain the existing team may carry before the new person starts. Good budgeting does not pretend every vacancy closes immediately; it reflects the actual hiring process.
Send to the team:
- “Please reflect recruitment delay assumptions in the salary budget.”
Key point:
- Hiring delays affect total staff cost and timing.
Official links:
- 2 CFR 200.430 Compensation—personal services
- GAO Green Book
74. Q: How should an NGO budget for consultant costs?
A: Consultant costs should be budgeted with enough detail to show the purpose of the work, the expected level of effort, the rate or fee structure, the deliverables, and any approval requirements linked to the funding source. This matters because consultant spending is often scrutinized more closely than ordinary operating costs. If the budget only says “consulting,” it does not give management enough information to judge whether the cost is reasonable or allocable. A clear consultant budget also helps later when invoices arrive, because the finance team can compare the actual work to the original scope. That reduces the risk of unclear charges, unsupported extensions, or spending that no longer matches the project need.
Send to the team:
- “Please document the consultant scope, rate, and deliverables before budgeting the cost.”
Key point:
- Consultant budgets need clear scope and support.
Official links:
- 2 CFR 200.403 Factors affecting allowability of costs
- 2 CFR 200.405 Allocable costs
- 2 CFR 200.407 Prior written approval
75. Q: How should an NGO budget for overhead costs?
A: Overhead costs should be budgeted as the shared costs that make the organization work but cannot be linked to only one program on a direct basis. This includes finance, HR, IT, leadership, office support, and other central functions. The budget should show how those costs are expected to be allocated or recovered across grants and programs, because that allocation affects both the financial statements and donor reporting. Overhead should not be treated as a leftover bucket. It should be planned with the same discipline as direct program costs. When the organization understands the overhead structure, it can make better decisions about sustainability, cost recovery, and whether a grant truly covers its share of operating support.
Send to the team:
- “Please show the allocation method for overhead in the budget.”
Key point:
- Overhead must be planned and allocated consistently.
Official links:
- 2 CFR 200.405 Allocable costs
- 2 CFR 200.413 Direct costs
- 2 CFR 200.303 Internal controls
76. Q: What are support costs in budgeting?
A: Support costs are the operating costs that keep the organization functioning but do not directly deliver a single project output. They typically include administration, finance, human resources, IT, governance, office operations, and sometimes shared facilities or management. Budgeting support costs well is important because these costs enable the program team to do its work, even though they are not always visible to donors or external users. If support costs are underbudgeted, the organization may appear lean on paper while actually starving the systems that make delivery possible. If they are overbudgeted, the organization may crowd out mission spending. A thoughtful budget treats support as necessary infrastructure rather than as an inconvenience.
Send to the team:
- “Please classify support costs separately from direct program costs.”
Key point:
- Support costs enable program delivery.
Official links:
- 2 CFR 200.413 Direct costs
- 2 CFR 200.405 Allocable costs
- GAO Green Book
77. Q: How should support costs be allocated in the budget?
A: Support costs should be allocated using one documented basis that is reasonable, consistent, and linked to the benefit received. Common allocation bases include headcount, time spent, floor space, number of users, transaction volume, or another measure that reflects usage. The most important rule is consistency: the same kind of cost should not be spread differently from month to month without a sound reason. Allocation should also be simple enough to operate, because a method that cannot be maintained will eventually fail. In the budget, the allocation method should be visible so managers can understand why a project carries a given share of overhead. That transparency supports donor confidence and internal fairness.
Send to the team:
- “Please use one documented allocation method consistently.”
Key point:
- Allocation should match the benefit received.
Official links:
- 2 CFR 200.405 Allocable costs
- 2 CFR 200.413 Direct costs
- GAO Green Book
78. Q: What is a contingency budget?
A: A contingency budget is a reserved amount set aside for risks that are plausible but not yet certain. It is there to absorb surprises such as price increases, short delays, emergency replacements, minor implementation shocks, or other cost pressures that could otherwise disrupt the plan. The point of contingency is not to hide spending. It is to make risk visible and controlled. A proper contingency should be clearly labeled, separately monitored, and released only under defined approval rules. In a nonprofit, that discipline matters because emergency costs can quickly distort project spending if the organization has no planned buffer. Contingency gives the organization room to respond without improvising every time something changes.
Send to the team:
- “Please keep contingency separate and clearly labeled.”
Key point:
- Contingency is a planned reserve, not hidden spending.
Official links:
- 2 CFR 200.403 Factors affecting allowability of costs
- 2 CFR 200.407 Prior written approval
- GAO Green Book
79. Q: Why might an NGO include contingency in planning?
A: An NGO may include contingency because real projects rarely unfold exactly as planned. Prices may rise, exchange rates may move, procurement may be delayed, staff may need replacement, or field conditions may change. A contingency helps absorb those risks without immediately threatening mission delivery or forcing rushed cuts elsewhere. It also shows that management has thought about uncertainty rather than assuming perfect conditions. For donors and boards, a small and disciplined contingency can be a sign of mature planning. It shows that the organization is preparing for variability while still staying accountable. The key is to size the contingency reasonably and control its use tightly so it supports resilience rather than encouraging loose spending.
Send to the team:
- “Please include a reasonable contingency based on the risk level.”
Key point:
- Contingency improves resilience.
Official links:
- 2 CFR 200.303 Internal controls
- 2 CFR 200.403 Factors affecting allowability of costs
80. Q: How should contingencies be controlled?
A: Contingencies should be controlled just like any other sensitive budget area. The organization should set approval thresholds, keep the contingency in a separate code or line, define the conditions under which it may be used, and review it regularly so it does not become a hidden source of spending. Without that discipline, contingency can quietly become a substitute for poor budgeting. Good control also requires explanation: management should know why the contingency was released, by whom, for what purpose, and whether the original risk actually occurred. That visibility protects both internal accountability and donor confidence. A contingency is most useful when it is available but not casually accessible.
Send to the team:
- “Please set approval rules before using any contingency.”
Key point:
- Contingency needs the same discipline as regular budget lines.
Official links:
- 2 CFR 200.303 Internal controls
- 2 CFR 200.407 Prior written approval
- GAO Green Book
81. Q: What is budget approval?
A: Budget approval is the formal act that turns a draft plan into the organization’s authorized financial baseline. Depending on the governance structure, approval may come from management, the board, or the donor. Once approved, the budget becomes the standard against which actual results are monitored and explained. This is important because an unapproved budget is only a working paper, not a control reference. Approval also clarifies accountability: it tells staff what has been authorized, what assumptions were accepted, and what limits apply. In a nonprofit, formal budget approval is one of the most important governance steps because it connects mission planning to financial responsibility.
Send to the team:
- “Please confirm the budget has been formally approved before use.”
Key point:
- Approval creates the official baseline.
Official links:
- 2 CFR 200.308 Revision of budget and program plans
- GAO Green Book
82. Q: Why is formal budget approval important?
A: Formal budget approval is important because it creates a clear starting point for control, reporting, and accountability. Without approval, the organization has no agreed benchmark for asking whether spending is acceptable or whether a forecast revision is needed. Approval also helps prevent disputes later, because everyone can see which version of the budget was actually authorized. In a donor-funded environment, approval may also be needed to satisfy award conditions or internal governance requirements. The process should therefore be documented carefully and communicated to the people responsible for spending. When the approved budget is clear, monthly monitoring becomes more reliable and variance explanations become more defensible.
Send to the team:
- “Please use the approved budget as the control baseline.”
Key point:
- Approval supports accountability and control.
Official links:
- 2 CFR 200.303 Internal controls
- 2 CFR 200.308 Revision of budget and program plans
83. Q: What should happen after budget approval?
A: After budget approval, the organization should not simply file the document away. It should communicate the approved version to the relevant teams, load it into the accounting or planning system, and use it as the basis for monthly monitoring. Budget owners should understand their lines, their responsibilities, and the reporting calendar. Any changes to the plan should then go through the proper revision process instead of being made informally. This is the stage where the budget becomes operational. If the approved budget is not actively used, the organization loses the benefit of the approval process and turns the budget into paperwork rather than a management tool.
Send to the team:
- “Please distribute the approved budget and start monthly monitoring.”
Key point:
- Approval is the start of active monitoring.
Official links:
- 2 CFR 200.302 Financial management
- 2 CFR 200.329 Monitoring and reporting program performance
84. Q: How should budget revisions be documented?
A: Budget revisions should be documented in a way that makes the change easy to trace later. The record should show what changed, when it changed, why the change was needed, who approved it, which lines were affected, and whether the donor or governing body also had to approve it. This documentation matters because budget changes are normal, but undocumented changes create confusion and weaken control. A revision log also helps management see the history of the project, not just the latest version. If a later report is questioned, the organization can show exactly how the approved plan evolved and why. That makes the budget process auditable and transparent rather than improvised.
Send to the team:
- “Please keep a written record of every budget revision.”
Key point:
- Revisions need a clear audit trail.
Official links:
- 2 CFR 200.308 Revision of budget and program plans
- 2 CFR 200.407 Prior written approval
- GAO Green Book
85. Q: What is the difference between a working budget and an approved budget?
A: A working budget is a draft planning document used to test assumptions, explore options, and prepare for review. An approved budget is the formal version that the organization has authorized for control and reporting. The difference matters because drafts can change frequently, while the approved budget becomes the reference point for performance monitoring. Confusing the two can create serious problems, especially when staff start spending against a draft that was never formally accepted. For that reason, the organization should label versions clearly and make sure everyone knows which budget is current and binding. A simple naming discipline can prevent a lot of later dispute.
Send to the team:
- “Please label drafts separately from the approved version.”
Key point:
- Drafts are for planning; approved budgets are for control.
Official links:
- 2 CFR 200.308 Revision of budget and program plans
- GAO Green Book
86. Q: How should mid-year budget changes be managed?
A: Mid-year budget changes should go through a disciplined review process rather than being made informally by email or verbal instruction. The organization should identify the reason for the change, assess the financial and program impact, obtain the proper approval, and update both the budget and the forecast so the new information is reflected consistently. This is especially important when donor approval is required or when a grant’s scope or timing is affected. Mid-year changes are normal in nonprofit work, but they should be treated as governance events, not routine edits. That approach keeps the budget useful as a control tool and protects the organization from accidental noncompliance.
Send to the team:
- “Please route mid-year changes through the approval and reforecast process.”
Key point:
- Mid-year changes need control, not improvisation.
Official links:
- 2 CFR 200.308 Revision of budget and program plans
- 2 CFR 200.329 Monitoring and reporting program performance
- 2 CFR 200.303 Internal controls
87. Q: What is forecast ownership?
A: Forecast ownership is the assignment of responsibility for keeping the forecast current, accurate, and useful. A forecast that belongs to no one quickly becomes outdated, inconsistent, or disconnected from real operations. Ownership does not mean finance alone creates the whole forecast. It means someone is clearly accountable for gathering the inputs, checking consistency, updating assumptions, and presenting the final version for management review. In a nonprofit, that role often sits with finance but depends on strong input from program, operations, HR, and fundraising. Clear ownership is important because the forecast is a management tool, not a passive document. Someone must guard its quality if it is going to guide decisions.
Send to the team:
- “Please assign one owner for the forecast and one process for updates.”
Key point:
- Forecasts need a clear owner.
Official links:
- 2 CFR 200.303 Internal controls
- 2 CFR 200.329 Monitoring and reporting program performance
88. Q: Who should update the forecast in an NGO?
A: Finance should usually coordinate the forecast because finance holds the numbers, structure, and reporting rhythm. But the forecast itself should not be built in isolation. Program teams know the work plan, operations know procurement and logistics timing, HR knows hiring and vacancy timing, and fundraising knows the status of expected income. Those inputs make the forecast more credible. When one department builds the forecast alone, the result is often technically neat but operationally wrong. In a good NGO process, finance acts as the coordinator and quality controller, while budget holders contribute the real-world assumptions that make the forecast meaningful. That shared ownership is what keeps the forecast close to reality.
Send to the team:
- “Please update the forecast using input from all budget owners.”
Key point:
- Forecasting works best as a cross-functional process.
Official links:
- 2 CFR 200.302 Financial management
- 2 CFR 200.329 Monitoring and reporting program performance
89. Q: What information is needed for a reliable forecast?
A: A reliable forecast needs a strong base of current information. At minimum, it should include actual results to date, open commitments, staff plans, donor payment timing, project schedules, procurement expectations, and known risks that may shift cost or timing. It should also reflect updated assumptions rather than copying the original budget. A forecast is only useful if it shows what is most likely to happen next, not what was hoped for at the beginning of the year. In practice, good forecasting depends as much on operational input as on accounting data. The better the underlying information, the more useful the forecast becomes for management action, donor conversations, and liquidity planning.
Send to the team:
- “Please base the forecast on actuals, commitments, and current assumptions.”
Key point:
- Forecasts are only as good as the data behind them.
Official links:
- 2 CFR 200.302 Financial management
- 2 CFR 200.329 Monitoring and reporting program performance
- GAO Green Book
90. Q: How should actual results be compared with forecast results?
A: Actual results should be compared to the forecast on a regular schedule, usually monthly, so management can see whether the outlook is still accurate. The comparison should not just highlight a difference; it should explain why the difference occurred and whether the assumptions that supported the forecast have changed. If the gap is one-off, the forecast may only need a small adjustment. If the gap repeats, the assumptions need a deeper review. The forecast should then be updated so that the organization does not keep using an outdated plan. This is what makes forecasting a living control process. It learns from actual results and improves the next prediction rather than staying fixed in the past.
Send to the team:
- “Please compare actuals to forecast every month and update assumptions.”
Key point:
- Forecasts should be refreshed, not left static.
Official links:
- 2 CFR 200.328 Financial reporting
- 2 CFR 200.329 Monitoring and reporting program performance
91. Q: What is a rolling forecast?
A: A rolling forecast is a forecast that is continuously updated so the organization always has a forward-looking view over a defined horizon, such as the next six or twelve months. Instead of ending at year-end and waiting for a new annual cycle, the forecast rolls forward as each month passes. This is useful in NGO finance because funding, staffing, and program conditions often change during the year. A rolling forecast helps leaders see risk early and keep the organization responsive. It is especially valuable when the annual budget becomes outdated quickly because of donor timing, implementation delays, or changing operating conditions. In practice, a rolling forecast gives management a fresher picture of what is likely to happen next.
Send to the team:
- “Please update the forecast on a rolling basis.”
Key point:
- Rolling forecasts keep management looking forward.
Official links:
- 2 CFR 200.301 Performance measurement
- 2 CFR 200.329 Monitoring and reporting program performance
92. Q: Why is a rolling forecast useful in nonprofit finance?
A: A rolling forecast is useful because it keeps the organization’s view current instead of locked into assumptions that may no longer be true. Nonprofits often face changes in donor payment timing, activity pace, staffing, and external conditions, so a static annual plan can become outdated very quickly. A rolling forecast helps management respond before problems become urgent. It can show whether the organization is heading toward a funding gap, a cash shortage, or an overspend trend. It also supports better planning conversations with program and fundraising teams because it shows what the next period now looks like, not just what the year looked like when it began. That makes the organization more resilient and more honest with itself.
Send to the team:
- “Please use the rolling forecast to flag new risks early.”
Key point:
- Rolling forecasts improve responsiveness.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- 2 CFR 200.329 Monitoring and reporting program performance
93. Q: How should program delivery risks be reflected in the forecast?
A: Program delivery risks should be translated into financial terms inside the forecast. If a project is likely to start later, deliver fewer units, or require more support to complete, the forecast should show the effect on timing, cost, and possibly income. That means the forecast is not only a finance document but a management interpretation of operational risk. For example, a delayed field activity may reduce travel costs in the near term but increase pressure later when work must be compressed. A forecast that reflects these shifts gives leadership a realistic basis for decisions. It helps the organization avoid the false comfort of a plan that ignores implementation risk.
Send to the team:
- “Please reflect delivery risks in both timing and cost assumptions.”
Key point:
- Forecasts should show the financial effect of operational risk.
Official links:
- 2 CFR 200.329 Monitoring and reporting program performance
- GAO Green Book
94. Q: How should donor payment delays be reflected in the forecast?
A: Donor payment delays should be reflected by pushing the expected cash receipt date later in the forecast and by testing whether the organization can still meet obligations in the meantime. This is important because a delay in donor cash can quickly affect payroll, vendor payments, and program continuity even when the grant is otherwise secure. The forecast should therefore separate the promise of funding from the actual movement of cash. If the delay is short, the organization may bridge it with existing liquidity. If it is longer, management may need to slow spending or discuss revised payment timing with the donor. A good forecast makes the delay visible before it becomes a payment crisis.
Send to the team:
- “Please move the cash receipt date if the donor payment is delayed.”
Key point:
- Payment timing affects cash availability immediately.
Official links:
- 2 CFR 200.305 Federal payment
- 2 CFR 200.328 Financial reporting
95. Q: How should a nonprofit use the budget to support decision-making?
A: A nonprofit should use the budget as the main financial decision framework. It should help leaders judge whether a proposed activity is affordable, whether a new hire can be supported, whether a delay can be absorbed, and whether funding is sufficient for the planned period. The budget should also help the organization compare different options, such as continuing as planned, reducing scope, or redirecting resources to a higher priority area. In that sense, the budget is not only a report of intent. It is a tool for choosing among alternatives. When used well, it makes decisions more disciplined, more transparent, and easier to explain later to the board, donor, or auditor.
Send to the team:
- “Please use the budget as the main tool for financial decisions.”
Key point:
- The budget should guide choices, not just record plans.
Official links:
- 2 CFR 200.301 Performance measurement
- 2 CFR 200.329 Monitoring and reporting program performance
- GAO Green Book
96. Q: What makes a budget realistic rather than theoretical?
A: A realistic budget is grounded in facts that the organization can reasonably expect to happen. That means it is built from current assumptions, approved staffing plans, known timing, real procurement expectations, and income that is actually likely to be received. A theoretical budget often looks complete on paper but ignores practical limits such as recruitment delays, donor timing, inflation, or implementation constraints. A realistic budget also balances ambition with capacity. It should be challenging enough to support the mission, but honest enough to guide actual management. The best test is whether the people responsible for delivery recognize the numbers as plausible. If they do, the budget can drive action. If they do not, the budget will be ignored the moment pressure starts.
Send to the team:
- “Please base the budget on evidence rather than optimistic assumptions.”
Key point:
- Realistic budgets are grounded in evidence.
Official links:
- 2 CFR 200.302 Financial management
- 2 CFR 200.403 Factors affecting allowability of costs
- 2 CFR 200.329 Monitoring and reporting program performance
97. Q: What are common mistakes in NGO budgeting?
A: Common budgeting mistakes usually come from weak assumptions and poor timing discipline. They include copying last year’s budget without serious review, assuming every position is filled immediately, underestimating support costs, failing to separate restricted and unrestricted money, and treating donor income as if it will arrive exactly when hoped. Another frequent mistake is building the budget only from finance’s perspective and not from program reality. That creates a technically neat plan that does not reflect what the team can actually deliver. A strong budget avoids these mistakes by testing assumptions, involving budget holders, and checking the plan against current funding and operational constraints. Most budget failures are not calculation failures; they are planning failures.
Send to the team:
- “Please review last year’s assumptions before reusing any budget line.”
Key point:
- The biggest budgeting mistakes come from stale assumptions.
Official links:
- 2 CFR 200.303 Internal controls
- 2 CFR 200.308 Revision of budget and program plans
- 2 CFR 200.329 Monitoring and reporting program performance
98. Q: What are common mistakes in nonprofit forecasting?
A: Common forecasting mistakes include leaving actuals out of the model, ignoring commitments that are already approved, using last quarter’s assumptions as if they were still current, and confusing revenue promises with cash in hand. Another mistake is failing to update the forecast when circumstances change, which leaves management looking at an old version of reality. Forecasting also goes wrong when it is done too narrowly by finance alone, without input from program, procurement, HR, and fundraising. A useful forecast should be more than a spreadsheet. It should be a current management view of what is likely to happen next. When the underlying assumptions are stale or incomplete, the forecast becomes misleading even if the formulas are correct.
Send to the team:
- “Please update the forecast with actuals and commitments every month.”
Key point:
- Forecast mistakes usually come from stale data and delayed updates.
Official links:
- 2 CFR 200.302 Financial management
- 2 CFR 200.305 Federal payment
- 2 CFR 200.329 Monitoring and reporting program performance
99. Q: How can budgeting improve financial discipline in an NGO?
A: Budgeting improves financial discipline by setting boundaries around what the organization intends to spend, when it expects to spend it, and what level of approval is required before changes are made. It also gives managers a standard reference for asking whether the money is being used responsibly and whether the spending pattern supports the mission. When budgeting is done well, it creates a rhythm of planning, monitoring, explaining, and correcting. That rhythm reduces the chance of uncontrolled spending or accidental drift. It also gives staff a framework for making decisions without reinventing the rules each time. In a nonprofit, that kind of structure protects both donor confidence and program stability. Discipline is not just about restriction; it is about clarity and consistency.
Send to the team:
- “Please follow the approved budget and explain any deviations quickly.”
Key point:
- Budgeting creates structure and accountability.
Official links:
- 2 CFR 200.303 Internal controls
- 2 CFR 200.329 Monitoring and reporting program performance
- GAO Green Book
100. Q: How can forecasting help an NGO stay financially resilient?
A: Forecasting helps an NGO stay financially resilient by turning uncertainty into early information. A good forecast can show a coming income shortfall, a cash squeeze, a staffing mismatch, or an overspending trend before any of those problems become fatal. That early visibility gives management time to adjust spending, improve fundraising, delay nonessential commitments, or renegotiate timing with funders. Forecasting also helps the organization think in scenarios rather than in a single optimistic path. In practice, resilience comes from being able to see trouble while there is still room to act. A forecast that is updated regularly and discussed honestly is one of the strongest tools a nonprofit has for protecting delivery and stability at the same time.
Send to the team:
- “Please use forecasting as an early warning tool for risk and liquidity.”
Key point:
- Forecasting supports early correction and resilience.
Official links:
- 2 CFR 200.305 Federal payment
- 2 CFR 200.329 Monitoring and reporting program performance
- GAO Green Book
Rows: 100
Table 3
Q&A NGO Finance and Accounting
1. Q: What is financial reporting in an NGO?
A: Financial reporting in an NGO is the process of converting day-to-day accounting records into statements, schedules, and narrative reports that explain how resources were obtained, applied, and still held by the organization. It is more than a compliance exercise. It shows whether the NGO is stewarding restricted and unrestricted resources properly, whether program spending matches approved budgets, and whether leaders can trust the numbers they use for decisions. Good reporting ties every summary figure back to the ledger and support files so that donors, auditors, managers, and board members can trace the story from transaction to final report.
Send to the team:
- “Please keep financial reports tied to the ledger and support files.”
Key point:
- Financial reporting turns records into accountability.
Official links:
- IRS Publication 557
- FASB ASU 2016-14
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
2. Q: Why is month-end close important in nonprofit finance?
A: Month-end close is important because it creates a disciplined cut-off point for the accounting period. At that point, finance staff verify that all invoices, receipts, journals, accruals, prepayments, and bank movements for the month have been captured and reviewed. Without that discipline, reports can drift, and one department may be working from incomplete or outdated numbers. A good close makes the monthly pack dependable, helps managers spot variances early, and gives leadership confidence that the period has been finalized rather than still being edited after the fact.
Send to the team:
- “Please complete month-end close before publishing the monthly pack.”
Key point:
- Close creates a clean reporting period.
Official links:
- FASB ASU 2016-14
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
- IRS Publication 557
3. Q: What is the purpose of year-end close in an NGO?
A: Year-end close finalizes the annual books so the organization can move from ordinary monthly reporting into audited statements, donor reconciliations, tax filings where relevant, and board-level review. It is the moment when open items must be resolved, estimates must be supported, and balances must be checked for completeness. Year-end work often requires deeper review than monthly close because it affects the annual narrative that external stakeholders will see. A careful year-end process prevents surprises after the audit starts and reduces the risk that the organization will need material corrections later.
Send to the team:
- “Please treat year-end close as the final review for the annual books.”
Key point:
- Year-end close locks the annual financial story.
Official links:
- FASB ASU 2016-14
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
- IRS Publication 557
4. Q: What does a good closing process include?
A: A good closing process includes a repeatable sequence of steps that starts with cut-off review and ends with approval of the final reports. It typically includes posting routine journals, reviewing accruals and prepayments, reconciling cash and balance sheet accounts, analyzing unusual movements, and obtaining sign-off from the responsible finance lead. It also includes exception follow-up, because unresolved items should never be hidden inside the final numbers. The process works best when it is documented, assigned by owner, and supported by filing rules for every key schedule and backup document.
Send to the team:
- “Please use a standard close process every month.”
Key point:
- Good close processes are structured and repeatable.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
- FASB ASU 2016-14
- IRS Publication 557
5. Q: What is the relationship between the ledger and the financial statements?
A: The ledger is the detailed accounting record of every transaction, balance, and posting account, while the financial statements are the summarized result that is prepared from that ledger. The statements do not exist independently; they are built from the underlying account balances and adjustment entries. That means the integrity of the statements depends on the integrity of the ledger. If the ledger is incomplete, miscoded, or unreconciled, the statements will inherit the same problem. For that reason, reporting teams should always perform tie-outs and review key schedules before issuing any external or internal pack.
Send to the team:
- “Please make sure the reports tie back to the general ledger.”
Key point:
- Statements are summaries of ledger data.
Official links:
- FASB ASU 2016-14
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
- IRS Publication 557
6. Q: What is a trial balance?
A: A trial balance is a listing of all ledger account balances at a specific date, usually at the end of a reporting period. Its main purpose is to provide a checkpoint before the team moves into reporting and analysis. Even though a trial balance does not prove that every account is correct, it is a useful control because it quickly shows whether debits and credits are in balance and whether any account appears unusual. In practice, finance teams use it as a bridge between raw ledger activity and the financial statements, and they review it carefully for missing postings, odd balances, and possible misclassifications.
Send to the team:
- “Please prepare the trial balance before reporting.”
Key point:
- The trial balance is a key close checkpoint.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
- FASB ASU 2016-14
- IRS Publication 557
7. Q: Why must a trial balance be reviewed before reporting?
A: A trial balance should never be treated as a formality. It needs review because posting errors, missing journals, duplicated entries, miscoded balances, or strange fluctuations often show up first at that stage. Reviewers should compare the balances with prior periods, budget expectations, and supporting schedules so that exceptions are investigated before reports are released. This is especially important in NGOs where grant balances, donor restrictions, payroll allocations, and cash balances can be affected by timing and coding issues. A clean review at trial balance stage prevents avoidable corrections after the report has already reached management or a donor.
Send to the team:
- “Please review the trial balance for unusual balances before close.”
Key point:
- Trial balance review prevents avoidable reporting errors.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
- FASB ASU 2016-14
- IRS Publication 557
8. Q: What is the difference between management reporting and donor reporting?
A: Management reporting is designed to help internal leaders understand performance, spending, cash, risks, and corrective actions. Donor reporting is designed to show how donor funds were used, whether the conditions of the award were met, and whether the project stayed within approved scope. The two reports may use the same underlying numbers, but they are not the same product. A management pack can be more analytical and flexible, while a donor report usually needs to follow the donor's format, line items, and submission deadlines. Good finance teams keep the audience and purpose clear so the right story is told in the right format.
Send to the team:
- “Please prepare internal and donor reports separately when their purposes differ.”
Key point:
- The audience and purpose are not the same.
Official links:
- FASB ASU 2016-14
- GAO Green Book
- IRS Publication 557
- GAO internal control standards
9. Q: What is the difference between internal reporting and statutory reporting?
A: Internal reporting is built for management use and can be shaped around decision-making needs, local dashboards, exception tracking, and operational review. Statutory reporting is prepared to meet legal, regulatory, or formal external filing requirements and usually follows fixed templates and deadlines. The same accounting records may feed both, but the controls are different because the audience is different. Internal reporting can move quickly and be more practical, while statutory reporting often requires stricter formatting, approvals, and review. An NGO should separate the calendars, owners, and sign-off processes so the internal pack does not get confused with the formal filing package.
Send to the team:
- “Please separate internal pack deadlines from statutory filing deadlines.”
Key point:
- Internal and statutory reporting serve different users.
Official links:
- IRS Publication 557
- FASB ASU 2016-14
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
10. Q: What is the difference between financial reporting and operational reporting?
A: Financial reporting focuses on money: assets, liabilities, net assets, income, expenses, and cash movement. Operational reporting focuses on activity: outputs delivered, beneficiaries served, stock distributed, trips completed, or other program results. Both are essential in an NGO, but they answer different questions. Financial reports tell the organization what it spent and what it has left; operational reports tell the organization what it achieved with the resources spent. A strong system keeps the two streams aligned so program results can be interpreted in the context of actual financial usage, rather than in isolation.
Send to the team:
- “Please keep financial data and program activity data clearly labeled.”
Key point:
- Financial reporting and operational reporting answer different questions.
Official links:
- FASB ASU 2016-14
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
- IRS Publication 557
11. Q: What are the main financial statements in a nonprofit?
A: The main financial statements in a nonprofit usually include the statement of financial position, the statement of activities, the statement of cash flows, and the notes to the financial statements. Many organizations also present functional expense information so readers can see how costs were split between program, management, and fundraising functions. Together these reports explain what the organization owns, what it owes, how its net assets changed, and how its cash moved during the period. A complete set should be prepared consistently from one period to the next so readers can compare results and understand whether trends are improving or weakening.
Send to the team:
- “Please ensure all core nonprofit statements are prepared consistently.”
Key point:
- Nonprofit statements show position, activity, cash, and explanation.
Official links:
- FASB ASU 2016-14
- IRS Publication 557
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
12. Q: What does the statement of financial position show?
A: The statement of financial position shows the NGO's assets, liabilities, and net assets at one specific date. It is essentially the balance sheet view of the organization, showing what it controls, what it owes, and the residual resources available after obligations are deducted. In a nonprofit, this statement is especially important because restricted and unrestricted resources may exist side by side and must be presented clearly. Reconciled cash, confirmed receivables, supported liabilities, and properly classified net assets all feed into this report. If the underlying balance sheet accounts are weak, the statement becomes less meaningful and more difficult to trust.
Send to the team:
- “Please reconcile balance sheet accounts before issuing the statement of financial position.”
Key point:
- It shows the organization’s financial position on one date.
Official links:
- FASB ASU 2016-14
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
- IRS Publication 557
13. Q: What does the statement of activities show?
A: The statement of activities shows income, expenses, and the resulting change in net assets over the reporting period. In a nonprofit, this statement is central because it demonstrates how resources were raised and how they were used across programs and support functions. It also helps readers see whether restricted contributions were recognized appropriately and whether the organization is accumulating or reducing net assets over time. Well-classified revenue and expenses are essential here, because even a small coding mistake can distort the story about mission delivery and financial sustainability. The statement should be read together with budgets and notes to understand the drivers behind the numbers.
Send to the team:
- “Please review revenue and expense classifications before finalizing the statement of activities.”
Key point:
- It shows how the period changed net assets.
Official links:
- FASB ASU 2016-14
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
- IRS Publication 557
14. Q: What does the statement of cash flows show?
A: The statement of cash flows shows where cash came from and where it went during the period. It separates operating, investing, and financing-type movements so users can see whether the organization is generating enough cash to meet immediate commitments. For NGOs, this report is vital because accounting surplus does not always mean cash is available; a project can show a healthy result while receivables remain unpaid or grants are still restricted. The cash flow statement helps management understand liquidity pressure, payment timing, and whether the organization is relying on delayed receipts or short-term borrowing to stay afloat.
Send to the team:
- “Please reconcile cash movements before issuing the cash flow statement.”
Key point:
- Cash flow reporting explains liquidity movement.
Official links:
- FASB ASU 2016-14
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
- IRS Publication 557
15. Q: What is the purpose of notes to the financial statements?
A: The notes provide the explanation behind the numbers. They describe accounting policies, restrictions, contingencies, commitments, related balances, and other details that are too important to leave hidden inside the main statements. For an NGO, the notes can clarify how contributions were recognized, how restricted funds are tracked, how allocations are made, and what risks or obligations are not obvious from the face of the statements. They also help the reader understand estimates, such as accruals or fair values, where judgment was used. Good notes make the financial statements more transparent, more defensible, and much easier to review in audit or donor settings.
Send to the team:
- “Please include complete notes for major balances and policies.”
Key point:
- Notes give context that the main statements cannot show fully.
Official links:
- FASB ASU 2016-14
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
- IRS Publication 557
16. Q: What is a reporting calendar?
A: A reporting calendar is the timetable that shows when close tasks, reconciliations, reviews, drafts, and final submissions are due. It is a control tool as much as a scheduling tool because it assigns responsibility and prevents key work from being compressed into the last minute. A good calendar covers monthly, quarterly, and annual deadlines, and it should also include donor-specific or statutory deadlines where applicable. In an NGO, this helps finance, programs, and leadership coordinate their contributions to the reporting process. When everyone can see the timetable, fewer items are missed and fewer deadlines are discovered too late.
Send to the team:
- “Please use one reporting calendar for all recurring finance deadlines.”
Key point:
- A reporting calendar keeps close work on track.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
- FASB ASU 2016-14
- IRS Publication 557
17. Q: Why should reporting deadlines be fixed in advance?
A: Reporting deadlines should be fixed in advance so the organization can work toward a predictable rhythm instead of improvising each month. Clear deadlines create discipline for data collection, review, and approvals, and they make it easier to hold people accountable when information arrives late. They also reduce friction between finance and program teams because everyone knows what is needed and by when. For donor reporting, fixed deadlines are especially important because late submissions can damage trust even if the numbers are correct. A stable reporting rhythm gives leadership enough time to ask questions before the final report is sent.
Send to the team:
- “Please keep reporting deadlines standard each month and year.”
Key point:
- Fixed deadlines improve control and discipline.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
- FASB ASU 2016-14
- IRS Publication 557
18. Q: Who should own the month-end close process?
A: The month-end close process should have one clearly named owner, usually the finance manager, controller, or another designated finance lead. That person does not have to perform every task personally, but they should coordinate the timetable, clear bottlenecks, resolve exceptions, and confirm that the period can be closed. Without a clear owner, close tends to drift between departments and unresolved items remain open from one month to the next. Ownership matters because it creates accountability for the final numbers and for the discipline of the process itself.
Send to the team:
- “Please assign one owner for month-end close.”
Key point:
- Close needs one accountable owner.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
- FASB ASU 2016-14
- IRS Publication 557
19. Q: What are the main steps in the closing process?
A: The main steps in the closing process are: review cut-off, post routine journals, calculate accruals and prepayments, reconcile balance sheet and cash accounts, review unusual movements, prepare draft reports, and obtain final sign-off. In practice, some organizations also include grant reconciliations, payroll checks, fixed asset updates, and review of restricted balances. The important point is consistency: the team should use the same sequence every month so the process becomes predictable and auditable. Once the steps are documented, each task can be assigned, checked, and followed up without confusion.
Send to the team:
- “Please follow the same close sequence every month.”
Key point:
- Close should follow a repeatable sequence.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
- FASB ASU 2016-14
- IRS Publication 557
20. Q: What documents should be completed before close can be finalized?
A: Typical close support includes bank reconciliations, receivables aging, payables aging, grant schedules, payroll summaries, fixed asset updates, accrual schedules, prepaid amortization schedules, and journal-entry support. These documents are important because they prove that the reported balances were not guessed or left unsupported at the end of the period. The close should not be treated as complete until the key schedules are signed, filed, and traceable to the ledger. In a stronger control environment, the final review also confirms that any unresolved items are explicitly listed rather than quietly carried forward.
Send to the team:
- “Please complete all close support before final sign-off.”
Key point:
- Close should not finish without support files.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
- FASB ASU 2016-14
- IRS Publication 557
21. Q: What is cut-off in financial reporting?
A: Cut-off means recording income and expenses in the correct reporting period based on when the transaction occurred, not simply when cash was received or paid. It is one of the most important period-end concepts because it keeps monthly and annual results aligned with reality. If cut-off is weak, expenses can move between periods, grant balances can be misstated, and management may make decisions based on the wrong timing. Good cut-off control uses invoices, delivery evidence, service dates, approval dates, and accrual review to place transactions in the correct month or year.
Send to the team:
- “Please check that transactions are recorded in the correct month or year.”
Key point:
- Cut-off protects period accuracy.
Official links:
- FASB ASU 2016-14
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
- IRS Publication 557
22. Q: Why is cut-off important at month end?
A: Month-end cut-off matters because the reporting period is short, so even a few late entries can distort the whole picture. An invoice received after month end may still belong to the previous month if the service was already delivered, and a receipt booked too early may overstate cash-linked revenue timing. In NGOs, these timing differences can affect grant usage, donor restrictions, and management's view of available resources. A careful cut-off review of late invoices, late receipts, and unposted journals helps keep monthly reports credible and prevents repeated adjustments in later periods.
Send to the team:
- “Please review late invoices, late receipts, and late postings before closing.”
Key point:
- Cut-off errors distort monthly results.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
- FASB ASU 2016-14
- IRS Publication 557
23. Q: What is accrual accounting in closing?
A: Accrual accounting in closing means recognizing revenue when it is earned and expenses when they are incurred, even if cash has not yet moved. This gives the reporting period a more accurate reflection of economic activity. In an NGO, accruals are often necessary for unpaid salaries, unbilled services, earned grant revenue, utilities, and other commitments that belong to the period but remain unpaid at the reporting date. Proper accruals prevent the books from looking artificially strong or weak just because payment timing has not caught up with actual activity.
Send to the team:
- “Please record accruals before finalizing the close.”
Key point:
- Accruals align reporting with economic activity.
Official links:
- FASB ASU 2016-14
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
- IRS Publication 557
24. Q: What are accruals?
A: Accruals are amounts recognized for income earned or expenses incurred before cash is received or paid. Common examples include salary accruals, utilities, interest, professional fees, or grant-related amounts earned but not yet invoiced. They are crucial in a nonprofit environment because operations, funding conditions, and payment cycles often do not line up neatly. Accrual schedules should show the calculation, source evidence, and reversal or release logic so the entry can be checked later. When reviewed properly, accruals improve both the accuracy of the profit-and-loss view and the completeness of liabilities and receivables on the balance sheet.
Send to the team:
- “Please identify all significant accruals before close.”
Key point:
- Accruals capture timing differences.
Official links:
- FASB ASU 2016-14
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
- IRS Publication 557
25. Q: What are prepayments?
A: Prepayments are amounts paid in advance for goods or services that will be consumed in a later period. They are not immediate expenses because the organization has not yet used the full benefit. In an NGO, common prepayments include insurance, rent, software subscriptions, and other annual commitments paid ahead of time. The finance team should amortize them over the periods in which the benefit is received and should review the balance regularly to make sure nothing has been left sitting on the books after the benefit has already passed. This keeps both assets and expenses accurate.
Send to the team:
- “Please review prepaid balances and release them to expense over time.”
Key point:
- Prepayments are future-period benefits already paid for.
Official links:
- FASB ASU 2016-14
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
- IRS Publication 557
26. Q: Why must accruals be reviewed during close?
A: Accrual review is essential because accruals often depend on judgment and can easily be undercounted, duplicated, or misstated. Finance should verify that the expense or income truly belongs to the period, that the amount is reasonable, and that the supporting evidence is complete. In a grant-funded environment, accruals can affect donor reports, program spending, and the presentation of unpaid obligations. Review also matters because accruals are one of the most common places where a close can drift from disciplined accounting into assumptions that nobody checks later.
Send to the team:
- “Please review accruals with source evidence before posting.”
Key point:
- Accrual review prevents period misstatement.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
- FASB ASU 2016-14
- IRS Publication 557
27. Q: Why must prepayments be reviewed during close?
A: Prepayment review keeps expenses from being recognized too early and keeps the balance sheet asset from being overstated after the benefit has been used. This matters because an NGO may pay cash upfront for a service that benefits several months, and each month should carry only its fair share of the cost.
