Reviewing prepayments monthly helps the organization avoid both expense distortion and hidden asset build-up. It also supports better cash planning because management can see which future services have already been funded in advance.
Send to the team:
- “Please confirm prepaid balances each month.”
Key point:
- Prepayment review keeps expenses in the right period.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
- FASB ASU 2016-14
- IRS Publication 557
28. Q: What is the purpose of reconciliations?
A: Reconciliations compare accounting records to independent evidence so errors, omissions, and unusual items can be found and corrected. They are one of the strongest basic finance controls because they force the numbers to prove themselves against another source. In an NGO, this can mean comparing the ledger to the bank statement, donor schedule, supplier statement, payroll register, inventory count, or grant report. Reconciliations should not be rushed or treated as paperwork. They should be reviewed, signed, filed, and followed up until any difference is explained or corrected.
Send to the team:
- “Please reconcile key accounts every month.”
Key point:
- Reconciliations prove the books are reliable.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
- FASB ASU 2016-14
- IRS Publication 557
29. Q: Which balance sheet accounts should be reconciled monthly?
A: Cash, bank accounts, accounts receivable, accounts payable, grant balances, donor balances, prepayments, accruals, fixed assets, deferred revenue, and any other material balance sheet accounts should be reconciled monthly. Some accounts may be low-risk or low-value, but material accounts should always receive regular attention because they can hide errors for long periods if they are ignored. Reconciliation frequency should reflect both risk and transaction volume. Where activity is high or donor reporting is sensitive, monthly review is the minimum practical standard, and more frequent checks may be needed for cash or field balances.
Send to the team:
- “Please reconcile all material balance sheet accounts monthly.”
Key point:
- Material balance sheet accounts need regular reconciliation.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
- FASB ASU 2016-14
- IRS Publication 557
30. Q: What is a bank reconciliation?
A: A bank reconciliation compares the cash balance recorded in the books with the bank statement and explains the differences between them. Those differences may include timing items, bank charges, unpresented payments, deposits in transit, or entries that need correction. It is one of the most important controls for safeguarding cash because it helps detect errors and unauthorized transactions early. A reconciled bank account gives management better confidence in the liquidity position and helps ensure that reported cash is neither overstated nor understated.
Send to the team:
- “Please complete the bank reconciliation before close.”
Key point:
- Bank reconciliation confirms cash accuracy.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
- FASB ASU 2016-14
- IRS Publication 557
31. Q: Why is bank reconciliation part of the reporting cycle?
A: Bank reconciliation belongs in the reporting cycle because cash is one of the most sensitive balances in the financial statements. If cash is not verified, the statement of financial position and the statement of cash flows can both be misleading. Reconciliation also supports fraud prevention because unexplained bank differences can reveal duplicate payments, unauthorized withdrawals, or timing problems that need correction. In an NGO, where cash may be restricted, held in multiple locations, or managed by field teams, the bank reconciliation is a practical safeguard and not just an accounting formality.
Send to the team:
- “Please reconcile cash before the monthly reports are issued.”
Key point:
- Cash must be verified before reporting.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
- FASB ASU 2016-14
- IRS Publication 557
32. Q: What is an accounts receivable reconciliation?
A: An accounts receivable reconciliation compares the receivables subledger, customer or donor balances, and the general ledger to make sure they agree. It helps identify unsupported balances, overdue receivables, unapplied receipts, and entries that may have been posted to the wrong account. In NGO settings, receivables often involve donor claims, advances, or partner recoveries, so completeness matters as much as accuracy. A good reconciliation should also show the age of each balance and whether the amount is expected to be collected or should be written off or followed up.
Send to the team:
- “Please reconcile receivables to the ledger each month.”
Key point:
- Receivables should tie to both detail and ledger.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
- FASB ASU 2016-14
- IRS Publication 557
33. Q: What is an accounts payable reconciliation?
A: An accounts payable reconciliation compares supplier statements, unpaid invoices, purchase orders, and ledger balances to confirm that obligations are complete and accurate. It helps prevent both missed liabilities and duplicate payments. For an NGO, this is especially important when procurement is decentralized, because not every commitment reaches finance at the same time. A monthly review helps finance spot old balances, disputed charges, unrecorded invoices, and possible cutoff problems. It also gives managers a clearer picture of what still needs to be paid versus what has already been recognized.
Send to the team:
- “Please reconcile supplier balances before close.”
Key point:
- Payables reconciliation protects against missing liabilities.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
- FASB ASU 2016-14
- IRS Publication 557
34. Q: What is a cash count reconciliation?
A: A cash count reconciliation compares physically counted cash with the recorded petty cash or cash-on-hand balance. The count should be done by someone independent where possible, because physical cash is vulnerable to error and misuse if custody and review are not separated. Any difference must be explained immediately and documented with evidence. In field or program offices, cash counts are critical because small balances move often and may not be processed through central finance every day. A disciplined count process helps confirm that the book balance reflects what is actually in the drawer, safe, or mobile cash float.
Send to the team:
- “Please count cash and reconcile it to the record.”
Key point:
- Physical cash must match the book balance.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
- FASB ASU 2016-14
- IRS Publication 557
35. Q: What is a grant balance reconciliation?
A: A grant balance reconciliation compares grant income, grant-related expenses, advances, restrictions, and remaining balances to confirm that each grant is tracked correctly. It is especially important where donor conditions apply or where reporting is tied to milestones, budget lines, or spending periods. The reconciliation should show what has been received, what has been spent, what is still restricted, and what remains to be reported or liquidated. This protects the NGO from overspending restricted funds and also helps prevent under-reporting when earned grant income has already been achieved but not yet recognized.
Send to the team:
- “Please reconcile each grant schedule to the ledger.”
Key point:
- Grant balances must tie to donor conditions and ledger balances.
Official links:
- FASB ASU 2016-14
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
- IRS Publication 557
36. Q: What is a donor report reconciliation?
A: A donor report reconciliation compares the amounts shown in the donor report with the general ledger and supporting schedules. Its purpose is to confirm that the report is not just internally consistent but also traceable to the accounting records that support it. In practice, this means the finance team should be able to explain any variance between the report, the budget, and the ledger without delay. Reconciliation is important not only before submission but also after submission so corrections or clarifications can be made if the donor asks questions later.
Send to the team:
- “Please reconcile donor reports before submission.”
Key point:
- Donor reports should always tie back to the books.
Official links:
- FASB ASU 2016-14
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
- IRS Publication 557
37. Q: Why do reports need to tie back to the ledger?
A: Reports need to tie back to the ledger because the ledger is the official accounting record, and the report is only as reliable as the ledger behind it. Tie-outs create discipline and make sure nobody is presenting a management pack, donor report, or board summary that has drifted away from the source data. If a report does not tie, the team must identify whether the issue is timing, classification, omission, or a faulty manual adjustment. That review protects both accuracy and credibility, which are essential in a nonprofit setting where financial trust is closely linked to mission trust.
Send to the team:
- “Please trace every report total back to the general ledger.”
Key point:
- Ledger tie-out is the basis of trust.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
- FASB ASU 2016-14
- IRS Publication 557
38. Q: What causes report and ledger differences?
A: Common causes include timing differences, coding errors, missing journal entries, unsupported manual adjustments, foreign exchange effects, and incomplete reconciliations. Differences can also occur when reports use different definitions, cut-off dates, or grouping logic than the general ledger. The right response is not to ignore the difference but to classify it quickly and trace it to the source. Good teams keep a log of unresolved items and close them only when the cause is clear. This makes the next reporting cycle faster and reduces repeat errors.
Send to the team:
- “Please investigate every ledger-to-report difference.”
Key point:
- Differences usually come from timing, coding, or completeness issues.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
- FASB ASU 2016-14
- IRS Publication 557
39. Q: How should unsupported balances be handled?
A: Unsupported balances should be investigated, documented, corrected if necessary, and escalated if they cannot be resolved before reporting. They should never remain in the books merely because they are small, old, or inconvenient to investigate. In a strong control environment, every unsupported balance has an owner, a reason, a target resolution date, and a status in the exception log. That approach prevents unexplained numbers from becoming part of the permanent record and helps management see where accounting weakness still exists.
Send to the team:
- “Please resolve or document unsupported balances before close.”
Key point:
- Unsupported balances require action, not silence.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
- FASB ASU 2016-14
- IRS Publication 557
40. Q: What is the purpose of reviewing journal entries before close?
A: Journal-entry review helps confirm that entries are complete, properly classified, supported, and authorized before the ledger is finalized. It is one of the best ways to catch unusual adjustments, manual overrides, and entries posted to fix problems that should have been prevented earlier in the process. For NGOs, this review is especially important for accruals, grants, payroll, foreign exchange, and reclassifications between funds or programs. A good review asks not only whether the debit and credit balance, but also whether the entry makes business sense and whether the support file is strong enough for audit or donor review.
Send to the team:
- “Please review all non-routine journal entries before posting final reports.”
Key point:
- Journal review protects the integrity of the ledger.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
- FASB ASU 2016-14
- IRS Publication 557
41. Q: What is the difference between a recurring journal entry and a manual journal entry?
A: A recurring journal entry is a routine posting that repeats each period, such as depreciation, amortization, or a standard payroll accrual. A manual journal entry is entered individually and usually reflects a specific judgment, correction, or one-time event. Manual journals therefore deserve more review because they are less standardized and can be used to override the normal control flow. In a nonprofit, many sensitive items are posted manually, so the review should confirm that the entry is supported, approved, and consistent with policy. Recurring entries should also be monitored to make sure the formula and amounts remain valid over time.
Send to the team:
- “Please separate routine recurring journals from one-off manual journals.”
Key point:
- Manual journals need more scrutiny than recurring ones.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
- FASB ASU 2016-14
- IRS Publication 557
42. Q: Why should manual journals be reviewed carefully?
A: Manual journals can be used to correct errors, but they can also be used to conceal weak processes if they are not reviewed carefully. A strong review checks whether the entry is necessary, whether it is backed by source documents, whether the account codes are correct, and whether the person posting it had authority to do so. In an NGO, manual journals often affect grants, payroll, allocations, and closing accruals, so a weak review can create broad misstatement across several reports. Careful review preserves the integrity of both the ledger and the trust placed in it by management and external stakeholders.
Send to the team:
- “Please require review and approval for all manual journals.”
Key point:
- Manual journals are a higher-risk control point.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
- FASB ASU 2016-14
- IRS Publication 557
43. Q: What is supporting documentation in reporting?
A: Supporting documentation is the evidence that explains why a transaction or balance was recorded and why the amount is correct. It may include invoices, receipts, contracts, grant letters, payroll records, delivery notes, calculations, approvals, and reconciliations. The goal is not just filing for the sake of filing; it is to make the reported number traceable and defensible later. In NGO finance, good support often needs to show both financial validity and donor compliance, because the same entry may need to satisfy accounting rules, grant conditions, and internal control requirements at the same time.
Send to the team:
- “Please attach support to every material balance and adjustment.”
Key point:
- Reporting needs evidence behind it.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
- FASB ASU 2016-14
- IRS Publication 557
44. Q: Why is documentation important for financial close?
A: Documentation is important because a close can only be trusted if the balances and adjustments can be traced back to proof. The close is not complete when the numbers are entered; it is complete when the numbers are supported, reviewed, and retrievable later. Good documentation also makes audit work easier, reduces time spent answering follow-up questions, and helps new staff understand what happened in the prior period. In a nonprofit, where accountability to donors and boards is central, the documentation file is part of the evidence of stewardship, not merely an internal convenience.
Send to the team:
- “Please file all close support in a way that is easy to trace.”
Key point:
- Close without documentation is not defensible.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
- FASB ASU 2016-14
- IRS Publication 557
45. Q: What is a close checklist?
A: A close checklist is a structured list of tasks, reconciliations, reviews, and approvals that must be completed before the period is closed. It turns a loose process into a controlled workflow. Each item should have an owner, a due date, a status, and evidence of completion so that the finance lead can see the state of the close at a glance. In an NGO, the checklist may include grants, donor restrictions, payroll, cash, inventory, and fixed assets in addition to the usual accounting tasks. The purpose is to make the process repeatable and less dependent on memory.
Send to the team:
- “Please use the close checklist for every reporting period.”
Key point:
- A checklist makes close repeatable and controlled.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
- FASB ASU 2016-14
- IRS Publication 557
46. Q: Why should an NGO use a close checklist?
A: An NGO should use a close checklist to avoid missed steps, hidden delays, and inconsistent review quality. When several people are involved, a checklist creates one shared standard for what must be done before the books can be considered complete. It also helps managers identify bottlenecks, because an item that repeatedly stays open may point to a staffing gap, a system issue, or an unclear approval path. Over time, the checklist becomes a training tool as well as a control tool, especially for junior staff who need a visible map of the monthly process.
Send to the team:
- “Please follow the checklist and mark each item complete.”
Key point:
- The checklist reduces missed steps and control gaps.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
- FASB ASU 2016-14
- IRS Publication 557
47. Q: What are typical items on a close checklist?
A: Typical items include bank reconciliations, receivables and payables reviews, accruals, prepayments, payroll checks, grant reconciliations, fixed asset updates, journal reviews, and final approvals. Some NGOs also include cash counts, inventory counts, restricted fund analyses, and partner balance confirmations. The exact list should reflect the complexity of the organization, but the principle is the same: every material area should be touched at least once before the books are closed. A good checklist helps the team move systematically from raw data to finalized reports without relying on ad hoc memory or individual habit.
Send to the team:
- “Please include all material reconciliations and reviews in the checklist.”
Key point:
- Close checklists should cover every material area.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
- FASB ASU 2016-14
- IRS Publication 557
48. Q: What is a certification sheet in month-end close?
A: A certification sheet is a sign-off form used by responsible finance staff to confirm that key reviews, reconciliations, and checks were completed. It creates accountability for the final numbers and helps show who confirmed that the period was ready for reporting. The sheet should not be signed until the supporting evidence is actually reviewed, because the value of the form comes from the control it represents. In a nonprofit, certification is especially useful for sensitive items like donor balances, payroll accruals, and bank reconciliations, where the organization needs a clear review trail.
Send to the team:
- “Please sign the certification sheet only after all close work is complete.”
Key point:
- Certification proves the review was done.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
- FASB ASU 2016-14
- IRS Publication 557
49. Q: Why should finance staff sign off on reports?
A: Finance staff should sign off on reports to confirm they reviewed the figures, checked the supporting reconciliations, and accept responsibility for the accuracy of the report. Sign-off is a control step, not just a formality. It shows that someone with the right knowledge reviewed the report before it reached management, the board, or a donor. In larger NGOs, sign-off can also indicate that the preparer and reviewer were different people, which strengthens segregation of duties and improves accountability. When sign-off is skipped, the organization weakens the audit trail and makes later questions harder to answer.
Send to the team:
- “Please sign off on reports only after review and reconciliation.”
Key point:
- Sign-off shows review and ownership.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
- FASB ASU 2016-14
- IRS Publication 557
50. Q: What is a review note or commentary in financial reporting?
A: A review note or commentary is a short narrative that explains the results, major variances, unusual balances, risks, and actions needed. It turns the numbers into management information by showing what changed, why it changed, and what should happen next. Good commentary does not repeat the table; it interprets it. In NGO finance, commentary is especially useful for budget variances, grant underspends, cash pressure, cost overruns, and timing shifts. A strong commentary helps leadership focus on the few issues that matter most instead of scanning a page of numbers without context.
Send to the team:
- “Please add clear commentary to every reporting pack.”
Key point:
- Commentary turns numbers into useful management information.
Official links:
- FASB ASU 2016-14
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
- IRS Publication 557
51. Q: Why is variance commentary important?
A: Variance commentary is important because raw numbers rarely explain themselves. A budget line might show that spending was higher than planned, but the number alone does not tell management whether the cause was a timing shift, an emergency response, inflation, a coding error, or a real increase in activity. Commentary turns a report from a passive record into a management tool. It gives leaders the context they need to judge whether the result is acceptable, temporary, fixable, or a sign of deeper control problems. In an NGO setting, that matters even more because funding is often purpose-bound, donor timelines are tight, and program teams need quick decisions when spending patterns move away from plan. Strong variance commentary also creates discipline. It forces the finance team and budget holders to look beyond the ledger and ask what actually happened, who approved it, what evidence supports it, and whether action is needed. Over time, this improves forecasting quality, budget ownership, and accountability across the organization.
Send to the team:
- “Please explain major budget variances with clear drivers, timing effects, and any corrective action needed.”
Key point:
- Commentary turns numbers into decisions and accountability.
Official links:
- IRS Publication 557
- GAO Green Book
52. Q: What is budget versus actual reporting?
A: Budget versus actual reporting is the basic comparison between what the organization planned to spend or earn and what actually happened during the period. It can be prepared by account, donor, fund, project, department, location, or program, depending on how the NGO manages its operations. The report is useful because it shows whether activity is moving in the right direction, whether spending patterns are under control, and whether the assumptions behind the budget still hold. In practice, this report should not be treated as a one-time printout. It is most useful when it is reviewed regularly, discussed with the people responsible for the numbers, and connected to operational action. For example, if a project spends less than planned, the reason might be delayed recruitment, delayed procurement, or a phase shift in implementation. If it spends more than planned, the reason might be emergency needs, price increases, or weak spending control. Budget versus actual reporting becomes meaningful when it is paired with explanation, evidence, and follow-up.
Send to the team:
- “Please prepare a budget versus actual report each month and explain material differences.”
Key point:
- It shows what was planned versus what actually happened.
Official links:
- IRS Publication 557
- GAO Green Book
53. Q: Why do nonprofits compare actual results to budget?
A: Nonprofits compare actual results to budget because budget control is one of the most practical ways to protect mission resources. A budget is not only a financial plan; it is also a spending boundary, a forecasting tool, and a way to keep donor-funded work aligned with approved use. Comparing actual results to budget helps the organization detect overspending early, identify under-delivery, and check whether restricted funds are being used in the intended way. It also helps management decide whether the issue is temporary or structural. For example, a cost overrun in one month may simply reflect timing, while a repeated overrun may point to a weak procurement process, poor assumptions, or inadequate approvals. This comparison is also important for accountability. Boards, donors, and auditors expect the NGO to be able to explain why actual outcomes differ from plan and whether the differences were managed responsibly. In that sense, the comparison is both a control activity and a governance discipline.
Send to the team:
- “Please compare actuals to budget, explain all major differences, and flag any risk to funding or delivery.”
Key point:
- Comparison supports control, accountability, and early action.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
54. Q: What is a favorable variance?
A: A favorable variance is a result that is better than budget or expectation. It might mean spending was lower than planned, revenue was higher than expected, or a program achieved the same output at a lower cost. In NGO finance, however, favorable does not automatically mean good. A low spend may be favorable only if the organization still delivered the planned activity, met donor conditions, and maintained quality. If spending was low because implementation stalled, documents were missing, or activities were not completed, then the variance may look favorable on paper but be operationally harmful. That is why favorable variances should always be tested for their real meaning. Finance teams should ask whether the result reflects efficiency, timing, incomplete delivery, or a coding issue. A true favorable variance can signal strong cost discipline or better-than-expected income. A false favorable variance can hide delivery delays or unrecorded obligations. Good commentary distinguishes between the appearance of good performance and the actual business reality behind it.
Send to the team:
- “Please identify favorable variances and confirm they reflect real performance, not missing costs or delayed activity.”
Key point:
- Favorable means better than planned, but it still needs checking.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- IRS Publication 557
55. Q: What is an unfavorable variance?
A: An unfavorable variance is a result that is worse than budget or expectation. It may appear as overspending, under-collection of income, higher operating costs, or lower-than-planned delivery efficiency. In an NGO, this can matter quickly because many budgets are tightly tied to grant conditions, restricted activity plans, and cash availability. An unfavorable variance may indicate genuine pressure such as inflation, exchange-rate movement, emergency response, or staffing delays. It may also indicate a control issue such as poor procurement discipline, weak approval controls, late billing, or misclassification. The purpose of identifying the variance is not to blame people but to understand whether immediate correction is needed. Management needs to know the size of the issue, whether it is recurring, whether it affects donor compliance, and whether it changes the forecast for the rest of the year. The faster the organization identifies the problem, the more options it has to fix it without disrupting the mission.
Send to the team:
- “Please flag unfavorable variances early, explain the cause, and state the action needed.”
Key point:
- Unfavorable means worse than planned and may signal risk.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
56. Q: How should variances be explained clearly?
A: Variances should be explained clearly by answering four questions: what changed, why it changed, how large the impact was, and what will happen next. A strong variance note is specific enough to be useful but not so detailed that it becomes unreadable. It should separate timing effects from permanent changes, separate accounting issues from operational issues, and separate one-off items from recurring drivers. For example, if travel costs are over budget, the explanation should say whether this was caused by additional field visits, currency movement, emergency deployment, or delayed booking of prior-period costs. The note should also identify whether the issue needs action, such as reforecasting, spending restraint, donor communication, or process correction. Clear variance explanations reduce confusion and help leaders make faster decisions. They also improve the quality of future budgets because the team learns what assumptions were wrong and what trends are emerging. Over time, this creates a more disciplined planning culture and a more credible management pack.
Send to the team:
- “Please explain the reason, impact, timing, and follow-up for each major variance.”
Key point:
- Clear variance notes answer why, how much, and what next.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
57. Q: What is trend analysis in financial reporting?
A: Trend analysis is the review of financial information across multiple periods to see how numbers move over time. Instead of focusing on one month or one report only, it looks at patterns such as rising costs, falling liquidity, repeated underspending, improving collection rates, or recurring grant delays. In an NGO, trend analysis is especially useful because many risks do not appear in a single period. They emerge gradually. A one-month cash decline may not be alarming, but a three-month downward pattern may signal funding concentration, slow receivables, or higher burn than planned. Trend analysis can also reveal whether management actions are working. If a corrective plan was introduced, the trend should show improvement after implementation. It is therefore both a diagnostic and monitoring tool. Good trend analysis requires consistent definitions, stable coding, and reliable historical data. Without that, the analysis may highlight noise rather than real movement. When done properly, it gives leaders early warning and supports better decisions.
Send to the team:
- “Please compare this period with prior periods and highlight the main trends, not just the current number.”
Key point:
- Trends show direction, not just one-period results.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- IRS Publication 557
58. Q: Why is trend analysis useful for management?
A: Trend analysis is useful for management because leaders need to understand not only where the organization stands today, but also where it is heading. A nonprofit can look stable in one month and still be moving toward trouble if costs are increasing faster than income, if grant utilization is lagging, or if cash balances are weakening over time. Trend analysis gives management a way to see progress, deterioration, or volatility before the issue becomes a crisis. It helps answer practical questions such as whether overhead is rising, whether a program is absorbing funding too quickly, whether collection of receivables is slowing, or whether a donor portfolio is becoming too concentrated. It also helps management compare current performance with prior periods and with the assumptions in the budget. When used consistently, trend analysis supports reforecasting, staffing decisions, funding strategy, and board oversight. It is a simple tool, but it becomes powerful when the same indicators are tracked every month and discussed in the same way.
Send to the team:
- “Please include trend lines for key financial indicators and explain any material movement.”
Key point:
- Trends help management act before problems grow.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
59. Q: What is a monthly management pack?
A: A monthly management pack is a structured set of reports and commentary prepared on a regular cycle to help leadership understand the organization’s financial position, operational risks, and key decisions needed. It is more than a financial statement package. A good management pack connects budget performance, cash, balance sheet issues, grant activity, and important exceptions into one view. It should give leaders enough information to oversee the organization without forcing them to search across different files and systems. In an NGO, the pack often includes budget versus actuals, cash status, grant utilization, overdue items, major risks, and action points. The pack is strongest when it is consistent from month to month because that allows leaders to compare performance over time and focus on changes rather than format. It should also be concise enough to use, but detailed enough to be trusted. The goal is not to flood leaders with data. The goal is to give them a reliable basis for oversight and decision-making.
Send to the team:
- “Please prepare the monthly management pack on the same schedule and in the same structure each month.”
Key point:
- The pack supports routine leadership review and action.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- FASB ASU 2016-14
60. Q: What should a management pack include?
A: A strong management pack should include the information leaders need to understand performance and take action. At minimum, that usually means budget versus actual results, cash position, key trends, major variances, outstanding risks, donor or grant issues, and the actions required from management. In some NGOs, it may also include receivables, payables, forecast changes, staffing updates, and program delivery highlights if those have financial consequences. The most important point is not simply to include more pages, but to include the right information in a clear order. Leaders should be able to see what happened, what it means, what is unusual, and what needs to happen next. A good pack does not bury the conclusion inside the detail. It surfaces the headline message and then gives the support behind it. The finance team should also ensure that the pack is internally consistent, reconciled to the ledger, and signed off before circulation. A pack that is easy to read but hard to trust is not useful.
Send to the team:
- “Please include financial results, cash, risks, and required actions in one reconciled pack.”
Key point:
- A good pack combines results, risks, and decisions.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
61. Q: What is the purpose of a summary dashboard?
A: A summary dashboard gives a fast, visual snapshot of the most important financial and operational indicators. Its purpose is speed and focus. Instead of reading long reports, leaders can quickly see whether the organization is within thresholds, whether one or two areas need attention, and whether a deeper review is necessary. In an NGO, this is especially helpful because leadership often has to oversee multiple grants, programs, or locations at once. A dashboard can make it easier to spot declining cash, rising budget variance, grant utilization issues, or overdue obligations. The best dashboards do not try to show everything. They show only the indicators that matter most for decision-making, and they keep the presentation stable enough that changes over time are easy to see. A dashboard should be built for management action, not for decoration. If it does not change decisions, it is probably too busy or too vague.
Send to the team:
- “Please show only the key indicators leaders need to see first.”
Key point:
- Dashboards condense the most important information.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- FASB ASU 2016-14
62. Q: What key indicators should appear in a finance dashboard?
A: A finance dashboard should show the indicators that best summarize the organization’s current financial health and delivery risk. Common examples include cash balance, budget variance, burn rate, restricted fund usage, payables due, receivables aging, and grant utilization. Depending on the NGO, it may also include forecast months of runway, overdue reconciliations, or major procurement commitments. The exact indicators should be selected based on the decisions leadership needs to make. For example, an organization with tight liquidity needs cash and burn-rate indicators. An organization with multiple donors may need fund utilization and restriction indicators. A growing NGO may need staffing and commitment indicators. The dashboard should be limited enough that users can understand it quickly, but detailed enough that it captures the real risk picture. A useful dashboard shows not only the current value but also whether the indicator is improving, stable, or deteriorating. That is what makes it a management tool rather than a decorative summary.
Send to the team:
- “Please track the few indicators that matter most for decisions and show their trend.”
Key point:
- A dashboard should highlight decision-critical metrics.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- IRS Publication 557
63. Q: How should reporting be adapted for leadership?
A: Reporting for leadership should be concise, strategic, and decision-focused. Leaders usually do not need every transaction detail. They need the financial story: where the organization stands, what changed, what is at risk, and what decisions are needed now. A leadership report should therefore emphasize liquidity, key variances, recurring trends, major compliance issues, and forecast pressure. It should not overwhelm the reader with account-level detail unless that detail affects a major decision. The best leadership reporting translates technical finance results into business meaning. For example, instead of saying only that a project is over budget, the report should explain whether the overspend is temporary, whether donor funding covers it, whether reforecasting is needed, and what action management should take. Leadership reports should also be consistent from period to period so executives can compare performance and track follow-up. The goal is to support governance and action, not to create a document that only accountants can decode.
Send to the team:
- “Please make leadership reports short, clear, and focused on risks, trends, and decisions needed.”
Key point:
- Leadership needs the big picture and the action points.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- FASB ASU 2016-14
64. Q: How should reporting be adapted for program teams?
A: Reporting for program teams should be translated into operational language that connects spending to activities and results. Program staff usually care less about account names and more about whether the budget is supporting delivery, whether the project is on track, and whether there is enough funding left for planned work. That means finance should avoid jargon where possible and present numbers in a way that helps program staff make decisions. For example, instead of listing a long set of ledger accounts, the report can group costs into activity-based or workplan-based categories. It should also explain the implication of the financial result for implementation. If spending is below budget, does that mean the activity was delayed? If spending is above budget, does that mean the scope changed? Program reports are most useful when they are a joint product of finance and operations, because both sides help explain the story. The goal is shared understanding, not simply financial compliance.
Send to the team:
- “Please translate financial results into program terms and link them to activity progress.”
Key point:
- Program staff need operational meaning, not accounting jargon.
Official links:
- IRS Publication 557
- GAO Green Book
65. Q: How should reporting be adapted for donors?
A: Donor reporting should follow the donor’s rules, format, and level of detail. The report should show how the funded resources were used, what activities were delivered, what remains available, and whether any conditions were met or still pending. Donors often care about both financial and narrative performance, so the report should reconcile the money with the project story. It should also be traceable back to the grant or contribution record so the donor can see that the numbers are consistent with the agreement. Good donor reports are clear, timely, and complete. They do not mix unrelated funds, and they do not hide exceptions. If there were delays, underspends, reallocations, or local adjustments, those should be explained honestly. Donor reporting is not only a compliance exercise. It also shapes trust, future funding decisions, and the donor’s confidence in the NGO’s systems. A clean, well-explained donor report can strengthen the relationship significantly.
Send to the team:
- “Please prepare donor reports in the format and detail the donor expects, and reconcile them to the grant record.”
Key point:
- Donor reports must follow donor conditions and be traceable.
Official links:
- FASB ASU 2016-14
- FASB ASU 2020-07
66. Q: How should reporting be adapted for auditors?
A: Reporting for auditors should be built for traceability, support, and reconciliation. Auditors need to understand how the reported amount was calculated, where it came from, what documents support it, and how it links back to the ledger and underlying source records. That means the finance team should keep schedules organized, calculations transparent, and review evidence available. Auditors do not only look for the final figure. They look for the path that produced it. A strong audit-ready report therefore makes it easy to follow the trail from source document to journal entry to report line. This reduces back-and-forth, speeds up testing, and lowers the risk of unsupported adjustments. It also helps management defend the accuracy of the report if questions arise. Audit-oriented reporting should be disciplined, complete, and consistent. The easier it is for the auditor to trace the number, the stronger the organization’s control environment appears.
Send to the team:
- “Please keep supporting schedules and evidence ready for audit review and make every number traceable.”
Key point:
- Auditors need traceability and support.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
67. Q: What is donor-specific financial reporting?
A: Donor-specific financial reporting is reporting prepared for one donor, grant, or contribution agreement. It shows the income received, spending incurred, balances remaining, and any compliance items tied to that funding source. In many NGOs, this is essential because different donors may have different periods, categories, approval rules, reporting formats, and restrictions. A donor-specific report should therefore be built from the correct fund or project code and should not be mixed with general organization activity unless the donor allows it. The report needs to be consistent with the donor agreement and the internal accounting records. It should also be supported by clear schedules so that the donor can see how the total was assembled. Good donor-specific reporting reduces disputes, supports renewals, and helps demonstrate that the NGO can manage funds responsibly. It also protects the organization from accidental misuse of restricted resources.
Send to the team:
- “Please separate donor reports by funding source, grant reference, and reporting period.”
Key point:
- Donor-specific reporting follows the donor’s rules and the grant record.
Official links:
- FASB ASU 2016-14
- FASB ASU 2020-07
68. Q: Why must donor reports match donor conditions?
A: Donor reports must match donor conditions because the donor has the right to expect that the money was used in accordance with the agreement. If the report does not reflect the donor’s use restrictions, timing conditions, or documentation requirements, the report may be misleading even if the numbers are mathematically correct. Matching the report to the donor conditions protects the organization from compliance problems, payment delays, and credibility loss. It also ensures that the narrative and financial sections of the report tell the same story. For example, if a donor requires quarterly reporting on a specific project, the report should not include unrelated work or a different period definition. If the donor allows certain cost categories only, those limits must be reflected in the presentation. In short, donor conditions shape both the content and structure of the report. The finance team should review the agreement before submission, not after a donor questions the report.
Send to the team:
- “Please check each report against the donor agreement before submission.”
Key point:
- Reporting must reflect the funding terms exactly.
Official links:
- FASB ASU 2016-14
- FASB ASU 2020-07
69. Q: What is the risk of late donor reporting?
A: Late donor reporting creates several risks at once. It can damage the donor’s trust, delay subsequent payments, trigger compliance concerns, and weaken the organization’s reputation for reliability. In some cases, late reporting can also cause practical problems inside the NGO, because the finance team may have to rush reconciliations, compress review time, or submit incomplete information just to meet a deadline. That often increases the chance of errors. Late reporting can also affect grant renewal or future funding decisions if donors see a pattern of poor discipline. The deeper issue is that reporting deadlines are usually part of the accountability framework. Missing them suggests weak internal control, weak coordination between finance and programs, or poor document flow. A good NGO treats donor deadlines as operational commitments, not optional admin tasks. Timely reporting protects both compliance and the relationship.
Send to the team:
- “Please submit donor reports on time and escalate any delay before the deadline is missed.”
Key point:
- Late reporting creates compliance, cash flow, and trust risk.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
70. Q: What is a funding utilization report?
A: A funding utilization report shows how much of a grant, donation, or budget allocation has been spent, committed, or remains available. It is one of the most useful control reports in NGO finance because it shows whether the project is on track, whether spending is moving too quickly or too slowly, and whether the funding will be enough to complete the planned work. A good utilization report usually includes opening balance, amounts received, amounts spent, open commitments if relevant, and the remaining balance. In some organizations, it also shows budget versus actual by line item or activity. The report should be accurate, timely, and reconciled to the source records. It is especially helpful when donor restrictions are involved because it allows the organization to see what is still available for the approved purpose and what has already been consumed. The report should not be treated as a static summary. It should drive decisions about reforecasting, procurement, and donor communication.
Send to the team:
- “Please show opening balance, spending, commitments, and remaining balance for each funding source.”
Key point:
- Utilization reports show how funding has been used and what remains available.
Official links:
- FASB ASU 2016-14
- FASB ASU 2020-07
71. Q: How should restricted funding be reported?
A: Restricted funding should be reported separately from unrestricted resources and shown in a way that reflects the donor’s purpose or time restrictions. That means the organization should not present restricted amounts as freely available cash just because the money has arrived in the bank. In reporting, the restriction must remain visible until the condition is satisfied or the purpose is fulfilled. This separation matters because it affects how management understands available resources, how the board assesses flexibility, and how donors evaluate compliance. It also helps the finance team prevent accidental overspending from the wrong pool. Restricted funding reporting should ideally connect the restriction to the coding structure, the donor agreement, and the report narrative. If the restriction is time-based, the report should make that timing clear. If it is purpose-based, the report should show the related activity. The more visible the restriction is in the report, the easier it is to manage properly.
Send to the team:
- “Please keep restricted funds separate in coding, reporting, and cash planning.”
Key point:
- Restricted funding cannot be treated as freely available.
Official links:
- FASB ASU 2016-14
- FASB ASU 2020-07
72. Q: How should unrestricted funding be reported?
A: Unrestricted funding should be reported as resources that the NGO can use for general operations, unless the board has internally designated them for a specific internal purpose. Even when unrestricted funds are available, the report should still show how much is actually liquid, how much is committed, and how much is being held for operating stability. That helps leaders understand real flexibility rather than just accounting classification. In some organizations, unrestricted funding may support salaries, overhead, or emergency response. In others, it may be reserved internally for continuity or future investment. The report should make those internal designations visible without confusing them with donor restrictions. Good reporting on unrestricted resources is important because it tells management what the organization can do without seeking donor approval. It is a key part of resilience and cash planning. A strong report also helps the board see whether the organization has enough general support to remain stable.
Send to the team:
- “Please show unrestricted funds clearly as available resources, and distinguish board designations from donor restrictions.”
Key point:
- Unrestricted funding is available unless internally designated.
Official links:
- FASB ASU 2016-14
- IRS tax-exempt organization terms
73. Q: What is a grant closeout report?
A: A grant closeout report is the final report prepared at the end of a grant period to confirm what was spent, what remains, whether deliverables were completed, and whether any final donor submission is required. Closeout is important because it is the point where the grant moves from active management to final settlement. The report should reconcile the financial activity fully, address any unspent balance, and confirm whether the donor requires repayment, rollover, or final approval. It should also reflect whether all reports, receipts, or narrative obligations have been satisfied. In a mature NGO, closeout is not left to the last minute. It is planned in advance so that procurement, payroll, and posting cutoffs are handled correctly. A good closeout report protects the organization from disputes and gives the donor confidence that the grant was managed properly. It also creates a clean starting point for future funding if the donor renews the relationship.
Send to the team:
- “Please reconcile the grant fully before closeout submission and confirm all donor requirements are complete.”
Key point:
- Closeout confirms the grant is complete, reconciled, and properly settled.
Official links:
- FASB ASU 2020-07
- FASB ASU 2016-14
74. Q: What is the purpose of period-end reporting?
A: Period-end reporting gives the organization a formal snapshot of financial activity, balances, and performance at the end of a reporting period such as a month, quarter, or year. Its purpose is to close out the period in a controlled way so that the numbers are complete, reviewed, and ready for management, donor, board, or audit use. In NGO finance, period-end reporting is important because many decisions depend on timely information about spend rate, funding availability, obligations, and grant progress. A good period-end report should not only show the final numbers but also confirm that reconciliations, accruals, cutoff adjustments, and review steps have been completed. This makes the report more reliable and reduces the chance of later corrections. Period-end reporting also creates a rhythm for accountability. Each period becomes a checkpoint where management can assess whether the organization is on course or drifting away from plan. Without period-end discipline, reporting becomes fragmented and less trustworthy.
Send to the team:
- “Please close the period, complete the reviews, and report the results on schedule.”
Key point:
- Period-end reporting locks in a reliable view of the period.
Official links:
- IRS Publication 557
- FASB ASU 2016-14
75. Q: Why should reporting be timely?
A: Reporting should be timely because information loses value when it arrives too late to affect decisions. In a nonprofit environment, delays can mean missed opportunities to correct overspending, reallocate resources, manage cash, or alert donors to issues before they escalate. Timely reporting is also important for accountability because users expect to see where the organization stands while the facts are still current. A report delivered weeks late may still be mathematically correct, but it may no longer be useful for management action. Timeliness matters in grant management, treasury control, and board oversight because those areas often depend on fast decisions. It also improves the credibility of the finance team when reports arrive consistently on schedule. Timely reporting usually reflects good process discipline upstream, including document submission, reconciliation, and review flow. It is therefore both a reporting goal and a sign of operational health.
Send to the team:
- “Please deliver reports quickly enough for management action and donor or board review.”
Key point:
- Late reports lose decision value.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
76. Q: Why should reporting be accurate?
A: Reporting should be accurate because the entire accountability chain depends on users being able to trust the numbers. If a report has errors in coding, calculations, balances, or disclosures, then the conclusions drawn from it may be wrong even if the structure looks professional. In an NGO, inaccurate reporting can affect donor relations, board decisions, budget control, and audit outcomes. Accuracy also matters because reports often feed into other processes such as forecasting, funding requests, and compliance submissions. A single error can therefore spread quickly across the organization. Accuracy is not only about mathematics. It also includes correct classification, correct timing, and correct presentation. That is why a strong close process includes review, reconciliation, and sign-off. Accuracy is one of the main foundations of financial discipline, because without it, even a timely report can mislead the people who depend on it.
Send to the team:
- “Please check source records, coding, and calculations before release.”
Key point:
- Accuracy is the basis of trust.
Official links:
- GAO internal control standards
- GAO Green Book
77. Q: Why should reporting be complete?
A: Reporting should be complete so that all material income, costs, balances, and exceptions appear in the final report. If something significant is left out, the report may look better or simpler than reality, but it will no longer represent the organization fairly. Completeness is particularly important in NGO finance because grants, commitments, accrued expenses, and donor restrictions can easily be missed if there is weak coordination between teams. A complete report should include the financial activity that belongs to the period, the balances that need to be shown at period end, and any significant exceptions that management needs to know about. Completeness also makes reporting more auditable because it demonstrates that the finance team checked for omissions rather than only for errors in what was already recorded. This is why reconciliation, cutoff testing, and review of unposted items are critical. They help ensure that the report contains the whole picture, not just the visible part of it.
Send to the team:
- “Please include all material transactions and required schedules before the report is finalized.”
Key point:
- Missing items can distort the result.
Official links:
- GAO internal control standards
- FASB ASU 2016-14
78. Q: Why should reporting be understandable?
A: Reporting should be understandable so that the intended users can read it, interpret it, and act on it without confusion. A report may be technically correct and still fail if the audience cannot make sense of it. In an NGO, different audiences need different levels of detail, so the presentation must match the reader. Finance staff may want account-level detail, while program staff may want activity-based summaries, and leadership may want concise decision points. Understandable reporting uses clear labels, stable structure, and plain language where possible. It also avoids unnecessary clutter that hides the important message. When reports are understandable, they are more likely to be used, discussed, and acted on. That increases the return on the time spent preparing them. Clarity is not a cosmetic issue; it is part of the effectiveness of the finance function because reporting is only useful if people can absorb it quickly and correctly.
Send to the team:
- “Please write reports in clear language and use simple labels where possible.”
Key point:
- Understandable reporting is usable reporting.
Official links:
- IRS Publication 557
- FASB ASU 2016-14
79. Q: What is materiality in financial reporting?
A: Materiality is the idea that not every item is equally important. A matter is material if it is large enough, unusual enough, or significant enough to influence a user’s understanding or decision. In an NGO, materiality helps the finance team focus attention on items that could change how management, the board, or donors view performance. For example, a small coding error may not matter, but a misclassified grant balance, a large unreconciled receivable, or a major restriction issue may be material because it affects decisions or compliance. Materiality is not only about size. Context matters too. An item may be material because it relates to a key donor, a restricted fund, or a control failure even if the amount is not huge. Used well, materiality keeps reporting practical and focused. Used badly, it becomes an excuse to ignore problems. That is why the finance team should apply judgment carefully and document the logic when the threshold is not obvious.
Send to the team:
- “Please focus review on items that are material to the report, the donor, or the decision.”
Key point:
- Material items deserve special attention.
Official links:
- FASB ASU 2016-14
- GAO Green Book
80. Q: Why does materiality matter in close and reporting?
A: Materiality matters during close and reporting because time and attention are limited. The finance team should spend its effort on items that could affect the truth or usefulness of the report, rather than on trivial amounts that do not change decisions. This helps the team close on time without sacrificing quality. In an NGO, that means prioritizing large balances, unresolved grants, significant accruals, major donor issues, and items that could affect compliance or cash planning. Materiality also helps management understand why some corrections are made before sign-off while others are tracked separately. It gives the close process a practical framework. However, materiality should never be used to hide recurring errors or weak controls just because each item is small. Small items can still signal a larger process problem. The right approach is to use materiality to focus the work, while still tracking patterns that suggest risk.
Send to the team:
- “Please prioritize the items that could materially affect the report or the control environment.”
Key point:
- Materiality helps focus the close process.
Official links:
- FASB ASU 2016-14
- GAO internal control standards
81. Q: What is a classification review?
A: A classification review is the process of checking whether transactions were coded to the correct account, fund, project, program, donor, or cost category before reports are finalized. This review matters because classification affects what the numbers mean. A transaction can be accurate in amount but still wrong in presentation if it is placed in the wrong bucket. In NGO finance, classification is especially important because donor reports, budget reports, and management reports often rely on the coding structure. A classification review looks for coding errors, inconsistencies, and items that should be reallocated or reclassified. It also helps confirm that direct and indirect costs are separated properly and that restricted spending is not hidden in general categories. The best classification reviews are done before reporting rather than after the report has been distributed. That prevents confusion and reduces the need for correction later. It also makes the ledger a more reliable source for decision-making.
Send to the team:
- “Please review coding before the reports are finalized.”
Key point:
- Good coding is the base of good reporting.
Official links:
- IRS Publication 557
- GAO internal control standards
82. Q: Why should expense classifications be checked before reporting?
A: Expense classifications should be checked before reporting because the way expenses are grouped affects how the organization understands its mission cost structure. If program, support, direct, and indirect costs are mixed together, the report may still balance but it will not be useful for management or donor scrutiny. In an NGO, classification errors can also distort cost allocation, overhead analysis, and grant compliance. A report that wrongly shows support costs as program costs may overstate delivery efficiency. A report that places restricted activity in the wrong class may create donor problems. Checking classifications before reporting gives the finance team a chance to correct these issues while the books are still open. It also supports consistency from period to period. The review should focus not only on obvious miscodes but also on patterns that suggest people are using the wrong accounts repeatedly. Those patterns often point to training or system design issues that need fixing at the source.
Send to the team:
- “Please review expense coding for program and support cost accuracy before the report is published.”
Key point:
- Classification affects the meaning of the report.
Official links:
- GAO internal control standards
- IRS Publication 557
83. Q: What is a posting review?
A: A posting review is the check that confirms transactions entered into the accounting system were posted correctly to the ledger. It looks at whether the right account, amount, date, fund, project, and reference were used after the transaction was processed. This review matters because errors can be introduced during data entry even when the underlying approval or source document was correct. In a busy NGO finance environment, posting reviews help catch mistakes before they roll into monthly reports. They are also useful for identifying duplicate entries, incomplete journal entries, and transactions that were posted to the wrong period. The review may happen after a batch entry, after a payment run, or before month-end close depending on the process. A good posting review is not only about finding errors. It is also a control step that reinforces accuracy and discipline in the accounting team. Over time, it reduces repeat mistakes and increases confidence in the ledger.
Send to the team:
- “Please review ledger postings for coding, amount, and period accuracy.”
Key point:
- Posting review catches entry errors early.
Official links:
- GAO internal control standards
- GAO Green Book
84. Q: Why should postings be checked after transaction entry?
A: Postings should be checked after transaction entry because even a well-approved transaction can still be recorded incorrectly. Errors in account code, amount, dimension, or date can change the report outcome and create follow-on problems in reconciliation or donor reporting. A post-entry review is one of the simplest ways to catch these issues before they become embedded in the period close. It also helps confirm that automation or batch uploads did not introduce hidden errors. In an NGO, the consequences of unreviewed postings can be significant because grant reports and budget reports often depend on correct coding by project and fund. If errors are only discovered after close, correction becomes slower and more disruptive. Reviewing postings after entry therefore acts as a practical quality check. It reduces the number of corrections later, improves the reliability of the trial balance, and supports stronger confidence in the financial statements.
Send to the team:
- “Please verify postings after each batch or transaction run.”
Key point:
- Review after posting helps prevent report errors.
Official links:
- GAO internal control standards
- GAO Green Book
85. Q: What is an exception report?
A: An exception report is a control report that lists items that do not meet normal expectations or rules. These may include unusual balances, missing approvals, unsupported postings, overdue reconciliations, or transactions outside the normal pattern. Exception reports are useful because they show where the process needs attention instead of forcing staff to search blindly through every transaction. In NGO finance, they are especially useful for monitoring grants, cash, payables, payroll, and field transactions where errors or delays can happen quickly. A good exception report should not stop at listing the problem. It should show who owns the issue, what the deadline is, and whether the exception has been resolved. That makes it a management tool rather than just a warning list. Regular review of exceptions also helps identify recurring process weaknesses. If the same type of exception appears every month, the issue is probably structural and needs a control fix rather than repeated manual correction.
Send to the team:
- “Please review all exceptions, assign an owner for each one, and track resolution until closure.”
Key point:
- Exceptions show where control needs attention.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
86. Q: Why should exceptions be tracked during close?
A: Exceptions should be tracked during close so that unresolved issues remain visible until they are fixed. Close is one of the few times when the finance team has a full view of what is still open: missing documents, unreconciled balances, unposted entries, unusual journal items, or pending approvals. If those items are not tracked formally, they may disappear into informal email threads or verbal follow-ups and never be resolved properly. Tracking exceptions during close creates accountability. It tells the team who owns the item, by when it must be resolved, and what action is required. It also helps management decide whether the issue can be deferred, corrected now, or escalated. In an NGO, this matters because some open exceptions may affect donor reporting, cash availability, or audit readiness. A close process that tracks exceptions is much stronger than one that simply hopes the issues will go away.
Send to the team:
- “Please log every exception, its owner, due date, and resolution status.”
Key point:
- Exception tracking keeps close issues visible and actionable.
Official links:
- GAO internal control standards
- GAO Green Book
87. Q: What are common close delays in an NGO?
A: Common close delays usually come from weak upstream discipline rather than from the close itself. Typical causes include late source documents, unposted transactions, unreconciled balances, missing approvals, and unclear coding that needs correction before the books can be finalized. In NGOs, close delays are also often caused by decentralized operations, field offices, or program teams that submit information late or in inconsistent formats. If payroll, procurement, or bank data arrive late, the finance team has to wait or make estimates. If estimates are too rough, the report quality suffers. This is why the close process should not be viewed only as a finance department task. It depends on the full organization. Good close discipline starts before period end, with deadlines for document submission, review, and posting. When those routines are stable, the close becomes faster and more reliable. When they are weak, close becomes a monthly firefight.
Send to the team:
- “Please clear documents and reconciliations before the close deadline.”
Key point:
- Close delays usually come from weak upstream discipline.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
88. Q: What causes reporting delays in a nonprofit?
A: Reporting delays are often caused by late inputs from programs, weak reconciliations, poor documentation, slow review cycles, or unresolved questions about coding and balances. In a nonprofit, reporting rarely depends only on the finance team. It depends on how well other functions feed information into finance and how quickly issues are resolved when they arise. If a procurement document is missing, if a bank reconciliation is late, or if a donor activity summary has not been submitted, reporting can stall. Slow approval flow also causes delays when reports sit waiting for sign-off. A second cause is instability in the data itself: if the finance team has to keep correcting errors or reclassifying transactions, the report will not be ready on time. The remedy is usually process discipline rather than heroics. Clear deadlines, standard templates, accountability for inputs, and early review all help reduce delay. Timely reporting is a cross-functional outcome.
Send to the team:
- “Please submit all inputs on time so reporting is not delayed.”
Key point:
- Reporting depends on timely inputs, review, and issue resolution.
Official links:
- GAO internal control standards
- GAO Green Book
89. Q: How can weak documentation affect reporting quality?
A: Weak documentation makes it harder to prove that a transaction is real, correctly coded, properly approved, and recorded in the right period. That weakens report quality even if the numbers appear reasonable. In NGO finance, documentation supports both accuracy and accountability. Without invoices, receipts, contracts, attendance sheets, delivery notes, approvals, or grant correspondence, it becomes difficult to explain why a balance is what it is. Weak documentation also slows down close because staff have to search for support after the fact. It increases the risk of audit adjustments, donor queries, and internal disputes. It can also create uncertainty about whether an expense belongs to the right grant or period. Good documentation is therefore not just a filing habit. It is part of the evidence chain that makes reporting defensible. A report with strong documentation behind it is much easier to trust and much easier to explain.
Send to the team:
- “Please attach support to every material transaction before period close.”
Key point:
- Weak evidence weakens the report.
Official links:
- GAO internal control standards
- GAO Green Book
90. Q: How can poor reconciliations affect reported results?
A: Poor reconciliations can leave errors, duplicates, omissions, or unmatched balances in the final report. A ledger that has not been reconciled properly may still produce a set of financial statements, but those statements may contain hidden problems that should have been identified earlier. In an NGO, poor reconciliations can affect bank balances, donor balances, advances, payables, receivables, and inter-entity accounts. If bank reconciliations are late, cash reporting becomes unreliable. If donor reconciliations are weak, grant balances may be misstated. If balance sheet items are not reviewed carefully, old errors can remain open for months. Reconciliation is one of the most important validation steps in the reporting cycle because it connects the accounting records to real-world support. Poor reconciliation therefore does not just affect one account. It can contaminate the whole report. The remedy is disciplined, timely, documented reconciliation with review and follow-up.
Send to the team:
- “Please reconcile all key balances before closing the period.”
Key point:
- Unreconciled balances can make reports unreliable.
Official links:
- GAO internal control standards
- GAO Green Book
91. Q: What is a reporting error?
A: A reporting error is any mistake in amount, classification, timing, presentation, or disclosure that makes the report incorrect or misleading. A reporting error may come from a bad journal entry, a coding mistake, an omitted balance, a reconciliation failure, or a calculation problem. In nonprofit reporting, errors can be especially serious because donors and boards often rely on the report to assess stewardship, compliance, and sustainability. A report with an error may lead users to believe cash is stronger than it is, a grant is more available than it really is, or a cost center is performing better than it actually is. Errors also weaken confidence in the finance team and can increase the workload later when corrections are needed. The main task after discovering a reporting error is to understand the cause, not just fix the surface number. A corrected report is stronger when the reason for the error is known and the control gap is addressed.
Send to the team:
- “Please identify the root cause of the reporting error before republishing the report.”
Key point:
- A reporting error affects the reliability of the output.
Official links:
- GAO internal control standards
- FASB ASU 2016-14
92. Q: How should reporting errors be corrected?
A: Reporting errors should be corrected in a controlled way. First, the team should identify the cause and determine whether the issue came from coding, posting, omission, timing, or calculation. Then the correct adjustment should be made in the ledger or the report, depending on where the error originated. The correction should be documented clearly so anyone reviewing the file later can understand what changed, why it changed, and who approved it. If the error affected a published report, the revised version should be issued with appropriate explanation. In an NGO, this process matters because transparency is part of credibility. A hidden correction may be more dangerous than the original error. Correcting the numbers without understanding the cause also means the same mistake may recur next month. A proper correction therefore combines action, documentation, and control improvement.
Send to the team:
- “Please correct the error, document the revised figures and reason, and confirm whether the issue needs a control fix.”
Key point:
- Corrections need both action and evidence.
Official links:
- GAO internal control standards
- GAO Green Book
93. Q: What is the difference between a correction and a reclassification?
A: A correction fixes an actual error in the original entry or report. A reclassification does not necessarily mean something was wrong in substance; it means the transaction is moved to a more appropriate category, account, or presentation line. In NGO finance, this distinction matters because corrections and reclassifications have different implications. A correction may indicate a control failure, a posting error, or a missing entry. A reclassification may simply mean that the item was recorded in a general account and now needs to be shown in a donor, program, or support category for reporting purposes. Both actions should be documented, but they should not be confused. Confusing them can make it hard to understand whether a problem was a genuine mistake or a reporting adjustment. A clean review file should identify which items are corrections and which are reclassifications so that management can see what type of issue was found and whether additional action is needed.
Send to the team:
- “Please separate true corrections from reclassifications in the review file.”
Key point:
- Corrections fix mistakes; reclassifications improve presentation.
Official links:
- GAO internal control standards
- FASB ASU 2016-14
94. Q: What is the role of finance in report publication?
A: Finance owns the quality control over the published numbers. Its role is to prepare the report, check the figures, ensure the support is in place, reconcile the data, and confirm that the package is ready before distribution. Finance should not simply compile a report and send it out. It should actively review whether the report is complete, accurate, and consistent with the ledger and the approved assumptions. In an NGO, this role is essential because published reports often feed into donor submissions, board packs, leadership meetings, and audit files. Once a report is issued, it becomes part of the organization’s official record and may influence decisions or external perceptions. That is why finance must treat publication as a controlled step, not an informal file-share activity. Review and approval before release help protect the organization from avoidable errors and preserve the credibility of the finance function.
Send to the team:
- “Please finalize the report package only after finance review and approval.”
Key point:
- Finance owns the quality of the published numbers.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
95. Q: What is the role of management in report review?
A: Management reviews the report to make sure the financial results make sense from an operational perspective. While finance checks the accuracy of the numbers, management checks the business meaning. That means looking at the size and cause of variances, the impact on delivery, the effect on cash, and whether any action needs to be taken. Management review is important because it adds judgment, experience, and accountability beyond the accounting process. It also helps ensure that the report is not only technically correct but also relevant to the organization’s actual priorities. In an NGO, management should ask whether the report aligns with program plans, donor commitments, staffing realities, and sustainability goals. A strong management review does not replace finance control. It complements it. When management review is active, the report becomes a shared governance tool rather than a passive finance output.
Send to the team:
- “Please review the report for business meaning, not only arithmetic accuracy.”
Key point:
- Management adds oversight and judgment.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
96. Q: What is the role of the board in financial oversight?
A: The board’s role is governance. It does not process transactions, but it does oversee whether management is using resources responsibly, whether financial risks are being managed, whether compliance is being maintained, and whether the organization remains financially sustainable. In an NGO, the board should use financial reports to ask the right questions: Are the funds sufficient? Are restricted resources being tracked properly? Are there major variances, control weaknesses, or cash risks? Is management responding appropriately?
