Board oversight is strongest when reports are clear, concise, and honest about both strengths and concerns. The board should not be flooded with transaction detail, but it should receive enough information to fulfill its fiduciary role. Good financial reporting therefore supports the board by giving it a reliable basis for challenge, approval, and strategic direction. Governance works best when the board sees both the numbers and the story behind them.
Send to the team:
- “Please prepare board reports that show key risks, results, and open issues clearly.”
Key point:
- The board provides governance, not transaction processing.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
97. Q: How does closing support accountability to donors?
A: Closing supports accountability to donors by making sure that donor-related income, spending, balances, and obligations are fully reconciled before the report is issued. Without a proper close, the donor report may contain unposted items, incomplete balances, or misclassified costs. A clean close gives the donor a reliable picture of how the grant or contribution was managed. It also shows that the organization has a disciplined process for matching transactions to the approved funding purpose. In practical terms, close work helps ensure that source documents are captured, accruals are considered, balances are checked, and exceptions are resolved before the report goes out. That makes the donor report more defensible and more useful. It also reduces the chance of follow-up questions or disputes later. A strong close is therefore not just an internal finance exercise. It is part of the NGO’s accountability promise to the donor.
Send to the team:
- “Please close the donor records before sending the report.”
Key point:
- Close creates a trustworthy donor report.
Official links:
- FASB ASU 2020-07
- FASB ASU 2016-14
98. Q: How does closing support accountability to the board?
A: Closing supports accountability to the board by giving directors a reliable and complete financial picture. The board depends on management and finance to provide numbers that have been checked, reconciled, and reviewed. If the close is weak, board members may make decisions based on incomplete or unstable data. A proper close means the organization has done the hard work needed to produce a dependable snapshot of results, balances, and risk. That allows the board to see whether cash is sufficient, whether the budget is holding, whether the organization is meeting obligations, and whether any strategic issue needs attention. A strong close also shows the board that management takes stewardship seriously. It creates confidence that the reports can be used for oversight and planning. In that way, close is not just an accounting routine. It is part of the board’s ability to govern well.
Send to the team:
- “Please ensure the close package is ready for board review and supported by reconciliations.”
Key point:
- A clean close gives the board trustworthy oversight information.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- FASB ASU 2016-14
99. Q: How does good reporting strengthen nonprofit control?
A: Good reporting strengthens nonprofit control because it makes problems visible early and turns information into action. A report that is timely, accurate, complete, and understandable allows management to notice unusual patterns, investigate exceptions, and correct issues before they grow. It also makes it easier to detect misuse, missing documents, coding problems, and weak processes. In an NGO, reporting is therefore not only a communication activity. It is also a control activity because it supports monitoring. Good reporting helps the organization compare actual results with expectations, identify control failures, and hold people accountable for explanations and follow-up. It also supports audit readiness because well-managed reporting tends to produce better documentation and cleaner reconciliations. Over time, regular reporting builds a stronger control culture because people know their work will be reviewed, explained, and acted on. That alone improves discipline across the organization.
Send to the team:
- “Please use reporting as a control tool, not only as a record of results.”
Key point:
- Reporting is part of internal control.
Official links:
- GAO internal control standards
- GAO Green Book
100. Q: What makes a financial close process strong in an NGO?
A: A strong financial close process is disciplined, repeatable, and well documented. It starts before period end, not after. The team knows what documents are due, what reconciliations are required, who reviews each section, and when the final approval happens. Key balances are checked, exceptions are logged, postings are reviewed, and supporting files are retained. In an NGO, a strong close also means the process works across programs, grants, and locations, not just in headquarters. The close should produce a report that is timely enough to be useful, accurate enough to be trusted, and complete enough to support donor, board, and audit needs. A strong close is also one that improves over time because recurring issues are tracked and fixed at the root. When close discipline is strong, the finance team spends less time repairing mistakes and more time supporting decisions. That is what makes the process sustainable.
Send to the team:
- “Please follow the close checklist, reconcile all key balances, document every exception, and keep the process consistent each period.”
Key point:
- Strong close combines discipline, evidence, review, and repeatability.
Official links:
- GAO internal control standards
- GAO Green Book
- FASB ASU 2016-14
Rows: 100
Table 4
Q&A NGO Finance and Accounting
1. Q: What is fund, grant, and donor management in an NGO?
A: A broad control system for donor-funded resources. It starts when the award arrives, then follows the money through setup, coding, commitments, payments, reconciliations, reporting, and closeout. The point is not just to record spending, but to prove that each amount was used for the approved purpose and that any balance can still be explained. In practice, fund, grant, and donor management links the agreement, the budget, the chart of accounts, the supporting documents, and the report into one traceable chain. Good management keeps restricted and unrestricted resources separate, makes deadlines visible, and gives finance and program teams one shared source of truth.
It is strongest when setup is done before spending starts, when reporting rules are written into the workflow, and when the file can be audited without reconstruction.
Send to the team:
- “The grant file should contain the signed agreement, setup sheet, coding map, budget, and reporting calendar before the first transaction is posted.”
Key point:
- It is the control system for donor-funded resources.
Official links:
- - IRS Publication 557
- FASB ASU 2016-14
- GAO Green Book
2. Q: Why is donor management important in nonprofit finance?
A: Donor management is important because the funding is usually conditional, time-bound, and tied to trust. A nonprofit does not merely receive cash; it accepts an obligation to use resources in a controlled way, report on them clearly, and preserve evidence that supports every major decision. Strong donor management reduces the risk of overspending, misclassification, and unsupported charges. It also protects the organization when donors ask for explanations, extensions, revisions, or final closeout records. Where donor management is weak, the organization may lose credibility even if the work in the field was useful, because the accounting trail cannot prove compliance. Where it is strong, the donor can see both mission delivery and financial discipline in the same package.
Send to the team:
- “Please manage donor funds with clear coding, controls, and reporting deadlines.”
Key point:
- Good donor management protects trust and compliance.
Official links:
- - IRS Publication 557
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
3. Q: What is the difference between a fund and a grant?
A: A grant is the funding arrangement or award itself. It is the promise, with its rules, amount, period, and reporting expectations. A fund is the accounting structure used to track what happens after the award is accepted. The grant defines the obligation; the fund organizes the ledger. In practice, a single grant may be tracked through one fund or several project codes, while a fund may contain multiple lines for cost centers, activities, or restrictions. That separation matters because staff often speak about the grant as a program and about the fund as the accounting container. Keeping the two ideas distinct prevents confusion when budgets are amended, balances are rolled forward, or donor reports are prepared.
Send to the team:
- “Please treat the grant as the source agreement and the fund as the tracking structure.”
Key point:
- The grant is the award; the fund is the ledger structure.
Official links:
- - IRS Publication 557
- FASB ASU 2016-14
- GAO Green Book
4. Q: How should an NGO set up a new grant in its finance system?
A: Setup should happen before any expense is committed. The finance team should create the grant code, donor record, project code, budget map, reporting dates, restriction flags, approval authority, and any special notes that explain what is allowed and what is not. Setup is not a clerical step; it is the point where the organization decides how the award will be controlled for its full life. If setup is incomplete, later transactions may be posted to the wrong line, the wrong period, or the wrong donor. A complete setup also gives program staff a clear operating frame, so they know where to send costs, when to ask for approval, and what evidence will be needed for reconciliation and reporting.
Send to the team:
- “Please complete grant setup before posting any expense to the project.”
Key point:
- Setup must come before spending.
Official links:
- - GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
- IRS Publication 557
5. Q: What information is needed before activating a donor-funded project?
A: The activation package should include the signed agreement, approved budget, start and end dates, reporting schedule, restriction terms, payment conditions, and the responsible owner. If the award allows reprogramming, matching, advances, or direct costs only, those points should be captured in the setup file. Finance also needs to know whether the project is cash-funded, reimbursement-based, or milestone-based, because that changes the payment and reconciliation rhythm. Once this information is assembled, the project can be activated with fewer surprises. Without it, the team may treat an award as open when it is not, or spend against a budget line that was never approved. Activation is therefore a control event, not just an administrative one.
Send to the team:
- “Please confirm the agreement, budget, and reporting terms before activation.”
Key point:
- Activation requires complete funding information.
Official links:
- - IRS Publication 557
- GAO Green Book
6. Q: Why must donor conditions be reviewed before spending begins?
A: Donor conditions determine whether a cost is allowable, when it becomes allowable, and whether it belongs under a specific line, site, or activity. Reviewing them before spending is the simplest way to prevent disallowed charges and later corrections. It also helps finance and program teams avoid committing money that the donor will not reimburse or accept. Some conditions are straightforward, such as a date range or budget cap. Others are more subtle, such as prior approval for transfers, procurement thresholds, or documentation standards. When those conditions are written into the workflow at the start, people are less likely to rely on memory. That makes the whole project easier to control and easier to explain later.
Send to the team:
- “Please review donor conditions before any commitment or payment is made.”
Key point:
- Spending must follow donor conditions from the start.
Official links:
- - FASB ASU 2016-14
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
7. Q: How should donor restrictions be recorded in the accounting system?
A: Restrictions should be visible at the point of coding, not hidden in a file cabinet. The system should capture fund code, project code, restriction tag, donor name, purpose note, and any date or location limits. That makes the restriction readable in reports and easy to test during reconciliation. A useful rule is that anyone reviewing the ledger should be able to see why the cost was posted there and what control applies to it. If the system does not show the restriction clearly, the team will rely on separate spreadsheets and manual memory, which creates risk. The goal is to make the accounting structure carry the compliance logic, not to separate them.
Send to the team:
- “Please code every restricted grant with the correct fund and restriction attributes.”
Key point:
- Restrictions must be visible in the system.
Official links:
- - FASB ASU 2016-14
- IRS Publication 557
- GAO internal control standards
8. Q: What is the role of grant coding in nonprofit accounting?
A: Grant coding is the practical bridge between daily transactions and donor reporting. It links each expense, receipt, or adjustment to the correct donor, project, and budget line. That link allows finance to trace spending from source document to ledger to report. Without it, reports become manual compilations and the team loses confidence in balances. Good coding also helps with review: a manager can quickly see whether travel, salaries, supplies, or local activity costs are sitting under the correct award. The benefit is not just accuracy; it is speed, because a well-designed coding structure reduces the effort needed for analysis, donor reporting, and audit support.
Send to the team:
- “Please use grant codes on every related transaction, from budget to final report.”
Key point:
- Coding creates traceability.
Official links:
- - GAO Green Book
- IRS Publication 557
- GAO internal control standards
9. Q: How do grant codes support tracking and reporting?
A: Grant codes give the organization a common language across the ledger, the budget, procurement records, and reports. Once codes are used consistently, finance can summarize totals by donor or activity without rebuilding the numbers every month. That consistency matters for budget-versus-actual review, cash planning, and donor submissions. It also helps identify when a cost was posted to the wrong grant, because the error can be seen through the code rather than inferred from the narrative. Over time, strong coding improves the quality of management conversations, because leaders can ask for one grant or one activity and receive the same answer from every report. The result is a cleaner audit trail and fewer reconciliation disputes.
Send to the team:
- “Please keep grant codes consistent across the ledger, budget, and reports.”
Key point:
- Consistent codes make reporting reliable.
Official links:
- - GAO internal control standards
- FASB ASU 2016-14
- GAO Green Book
10. Q: What is the difference between donor reporting and internal reporting for grants?
A: Donor reporting is external and must follow the donor’s format, timing, and rules. Internal reporting is managerial and should support control, forecast, and decision-making. The donor version may be concise and formal, while the internal version may be richer, more frequent, and more diagnostic. A good NGO keeps both aligned to the same ledger so the figures reconcile, but it does not confuse them. Internal reports should reveal issues early, such as underspend, overspend, or slow liquidation, even if those details never appear in the donor submission. Donor reports prove compliance; internal reports help management correct course before compliance is at risk.
Send to the team:
- “Please keep donor reports and internal management reports separate but reconciled.”
Key point:
- Donor reporting and internal reporting serve different users.
Official links:
- - IRS Publication 557
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
11. Q: How should an NGO track restricted funding separately from unrestricted funding?
A: Restricted and unrestricted resources should never be mixed in a way that obscures their use. The organization can separate them through fund codes, schedules, classes, or reporting columns, but the logic must be clear enough that a reader can tell which balance is available for general use and which balance is tied to a donor or purpose. This separation protects against accidental use of money that was never intended for general operations and makes it easier to explain year-end balances. It also helps leadership understand true flexibility. A strong system shows not only the amount received but also the part that remains limited, the part that has been spent, and the part that is still waiting for action.
Send to the team:
- “Please keep restricted and unrestricted balances in separate tracking views.”
Key point:
- Restricted and unrestricted money must stay separate.
Official links:
- - FASB ASU 2016-14
- IRS Publication 557
12. Q: What is the risk of mixing donor funds in one budget line?
A: Mixing donor funds in one line can hide overspending, blur responsibility, and create the impression that one grant is supporting another without approval. It also makes donor reporting less reliable, because the finance team cannot easily prove what belongs to which award. In practice, mixed lines often lead to manual allocations after the fact, and those allocations are harder to defend. The risk is not only accounting error; it is also control failure, because the approval path becomes unclear. Separate donor lines make it easier to test spending, monitor balances, and identify disallowed charges before the report is due.
Send to the team:
- “Please do not combine different donor funds in one budget line unless the donor allows it.”
Key point:
- Mixing funds weakens control and traceability.
Official links:
- - GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
13. Q: How should a nonprofit monitor grant balances?
A: Monitoring should combine the approved budget, actual spending, open commitments, outstanding advances, and remaining funding. Monthly review is usually the minimum, because a grant can look healthy until invoices, payroll, or travel claims are posted late. The finance team should not only watch the spent amount but also the committed amount, because commitments can consume future balance before cash moves. A practical review asks three questions: what has been spent, what has been reserved, and what remains available. When that picture is visible, managers can slow spending, request revisions, or accelerate reporting before the balance becomes negative or the report becomes inaccurate.
Send to the team:
- “Please review grant balances regularly and flag low or negative balances early.”
Key point:
- Monitoring must show what remains and what is already committed.
Official links:
- - GAO Green Book
- IRS Publication 557
14. Q: What is a grant balance reconciliation?
A: It is the process of proving that the balance shown for a grant in the finance system agrees with the supporting records. That means matching the ledger to the budget, the transactions, the commitments, the donor report position, and any amendments that changed the award. The reconciliation shows whether the reported balance is complete, whether expenses were classified correctly, and whether any items are missing or duplicated. It is especially important near reporting deadlines and at closeout, when a small posting error can change the remaining balance. In a strong process, the reconciliation is prepared by one person, reviewed by another, and retained with the grant file as evidence.
Send to the team:
- “Please reconcile each grant balance to the ledger and donor schedule.”
Key point:
- Reconciliation proves the grant balance is correct.
Official links:
- - GAO internal control standards
- GAO Green Book
15. Q: Why should grant balances be reconciled regularly?
A: Regular reconciliation prevents a backlog of errors from building up unnoticed. It surfaces coding mistakes, timing differences, unposted adjustments, unliquidated advances, and unsupported balances while there is still time to fix them. It also strengthens control over donor deadlines, because the team can see whether a grant is drifting toward overspend or underuse. A monthly rhythm is useful because it aligns with close and reporting cycles. When reconciliation is consistent, the organization spends less time in emergency corrections and more time managing the actual project. It also gives leadership a truer view of how far each award has progressed and what risks remain.
Send to the team:
- “Please reconcile grant balances on a fixed monthly schedule.”
Key point:
- Frequent reconciliation prevents surprises.
Official links:
- - GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
16. Q: What documents should support grant spending?
A: A complete support package usually includes the approved budget, invoice or claim, receipt or delivery evidence, contract or quotation where relevant, approval evidence, and any donor-specific document required by the award. The exact set depends on the type of cost, but the principle is the same: the transaction should be explainable without hunting through separate folders. Good documentation shows who requested the cost, why it was approved, what was received, when it occurred, and which grant line it hit. That chain is what makes the posting auditable. It also protects the organization when staff change or when the donor asks for proof months later.
Send to the team:
- “Please file the approval and source documents for every grant expense.”
Key point:
- Grant spending must be document-backed.
Official links:
- - GAO internal control standards
- GAO Green Book
17. Q: What is donor compliance in NGO finance?
A: Donor compliance means respecting the rules attached to the money: purpose, period, geography, spending categories, approval thresholds, procurement conditions, and reporting deadlines. Compliance is not only a donor issue; it is also a finance control issue because the records must show that the organization followed those rules in practice. A compliant system has visible thresholds, current schedules, and review points that catch exceptions before they become findings. It also assigns clear responsibility so somebody knows who checks the conditions, who approves exceptions, and who prepares the report. Compliance is therefore a workflow discipline, not just a policy statement.
Send to the team:
- “Please check donor compliance before posting spending and before reporting.”
Key point:
- Compliance means following donor rules exactly.
Official links:
- - IRS Publication 557
- GAO Green Book
18. Q: Why is compliance checking necessary for donor-funded activities?
A: Checking compliance before and during the project reduces the chance of disallowed spending, late reporting, and avoidable donor challenge. It is easier to stop a problem before payment than to explain it after the fact. Compliance checks also encourage better coordination between finance and program teams, because both groups see the same restrictions and deadlines. In many cases, the biggest risk is not fraud but simple omission: a missing approval, a late document, or a cost charged to the wrong line. Regular checks catch those issues while they are still fixable. That makes the project cleaner, the reporting easier, and the audit response faster.
Send to the team:
- “Please perform compliance checks before payment and during monthly review.”
Key point:
- Compliance checks reduce financial and reputational risk.
Official links:
- - GAO internal control standards
- GAO Green Book
19. Q: What are common donor conditions that affect spending?
A: Common conditions include time limits, purpose limits, geography restrictions, procurement rules, matching requirements, specific allowable cost categories, and pre-approval for changes. Some conditions are explicit and easy to code. Others are embedded in the narrative of the agreement and only become visible when a manager tries to spend against them. The finance team should capture each condition in a summary sheet so it is not left to memory. When conditions are translated into operating controls, staff know what to check before spending. That reduces the likelihood of later disputes over whether a cost was permitted.
Send to the team:
- “Please list all donor conditions in the grant file and staff briefing.”
Key point:
- Donor conditions define spending boundaries.
Official links:
- - FASB ASU 2016-14
- IRS Publication 557
20. Q: How should time restrictions be handled in grant management?
A: Time restrictions should be entered into the grant record, mapped to the reporting calendar, and used to prevent spending outside the approved period. The team should know whether the award starts on signature, on a specific date, or on fulfillment of a condition. It should also know whether the donor permits extensions and who must approve them. If the project spends after the end date without authority, the issue may become a compliance finding even if the expense was useful. Time controls therefore need both system support and human review, especially near the end of the period when commitments and invoices may arrive late.
Send to the team:
- “Please check grant dates before committing or paying any cost.”
Key point:
- Time restrictions control the valid spending period.
Official links:
- - GAO Green Book
- FASB ASU 2016-14
21. Q: How should purpose restrictions be handled in grant management?
A: Purpose restrictions should be written into the setup, reflected in the chart of accounts, and enforced through review of transactions against the approved activities. The core test is simple: does this cost advance the exact purpose the donor approved? If the answer is unclear, the item should be reviewed before posting. Purpose control is especially important for shared or mixed-use costs, because those charges can drift into a line that looks convenient but is not actually permitted. A clear purpose map helps program staff understand where their activities belong and helps finance explain why one item was accepted and another was not.
Send to the team:
- “Please post costs only to approved project purposes.”
Key point:
- Purpose must match the approved activity.
Official links:
- - FASB ASU 2016-14
- GAO internal control standards
22. Q: How should location restrictions be handled in grant management?
A: Location restrictions should be built into project setup, field instructions, and approval routing. If a donor approves spending only in a particular region, office, or district, that limitation should be obvious in the code and in the staff guidance. A system that does not surface location can allow field teams to spend correctly in substance but incorrectly in accounting terms, or vice versa. Good practice is to show the approved geography in the grant summary and to require review when a transaction is outside it. This is especially important for mobile programs or sub-offices where local staff may not be close to central finance.
Send to the team:
- “Please verify the approved location before spending or approving costs.”
Key point:
- Geographic limits must be checked before payment.
Official links:
- - GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
23. Q: How should donor-approved budget limits be enforced?
A: Budget limits should be checked at the point of commitment and again at payment if the workflow allows both stages. The control should compare the requested amount against the remaining approved balance, not only against the original budget. If the donor requires approval for transfers or overages, the system should route the request before it posts. Enforcement is easier when the budget is loaded into the finance system and not only held in a separate spreadsheet. That way, the control becomes part of the transaction flow. In practice, budget enforcement is what stops a small overrun from becoming a large one.
Send to the team:
- “Please stop or escalate any cost that exceeds approved budget limits.”
Key point:
- Budget limits must be enforced before spending posts.
Official links:
- - GAO internal control standards
- GAO Green Book
24. Q: What should happen when grant spending goes over budget?
A: The over-budget item should be identified immediately, explained, and either reapproved, reallocated, or stopped depending on donor rules. The longer the team waits, the harder the correction becomes. Management should look at the cause: wrong coding, price change, timing issue, or actual overspend. Each cause leads to a different response. A coding error can be corrected. A timing issue may resolve with a budget update. A real overrun may require donor consent or internal absorption. The important thing is not to normalize the exception. Once overspending becomes ordinary, the budget has stopped functioning as a control.
Send to the team:
- “Please escalate over-budget spend immediately.”
Key point:
- Overspending needs immediate review and approval.
Official links:
- - GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
25. Q: How should an NGO manage a grant budget revision?
A: Budget revisions should follow a simple path: document the reason, confirm whether donor approval is needed, obtain the right approvals, update the budget record, and replace the old monitoring version with the revised one. The goal is not just to approve a change but to keep one authoritative version of the budget alive. Without that, staff may continue using an outdated figure while finance uses a newer one. A good revision process also notes the effective date so the team knows from when the new allocation applies. This avoids confusion in variance analysis and donor reporting.
Send to the team:
- “Please document and approve every budget revision before using it.”
Key point:
- A revision must be approved and reloaded into the system.
Official links:
- - GAO Green Book
- IRS Publication 557
26. Q: Who should approve changes to donor-funded budgets?
A: Approval should follow both the donor agreement and the organization’s delegation of authority. That means the person who can approve internally is not automatically the person who can approve externally. Larger changes, especially those that alter purpose, geography, or total amount, often require donor consent as well as management sign-off. The approval trail should be visible in the grant file so anyone can see who authorized the change and on what basis. Good governance means the right person approves the right change at the right time; it does not mean every change gets the same approval path.
Send to the team:
- “Please follow both donor approval rules and internal delegation limits.”
Key point:
- Budget changes require the right authority.
Official links:
- - GAO internal control standards
- GAO Green Book
27. Q: What is a funding agreement?
A: It is the written source document that defines the award: amount, purpose, dates, reporting, restrictions, payment terms, and closeout expectations. In a nonprofit setting, the agreement is more than a contract; it is the operating rulebook for the grant. Finance should not rely on informal messages or meeting notes when the agreement exists, because the agreement determines what can be recognized, spent, and reported. The cleaner the agreement summary, the easier it is for program, procurement, payroll, and finance teams to work from the same assumptions. Every downstream control depends on the agreement being read correctly at the start.
Send to the team:
- “Please file the signed funding agreement and use it as the setup source.”
Key point:
- The agreement sets the grant rules.
Official links:
- - IRS Publication 557
- FASB ASU 2016-14
28. Q: Why is the funding agreement the starting point for grant management?
A: Because it contains the actual rules the donor expects the organization to follow. The setup, coding, monitoring, and reporting processes all depend on that text. If the team starts from memory or from a verbal explanation, the risk of misinterpretation rises. Using the agreement first also helps during audit or donor review, because the link between the award and the accounting treatment is easy to show. The agreement is therefore the source of truth for both control design and reporting design. Everything else should be a translation of it into operational terms.
Send to the team:
- “Please build the grant setup from the signed agreement, not from memory.”
Key point:
- The agreement is the source of truth.
Official links:
- - GAO Green Book
- IRS Publication 557
29. Q: What should be extracted from a donor agreement for finance use?
A: Finance should pull out the donor name, award amount, period, restrictions, budget lines, payment terms, reporting deadlines, audit rights, amendment rules, and closeout conditions. The extracted summary should be concise enough for daily use but complete enough to guide transaction review. A well-designed summary lets someone in procurement, payroll, or field finance know instantly whether a cost is allowed. It also supports a clean handover when the grant owner changes. The summary should be attached to the setup file so the team does not need to reopen the full agreement every time a question appears.
Send to the team:
- “Please convert the agreement into a finance summary sheet.”
Key point:
- Finance needs the operational terms, not just the signature page.
Official links:
- - GAO internal control standards
- IRS Publication 557
30. Q: How should finance staff summarize donor terms for program teams?
A: They should produce a short donor terms note in plain language: what the project is for, what dates apply, what can and cannot be spent, whether approval is needed for changes, and when reports are due. Program teams do not need the legal wording of every clause; they need the operational consequences. The note should be short enough to use in the field and clear enough to prevent mistakes. If a project has unusual restrictions, such as procurement limits or matching conditions, those should be highlighted prominently. This turns the agreement from a file into a practical working guide.
Send to the team:
- “Please issue a one-page donor terms summary to all program staff.”
Key point:
- Program staff need a clear, practical summary.
Official links:
- - GAO Green Book
- FASB ASU 2016-14
31. Q: What is the role of a grant setup checklist?
A: It is the control gate that prevents incomplete or incorrect grant activation. The checklist makes sure the agreement was reviewed, the budget was loaded, the codes were created, the approvals were assigned, and the reporting calendar was entered. It is useful because people forget different things at different times; a checklist makes the minimum standard visible. When signed and filed, it also provides evidence that the organization did the setup properly. A good checklist is not an administrative burden. It is the simplest way to reduce downstream errors in coding, payment, and reporting.
Send to the team:
- “Please use a checklist for every new grant before activation.”
Key point:
- The checklist prevents incomplete setup.
Official links:
- - GAO internal control standards
- GAO Green Book
32. Q: What should be included in a grant setup checklist?
A: The checklist should cover the signed agreement, budget approval, grant code, fund code, project code, responsibility assignment, reporting dates, restriction flags, payment terms, bank or cash arrangement if relevant, and the first reconciliation date. It should also confirm that the donor summary note was shared with staff who will spend against the award. If the project needs procurement or payroll attention, those controls should be flagged too. A checklist works best when it forces the team to think through both setup and control. That makes it more than a form; it becomes a design tool for the whole project.
Send to the team:
- “Please make the checklist cover setup, control, and reporting fields.”
Key point:
- A good checklist covers both data and control items.
Official links:
- - GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
33. Q: How should a donor tracker be structured?
A: The tracker should show the donor name, reference number, award amount, project title, start and end dates, restrictions, budget status, reporting dates, payment status, amendment history, and closeout status. Ideally, it should also show the grant owner and the person responsible for next action. The purpose is to give finance a single place where active awards can be seen without opening multiple files. A good tracker is both operational and managerial: it supports follow-up and gives leadership a clean view of exposure across donors and projects. It becomes especially important when the organization has multiple funding streams and overlapping periods.
Send to the team:
- “Please maintain one donor tracker for all active awards.”
Key point:
- The tracker is the master view of grant status.
Official links:
- - GAO Green Book
- IRS Publication 557
34. Q: What information should appear in a donor register?
A: The register should read like a master list of donor obligations. It should include donor identity, award amount, project name, dates, restrictions, required reports, responsible owner, current balance, and any amendments or special conditions. Some organizations also include communication notes so they can see when a donor approved a change or requested clarification. The register is useful because it turns many separate files into one management view. It also helps with succession: if a staff member leaves, the next person can see the full history without guessing. That continuity matters for audit, closeout, and future funding discussions.
Send to the team:
- “Please keep the donor register current with every award and amendment.”
Key point:
- The donor register is the reference list for awards.
Official links:
- - GAO internal control standards
- IRS Publication 557
35. Q: What is a funding compliance register?
A: It is a live log of donor conditions, due dates, evidence requirements, responsible owners, and current status. Think of it as a control register, not just a list. Each line tells the team what must be done, when it must be done, and how success will be proven. The register helps finance identify obligations that are not financial in the narrow sense but still affect compliance, such as narrative submissions or signed approvals. A good register makes obligations visible early, which reduces the chance that a deadline will pass unnoticed. It also helps managers assign follow-up by name rather than by vague department.
Send to the team:
- “Please log every grant condition in a compliance register.”
Key point:
- The register turns donor terms into trackable actions.
Official links:
- - GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
36. Q: Why is a funding compliance register useful?
A: It creates early warning. Instead of discovering a missed report or a forgotten approval at the end of the period, the organization sees the obligation in advance and can act on it. It also gives leadership a simple way to ask which donor conditions are due this week, this month, or this quarter. That visibility helps the whole organization stay in control when staffing is stretched. The register is especially useful for multi-project NGOs where conditions differ from grant to grant. By centralizing the obligations, it prevents the same question from being answered differently by different people.
Send to the team:
- “Please review the compliance register in every monthly finance meeting.”
Key point:
- The register is an early-warning control.
Official links:
- - GAO internal control standards
- GAO Green Book
37. Q: What is the difference between donor conditions and internal approvals?
A: Donor conditions are external requirements attached to the money. Internal approvals are the NGO’s own authorization rules for how staff may use that money. The two should work together, but they are not interchangeable. An internal approval cannot cancel a donor condition, and a donor condition does not replace the NGO’s own control steps. The strongest process applies donor rules first, then routes the transaction through internal delegation and review. This protects the organization from both compliance error and unauthorized internal action. It also makes the workflow easier to teach, because staff can see which rule comes from outside and which comes from inside.
Send to the team:
- “Please apply donor conditions first, then internal approvals.”
Key point:
- Donor rules and internal rules are not the same thing.
Official links:
- - FASB ASU 2016-14
- IRS Publication 557
38. Q: How should donor-specific approval rules be applied?
A: They should be built into the workflow at the transaction level. That means the system or procedure should route special cases, such as budget transfers, unusual purchases, and exceptions to the right approver before posting or payment. The approval evidence should then be stored with the transaction file. This keeps the control connected to the actual item, rather than buried in a separate email thread. Donor-specific approval rules are most effective when staff can see them before they commit to a cost. That helps prevent late-stage corrections and makes the audit trail easier to follow.
Send to the team:
- “Please route donor-specific exceptions to the correct approver before posting.”
Key point:
- Approval rules must match the donor agreement.
Official links:
- - GAO internal control standards
- GAO Green Book
39. Q: What is a grant utilization report?
A: It is a snapshot of how an award is being used: budget, actual spending, commitments, and remaining balance. A good utilization report gives the donor and management the same basic picture, even if the formatting differs. It shows whether the project is progressing as planned or whether spending is too slow, too fast, or off pattern. The report should be reconciled to the ledger before it is shared so that the numbers can stand up to questions. Its value is not just in reporting the past; it also helps the team forecast what will happen next if the current pace continues.
Send to the team:
- “Please prepare utilization reports by grant and by budget line.”
Key point:
- Utilization reports show use versus available funding.
Official links:
- - IRS Publication 557
- FASB ASU 2016-14
40. Q: Why do donors want utilization reports?
A: Donors want to know whether the award is being used as intended and whether the project remains on schedule. Utilization reports show progress, highlight underuse or overspend, and help donors decide whether to approve extensions, changes, or additional support. They also reduce uncertainty, because the donor can compare the approved plan with the actual pace of spending. For the NGO, a well-prepared utilization report is a confidence signal: it shows that the organization can monitor its own work and explain differences before they become problems. The report therefore supports both accountability and relationship management.
Send to the team:
- “Please make utilization reports clear, reconciled, and on time.”
Key point:
- Utilization reports show stewardship and progress.
Official links:
- - GAO Green Book
- IRS Publication 557
41. Q: How should utilization be compared with approved budgets?
A: The comparison should show approved budget, actual spending, commitments, variance, and remaining balance by line. The team should look not only at the total but also at the pattern, because a grant can have an overall favorable balance while one line is already under pressure. Variance commentary should explain the reason for each material difference in plain language. This is where finance adds value: it interprets the numbers rather than simply listing them. When the comparison is done regularly, the donor report becomes easier to prepare because the logic is already known and documented.
Send to the team:
- “Please compare actual grant use to budget every month.”
Key point:
- Budget comparison shows whether spending is controlled.
Official links:
- - GAO internal control standards
- GAO Green Book
42. Q: What causes differences between budgeted and actual grant use?
A: Timing delays are common, especially when hiring, procurement, or field activity starts later than planned. Price changes, staffing changes, exchange differences, coding errors, and changes in activity design can also create variance. The key is not to assume all variance is bad; the key is to understand the driver. Some differences show healthy discipline, such as lower travel because a workshop moved online. Others reveal a control issue, such as repeated miscoding or unapproved spending. Variance review should therefore ask whether the difference is expected, avoidable, or requiring action.
Send to the team:
- “Please explain each material variance before month-end close.”
Key point:
- Variance analysis should identify the real driver.
Official links:
- - GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
43. Q: How should unused grant balances be treated?
A: Unused balances should be reviewed against the donor agreement before any action is taken. Depending on the terms, they may be carried forward, reprogrammed, returned, or closed out. The organization should not assume that leftover cash is free to use elsewhere. The correct treatment depends on the award rules and the status of the project. Finance should also check whether the unused balance reflects real savings, unspent commitments, or simply timing delays. That distinction matters because each situation has a different consequence. The treatment should be documented clearly so future staff can understand why the final action was taken.
Send to the team:
- “Please confirm the donor’s rule on unused balances before closing the grant.”
Key point:
- Unused balances follow donor instructions.
Official links:
- - FASB ASU 2016-14
- IRS Publication 557
44. Q: What is grant closeout?
A: Grant closeout is the final stage where the organization stops new commitments, clears outstanding items, confirms the final balance, submits the required final report, and archives the supporting file. It is not complete until the financial, documentary, and donor-side obligations are all settled. Closeout matters because a grant that is left open can keep generating confusion long after the work ends. Strong closeout protects the organization by showing exactly what was done, what remains, and how the final position was reached. It also creates a clean starting point for the next award and for any audit or donor follow-up.
Send to the team:
- “Please close the grant only after all transactions and reports are complete.”
Key point:
- Closeout is the final control stage.
Official links:
- - GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
45. Q: What steps are needed to close a grant properly?
A: The closeout sequence usually begins with stopping new commitments, then clearing open payables, advances, and liquidations. After that, finance reconciles the final balance, prepares the final utilization report, obtains the required review and approval, and archives the evidence. If the donor requires a refund, carryforward, or transfer, that action should be recorded before the file is closed. A good closeout process also checks whether any remaining staff costs, vendor invoices, or field claims are still pending. The goal is a clean finish: nothing open, nothing unclear, nothing unsupported.
Send to the team:
- “Please follow the closeout checklist before declaring the grant closed.”
Key point:
- Closeout needs both financial and documentary completion.
Official links:
- - GAO internal control standards
- GAO Green Book
46. Q: What should be checked before a grant is closed?
A: Before closeout, the team should check open payables, advances, commitments, unprocessed corrections, budget variances, donor report status, and missing documents. The final check should confirm that the grant balance is credible and that any remaining amount is explained. It should also confirm whether the donor expects a final narrative, a financial report, or both. The reason for this final review is simple: once the grant is closed, late errors are harder to correct and easier to overlook. A clean final review avoids reopening the file later for issues that could have been settled in time.
Send to the team:
- “Please verify all open items before closing the grant record.”
Key point:
- Closeout requires a final completeness check.
Official links:
- - GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
47. Q: Why must grant closeout be documented?
A: Documentation proves that the closeout was performed correctly and that the final balance was not guessed. It shows the date of closure, the reports submitted, the approvals obtained, and the steps taken to settle outstanding items. That record matters for audit, donor queries, and future reference. It also helps the organization learn whether the grant ran cleanly or whether recurring closeout delays need attention. Proper documentation turns closeout into evidence. Without it, the organization may know the grant ended, but it cannot easily prove how or when it ended.
Send to the team:
- “Please archive the final report, approvals, and reconciliation with the grant file.”
Key point:
- Closeout evidence protects the NGO later.
Official links:
- - GAO internal control standards
- GAO Green Book
48. Q: What is donor reporting frequency?
A: It is the schedule the donor sets for financial or narrative reporting, such as monthly, quarterly, semiannual, or final-only submissions. Frequency matters because it drives the calendar for review, reconciliation, drafting, approval, and submission. A project with monthly reporting needs a much tighter internal rhythm than one with only a final report. The finance team should capture frequency in the tracker and use it to drive reminders. That prevents last-minute rushes and helps staff plan their own work around the donor timetable. Frequency is therefore a control factor, not merely a reporting detail.
Send to the team:
- “Please record each donor’s reporting frequency in the master tracker.”
Key point:
- Reporting frequency is a contract requirement.
Official links:
- - IRS Publication 557
- GAO Green Book
49. Q: Why does reporting frequency matter in grant management?
A: Because the frequency determines how often the organization must prove control. A monthly report means monthly reconciliation, monthly commentary, and monthly review. A quarterly report may allow a slightly wider cycle, but it still requires internal monitoring so the team is not surprised at quarter-end. Missing a deadline can affect trust, payments, and future awards. Frequency also affects workload: the finance team needs enough time to gather evidence, fix errors, and clear approvals before the submission date. When the internal calendar matches the donor rhythm, reporting becomes routine rather than emergency driven.
Send to the team:
- “Please align the internal finance calendar with donor reporting frequency.”
Key point:
- Frequency drives the control timetable.
Official links:
- - GAO internal control standards
- GAO Green Book
50. Q: How should deadlines for donor reports be tracked?
A: Deadlines should live in one active register that shows the donor, report type, owner, draft date, review date, submission date, and current status. A simple reminder system is useful, but the real value is ownership: one person should know that a report is due and another should be able to see whether it is late. The register should be reviewed frequently enough to catch upcoming deadlines before they become urgent. It should also store evidence of submission and any donor acknowledgment. That way the reporting cycle is not only planned, but also provable after the fact.
Send to the team:
- “Please maintain one live deadline tracker for all donor reports.”
Key point:
- Deadline tracking prevents missed reports.
Official links:
- - GAO internal control standards
- GAO Green Book
51. Q: What is the risk of late donor submission?
A: Late donor submission is more than a timing problem. It can interrupt cash inflows, delay reimbursement or tranche release, weaken the donor’s confidence in the organization, and create pressure on finance and program teams to compress review work into too little time. When a report goes out late, the organization may also lose the chance to correct issues early, which can make the next reporting cycle harder. In practice, repeated lateness often signals weak calendar control, unclear ownership, or poor coordination between finance and program staff.
Send to the team:
- “Please submit donor reports before the deadline and escalate any delay immediately.”
Key point:
- Late reporting can affect funding, compliance, and trust.
Official links:
- 2 CFR Part 200
- GAO Green Book
52. Q: How should a nonprofit manage multiple donor deadlines?
A: A nonprofit should manage multiple donor deadlines through one controlled reporting calendar rather than separate informal reminders spread across emails or notebooks. The calendar should show each donor’s due date, the reporting format, the internal preparer, the reviewer, and the final approver. It should also include a lead time for gathering support, checking figures, and resolving questions before submission. When several donors have overlapping requirements, the calendar becomes a control tool that prevents missed deadlines, duplicated effort, and last-minute errors.
Send to the team:
- “Please maintain one shared donor deadline calendar with owners and reminders.”
Key point:
- One calendar reduces missed deadlines and confusion.
Official links:
- 2 CFR Part 200
- GAO Green Book
53. Q: What is a donor reporting calendar?
A: A donor reporting calendar is a structured schedule that turns reporting obligations into visible, trackable work. It lists what report is due, when it is due, who prepares it, who checks it, what source data is needed, and when the file must be submitted. A good calendar also shows dependencies, such as bank reconciliations, allocation updates, or program data that must be completed first. This helps the team plan ahead instead of treating donor reporting as a last-minute task.
Send to the team:
- “Please keep one reporting calendar for all donor deliverables.”
Key point:
- A calendar turns deadlines into a controlled process.
Official links:
- 2 CFR Part 200
- GAO Green Book
54. Q: What should a donor communication log capture?
A: A donor communication log should capture enough detail to reconstruct the conversation later without relying on memory. At minimum, it should record the date, donor name, topic, request, summary of what was discussed, commitments made, follow-up actions, and the person responsible for next steps. If the communication changes a deadline, clarifies a budget issue, or approves a report adjustment, that should be noted clearly. A log like this protects the organization when staff change, when questions arise later, or when auditors ask how a decision was made.
Send to the team:
- “Please log every donor conversation with date, issue, and next action.”
Key point:
- A communication log preserves the audit trail.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- 2 CFR Part 200
55. Q: Why should donor communication be documented?
A: Donor communication should be documented because oral explanations are easily forgotten, misquoted, or disputed later. Written documentation shows what the donor asked, what the organization replied, what was agreed, and whether anyone gave approval or requested a correction. It also helps management review whether promises made in a conversation were actually followed up in the ledger, the report, or the grant file. Good documentation reduces ambiguity and makes the reporting process defensible.
Send to the team:
- “Please document donor emails, calls, and approvals in the grant file.”
Key point:
- Written records reduce dispute risk and support accountability.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- 2 CFR Part 200
56. Q: What should happen when a donor asks for clarification on spending?
A: The finance team should answer from records, not from memory or assumption. That means reviewing the ledger, the supporting documents, the approved budget, the donor agreement, and any prior correspondence before responding. If the question involves a specific cost line, finance should check whether the charge was coded correctly, whether the cost is allowable, and whether the explanation is consistent with what was submitted earlier. The response should be clear, factual, and stored in the grant file so the organization can show how it handled the query.
Send to the team:
- “Please verify the spending line, support, and donor rule before replying.”
Key point:
- Clarification should be answered from records and then filed.
Official links:
- 2 CFR Part 200
- GAO Green Book
57. Q: How should unsupported donor expenses be handled?
A: Unsupported donor expenses should never be reported as if they were fully valid. The safest approach is to hold the item for review, identify what evidence is missing, and determine whether the cost can be supported, reclassified, removed, or replaced by another funding source. If documentation cannot be produced, the item should not be passed through blindly to the donor report. This is not only a reporting issue; it is also a control issue because repeated unsupported costs usually show a breakdown in approval, filing, or transaction review.
Send to the team:
- “Please do not report unsupported costs until the evidence is complete.”
Key point:
- Unsupported costs should be reviewed before reporting.
Official links:
- 2 CFR Part 200 Subpart E
- GAO Green Book
58. Q: What is the role of backup documentation in donor compliance?
A: Backup documentation is the evidence that turns a transaction from a claim into a defensible record. It proves that the expense existed, that it was approved, that it was linked to the right grant or project, and that the cost was part of the approved work. Backup documents can include invoices, receipts, attendance sheets, delivery notes, contracts, approvals, and bank proof depending on the transaction type. Without them, the organization may still have a ledger entry, but it will not have a strong compliance file.
Send to the team:
- “Please attach backup documents to every donor-funded transaction.”
Key point:
- Documentation supports compliance and auditability.
Official links:
- 2 CFR Part 200 Subpart D
- GAO Green Book
59. Q: How should ineligible costs be identified?
A: Ineligible costs should be identified by comparing each expense against the grant agreement, the approved budget, donor rules, and allowability guidance. A cost may be ineligible because it falls outside the period, exceeds budget limits, lacks support, belongs to a different donor, or is specifically excluded by the award terms. The review should not stop at the accounting code; the real question is whether the expense satisfies the donor’s conditions and the organization’s own approval rules. The earlier this check happens, the easier it is to correct the error before the report goes out.
Send to the team:
- “Please check each cost against the grant conditions before reporting it.”
Key point:
- Eligibility depends on the grant rules and timing.
Official links:
- 2 CFR Part 200 Subpart E
- 2 CFR Part 200
60. Q: What should an NGO do if an ineligible cost is found?
A: If an ineligible cost is found, the NGO should correct it immediately rather than waiting for the donor or auditor to raise it later. That may mean reclassifying the cost to another funding source, reversing the entry, recovering the amount from the responsible party, or documenting the error and the corrective action taken. The organization should also ask why the error happened in the first place, because an isolated coding error and a repeated control failure require different responses. A correction is only complete when the books, the file, and the control environment are all addressed.
Send to the team:
- “Please correct ineligible costs immediately and document the fix.”
Key point:
- Ineligible costs must be corrected, not hidden.
Official links:
- 2 CFR Part 200 Subpart E
- GAO Green Book
61. Q: What is cost eligibility in donor management?
A: Cost eligibility is the rule set that determines whether a particular cost can be charged to a donor-funded project. It depends on the donor agreement, the project purpose, the approved budget, the timing of the expense, and whether the expense is supported and allowed under the relevant rules. In practice, eligibility is the gate that separates a valid charge from a charge that should not appear in the donor report. Strong donor management means checking eligibility before the cost is recorded, not after the report is already prepared.
Send to the team:
- “Please confirm cost eligibility before charging any donor-funded activity.”
Key point:
- Eligible costs can be charged; ineligible costs cannot.
Official links:
- 2 CFR Part 200 Subpart E
- 2 CFR Part 200
62. Q: Why do eligibility rules differ across grants?
A: Eligibility rules differ because each grant is built on its own purpose, scope, budget, time period, and special conditions. One donor may permit a cost that another donor excludes, and one project may allow a shared cost allocation method that another requires to be shown separately. That is why staff should never assume that a cost allowed under one grant is automatically allowed under another. The correct approach is to review each grant on its own terms and apply the right rule set every time.
Send to the team:
- “Please review each grant on its own terms before charging costs.”
Key point:
- Each grant must be checked separately.
Official links:
- 2 CFR Part 200
- GAO Green Book
63. Q: What is donor earmarking?
A: Donor earmarking means the donor has directed the contribution toward a specific purpose, project, or use. That direction creates a limit on how the organization should apply the funds, even if the money has already been received into the bank account. In NGO practice, earmarked funding must be treated as purpose-limited and traced carefully so that the organization can show it used the money in line with donor intent. The key point is not only whether the cash arrived, but whether the organization honored the restriction attached to it.
Send to the team:
- “Please treat earmarked funds as purpose-limited money.”
Key point:
- Earmarking creates a use restriction.
Official links:
- FASB ASU 2018-08
- FASB ASU 2016-14
64. Q: How should earmarked funding be tracked?
A: Earmarked funding should be tracked in a way that keeps the donor purpose visible from receipt to final use. That usually means using separate fund, donor, or project codes together with a schedule that shows opening balance, receipts, spend, adjustments, and the remaining restricted balance. The schedule should be reconciled to the ledger regularly so the organization can explain exactly what has been used and what is still available. Tracking is not only a reporting exercise; it is also the control mechanism that prevents restricted funds from being mixed with general resources.
Send to the team:
- “Please track earmarked funds separately from general funds.”
Key point:
- Separate coding protects restricted funding.
Official links:
- FASB ASU 2016-14
- 2 CFR Part 200
65. Q: What is the difference between earmarked funds and general support?
A: Earmarked funds are tied to a specific purpose and should be used only within that scope, while general support is flexible and can help fund broader organizational needs. General support usually gives management more room to cover core costs, stabilize operations, or fill urgent gaps that are not covered by restricted funds. Earmarked funding, by contrast, carries a duty to follow donor direction and report accordingly. If the organization mixes the two, it can easily misstate its available resources or misuse money that should have remained restricted.
Send to the team:
- “Please separate restricted earmarks from unrestricted support.”
Key point:
- Restricted money is not the same as general support.
Official links:
- FASB ASU 2016-14
- FASB ASU 2018-08
66. Q: How should donor advances be managed?
A: Donor advances should be managed as a separate balance until the organization has met the conditions for recognizing the funds as earned or usable. That means recording the advance clearly, linking it to the relevant grant or project, and reconciling it against actual spending or deliverables over time. The finance team should also monitor whether any unspent balance remains and whether it needs to be returned, carried forward, or reported as a liability or deferred amount. Good advance management prevents cash from being mistaken for earned revenue too early.
Send to the team:
- “Please track donor advances until they are earned, spent, or returned.”
Key point:
- Advances need separate control and reconciliation.
Official links:
- FASB ASU 2018-08
- GAO Green Book
67. Q: When should donor advances be recognized as revenue or liability?
A: Donor advances should not automatically be treated as revenue simply because cash has been received. The organization needs to assess whether the conditions for recognition have been met; if they have not, the amount should remain as a liability or deferred balance until the relevant obligations are satisfied. This distinction matters because reporting cash as revenue too early can overstate income, distort program performance, and create confusion in donor reports. The correct treatment depends on the underlying terms and the stage of fulfillment, not on the bank deposit alone.
Send to the team:
- “Please review whether each advance is still a liability or can be recognized.”
Key point:
- Receipt of cash is not always revenue.
Official links:
- FASB ASU 2018-08
- FASB ASU 2016-14
68. Q: What is a liquidating request or fund request?
A: A liquidating request or fund request is a request for cash tied to planned or approved spending that needs to be financed for program delivery. In practical terms, it is the step where program or field teams ask for funds based on a budget, forecast, or spending plan rather than on an open-ended estimate. The request should show why the amount is needed, when it will be spent, and what project or activity it will support. This allows finance to control cash flow while still supporting operational needs.
Send to the team:
- “Please support each fund request with budget and cash need.”
Key point:
- Cash requests should be tied to real spending needs.
Official links:
- 2 CFR Part 200
- GAO Green Book
69. Q: How should cash requests align with grant spending?
A: Cash requests should be aligned to the approved budget, the planned burn rate, the reporting timetable, and the expected timing of program activity. If cash is requested too early, the organization may hold idle balances that are not yet needed. If it is requested too late, programs may face payment delays and operational disruption. A good process uses forecasted spending and actual commitments together so the cash request reflects both the grant rules and the real field need.
Send to the team:
- “Please align cash requests with the grant budget and forecast.”
Key point:
- Funding should match expected use and timing.
Official links:
- 2 CFR Part 200 Subpart D
- GAO Green Book
70. Q: What is the relationship between grant cash flow and grant reporting?
A: Grant cash flow and grant reporting are related, but they are not the same thing. Cash flow shows when money enters or leaves the bank, while grant reporting shows how the grant was used, whether the work was completed, and what balances remain against the award. A grant can have strong cash inflow but weak reporting discipline, or strong reporting but tight cash timing, and the organization must manage both separately. The finance team should reconcile the cash timing with the reported activity so the narrative and the ledger tell the same story.
Send to the team:
- “Please reconcile cash timing with reported grant activity.”
Key point:
- Cash movement and reporting are related but not identical.
Official links:
- FASB ASU 2016-14
- 2 CFR Part 200
71. Q: How should donor receivables be tracked?
A: Donor receivables should be tracked in a way that lets the team see what is owed, by whom, for which grant, and for how long it has been outstanding. A useful schedule shows the donor name, grant reference, invoice or claim date, billed amount, amount received, balance outstanding, and aging category. This makes it easier to follow up, identify delays, and distinguish between a timing issue and a collection problem. Without aging and ownership, receivables can drift for months and eventually become difficult to collect or explain.
Send to the team:
- “Please maintain an aging schedule for all donor receivables.”
Key point:
- Receivables must be monitored until collected.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- FASB ASU 2016-14
72. Q: What is the difference between a receivable and a cash advance?
A: A receivable is money that the NGO is entitled to receive from another party, usually because it has already earned it, billed it, or fulfilled the required condition. A cash advance is the opposite timing pattern: the NGO has received money before the related expenditure, deliverable, or service is complete. The accounting and reporting treatment may be quite different, so the organization should not mix the two. Clear classification helps avoid overstating income, understating obligations, or misunderstanding the real funding position.
Send to the team:
- “Please classify balances correctly as receivables or advances.”
Key point:
- Owed money and received-before-use money are different.
Official links:
- FASB ASU 2018-08
- FASB ASU 2016-14
73. Q: How should multi-year grants be monitored?
A: Multi-year grants should be monitored by year, tranche, cumulative spend, remaining balance, and reporting obligations for each period. The organization needs to know not only how much of the total award remains, but also which portion belongs to which year or reporting cycle. This prevents costs from being charged to the wrong period and makes it easier to detect whether one year is moving faster or slower than expected. A multi-year schedule also helps management plan staffing, cash, and deliverables over the full award life rather than one month at a time.
Send to the team:
- “Please track each grant year separately and reconcile cumulative use.”
Key point:
- Multi-year grants need period-by-period control.
Official links:
- 2 CFR Part 200
- GAO Green Book
74. Q: Why is grant period tracking important?
A: Grant period tracking is important because eligibility often depends on whether a cost was incurred within the allowed time frame. If a transaction falls outside the start and end dates, the organization may not be allowed to charge it to the grant, even if the expense is otherwise reasonable. Tracking the period also helps prevent backdating, rushed cut-off errors, and confusion between old and current award activity. In short, period control protects compliance and keeps the reporting boundary clear.
Send to the team:
- “Please check the grant start and end dates before posting costs.”
Key point:
- Period tracking protects allowability.
Official links:
- 2 CFR Part 200 Subpart D
- 2 CFR Part 200 Subpart E
75. Q: What happens when spending occurs outside the grant period?
A: Spending outside the grant period usually cannot be charged to that grant and may need to be moved to another funding source, reversed from the donor report, or recovered depending on the circumstance. Even if the underlying activity was legitimate, the timing may make the cost ineligible for that award. The organization should document the reason, identify how the error occurred, and strengthen the process so the same issue does not repeat. This is one of the clearest examples of how cut-off control affects donor compliance.
Send to the team:
- “Please review any out-of-period spend and correct it immediately.”
Key point:
- Out-of-period spending is a compliance risk.
Official links:
- 2 CFR Part 200 Subpart D
- GAO Green Book
76. Q: How should donors be classified in the finance records?
A: Donors should be maintained in a master record that gives each donor one unique identity in the system. The record should include basic contact details, giving history, active grants, restrictions, reporting obligations, and any communication notes needed for follow-up. A clean master record helps reduce duplicate entries, prevent missed deadlines, and make reporting more reliable. It also makes it easier to see whether a donor is linked to one project, several projects, or a longer funding relationship.
Send to the team:
- “Please keep one clean master record for each donor.”
Key point:
- A unique donor record improves traceability.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- IRS Publication 557
77. Q: Why should each donor have a unique record?
A: Each donor should have a unique record because duplicate records create confusion in reporting, communication, and balance tracking. When the same donor appears under multiple names or IDs, finance may miss outstanding items, overstate balances, or send the wrong report to the wrong contact. A unique record also helps preserve history, which is important when a donor relationship spans several years or multiple projects. In a well-controlled system, one donor should equal one master record and one clear history.
Send to the team:
- “Please avoid duplicate donor records and use one donor ID per donor.”
Key point:
- One donor should equal one master record.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- IRS Publication 557
78. Q: How should an NGO manage donor-specific reporting formats?
A: An NGO should manage donor-specific reporting formats through a standard internal library that captures each donor’s required layout, field names, narrative style, and submission deadline. This avoids reinventing the report every time and reduces the chance that a staff member will omit a required section or use an outdated template. The library should be reviewed and updated whenever a donor changes requirements. Once set up well, it becomes a practical control because the team can reuse an approved format instead of building a new one under pressure.
Send to the team:
- “Please keep donor report formats on file and update them when donors change requirements.”
Key point:
- Standard templates reduce reporting errors.
Official links:
- 2 CFR Part 200
- GAO Green Book
79. Q: What is a donor report template?
A: A donor report template is a reusable reporting structure that tells staff exactly what information must be presented to the donor. It usually includes budget, actuals, variance, narrative progress, period balances, and any explanatory notes required by the grant. A good template makes reporting more consistent across projects and periods, and it helps reviewers check whether all the required fields have been completed before submission. The template is therefore both a reporting aid and a quality control tool.
Send to the team:
- “Please use one approved report template per donor.”
Key point:
- Templates make donor reporting consistent.
Official links:
- 2 CFR Part 200
- FASB ASU 2016-14
80. Q: How should a nonprofit ensure donor reports match the ledger?
A: A nonprofit should ensure donor reports match the ledger by reconciling the report figures to the general ledger, project codes, and supporting schedules before anything is sent out. The reconciliation should explain differences in timing, classification, allocation, or pending adjustments so the reviewer can see why numbers differ if they do. If the report does not tie to the books, the organization may end up correcting the same issue later during donor review or audit. A report that ties cleanly to the ledger is more reliable and easier to defend.
Send to the team:
- “Please reconcile every donor report to the ledger before sending it out.”
Key point:
- Reports must tie to the books.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- 2 CFR Part 200 Subpart D
81. Q: What is the role of program finance in donor management?
A: Program finance is the bridge between field activity and financial control. It helps make sure the right costs are coded to the right project, the right support is collected from implementation teams, and the right explanations are available when the donor asks how the money was used. Program finance also helps monitor whether activity is happening on schedule, because spending without delivery evidence creates risk. In strong organizations, program finance is not an afterthought; it is part of the control structure that keeps reporting honest and useful.
Send to the team:
- “Please involve program finance early in donor spending reviews.”
Key point:
- Program finance connects delivery to reporting.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- 2 CFR Part 200
82. Q: What is the role of central finance in donor management?
A: Central finance owns the system of record and is responsible for the ledger, reconciliations, final review, compliance checks, and consolidated reporting across grants and projects. While program teams may prepare supporting information, central finance ensures the numbers are complete, classified consistently, and tied to the correct accounting records. It also has the broader view needed to spot cross-project issues such as duplicate charges, unrecorded liabilities, or timing problems. Central finance is therefore the control center that gives donor reporting its final integrity.
Send to the team:
- “Please route final donor reporting through central finance review.”
Key point:
- Central finance provides control and final integrity.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- 2 CFR Part 200
83. Q: How should finance and program teams coordinate on grant spending?
A: Finance and program teams should coordinate through shared budgets, clear coding rules, timely supporting documents, and regular review meetings. Program staff usually know what happened in the field, while finance staff know how the transaction should be recorded and reported, so both views are needed to get the result right. The monthly review is especially important because it gives the teams a chance to correct missing support, reclassify wrong charges, and discuss upcoming spend before it happens. Coordination is what turns donor management from a reactive process into a controlled one.
Send to the team:
- “Please hold a monthly finance-program grant review.”
Key point:
- Coordination prevents coding and reporting errors.
Official links:
- 2 CFR Part 200
- GAO Green Book
84. Q: What is the role of management in donor compliance oversight?
A: Management should not only approve transactions; it should actively oversee whether donor rules are being followed across the full grant lifecycle. That means reviewing exceptions, checking whether deadlines are being met, confirming that corrective actions are taken, and ensuring that staff understand the limits of each award. When management is involved, compliance becomes part of daily discipline rather than a task left entirely to junior staff.
