This matters because weak oversight usually shows up only after the donor asks a question or an audit uncovers the gap.
Send to the team:
- “Please review grant compliance exceptions at management level.”
Key point:
- Management owns oversight, not just processing.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- 2 CFR Part 200
85. Q: What is the role of the board in grant oversight?
A: The board should look at grant oversight from a high-level risk perspective. Its role is to understand major funding concentration, persistent compliance issues, material exceptions, and whether management is using donor funds responsibly and sustainably. The board does not need to process every transaction, but it should be informed enough to challenge weak controls, question unusual balances, and support corrective action where needed. Good board oversight turns donor risk into governance, not just accounting, concern.
Send to the team:
- “Please include grant risk and compliance highlights in board reporting.”
Key point:
- The board provides high-level oversight.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- FASB ASU 2016-14
86. Q: How should grant performance be monitored beyond spending?
A: Grant performance should be monitored using outputs, milestones, deliverables, and outcome progress, not only the amount spent. A grant can be under budget and still underperform if the field work is late, incomplete, or poorly documented. Likewise, a grant can be heavily spent but still lack evidence that the intended results were achieved. The best monitoring approach links money, activity, and results so management can see whether the project is moving forward in the way the donor expected.
Send to the team:
- “Please track both spending and delivery results.”
Key point:
- Spending alone does not show performance.
Official links:
- 2 CFR Part 200
- GAO Green Book
87. Q: Why should NGOs track both financial and program progress for grants?
A: NGOs should track both because a grant report needs to show two things at once: that the money was used correctly and that the funded work actually happened. Financial progress tells the donor whether spending is on track, while program progress shows whether the project is delivering what was promised. If one side is missing, the report is incomplete and the donor may not be able to tell whether the project is healthy or at risk. Combined tracking also helps management catch problems earlier and explain variances more convincingly.
Send to the team:
- “Please report financial and program progress together.”
Key point:
- Financial and program progress should match.
Official links:
- 2 CFR Part 200 Subpart D
- GAO Green Book
88. Q: What is a grant milestone?
A: A grant milestone is a specific checkpoint, activity, or deliverable used to show that a project is moving as planned. Milestones can be tied to dates, outputs, approvals, or completed work packages, and they help management see progress before the final report is due. In financial terms, milestones also help explain whether spending is happening at the right pace for the stage of the project. When milestones are defined clearly, they become a practical bridge between narrative reporting and financial control.
Send to the team:
- “Please define each grant milestone clearly and assign an owner.”
Key point:
- Milestones turn plans into measurable steps.
Official links:
- 2 CFR Part 200
- GAO Green Book
89. Q: How should milestone achievement affect donor reporting?
A: Milestone achievement should be reported with evidence, dates, and any financial impact that followed the completion of the work. The report should show not just that a milestone was reached, but how the organization knows it was reached and what that means for the budget, timeline, or next phase of the project. If there was a delay or a change in scope, that should be explained instead of hidden. Good milestone reporting strengthens trust because it makes the narrative measurable and the financial story easier to follow.
Send to the team:
- “Please link each milestone report to evidence and the date of completion.”
Key point:
- Milestone reporting should be evidence-based.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- 2 CFR Part 200 Subpart D
90. Q: What is the risk of weak donor data management?
A: Weak donor data management can create errors that spread across the whole reporting process. If donor names, grant IDs, reporting dates, or restriction details are wrong or incomplete, finance may prepare the wrong report, miss a deadline, or apply the wrong coding to a transaction. Poor data also makes it harder to reconcile balances or prove that a report is complete. In practice, weak donor data management often shows up later as confusion, rework, and avoidable audit questions.
Send to the team:
- “Please clean donor data and review records regularly.”
Key point:
- Bad data creates reporting and compliance risk.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- 2 CFR Part 200
91. Q: How should donor records be stored and archived?
A: Donor records should be stored in a controlled file structure that makes retrieval easy and unauthorized changes difficult. The archive should preserve the grant agreement, reports, communication logs, approvals, support files, and any versioned schedules used to prepare submissions. Access should be limited to the right staff, and the file structure should be consistent enough that anyone trained on the process can follow it. Good archiving protects the organization long after the report has been sent, especially when questions arise months later.
Send to the team:
- “Please archive donor files in one controlled location.”
Key point:
- Controlled storage protects record integrity.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- 2 CFR Part 200 Subpart D
92. Q: What evidence is needed for donor audits?
A: Donor audits usually require a complete evidence trail that connects the award to the ledger and then to the supporting documents. Typical items include the grant agreement, budget, approvals, invoices, receipts, bank evidence, journal entries, reconciliations, reports, and the log of any corrections or explanations sent to the donor. The exact set depends on the grant and the transaction, but the principle is the same: every material number should be traceable. If the file is complete, the audit becomes a review of evidence rather than a search for missing records.
Send to the team:
- “Please keep a complete audit file for every donor grant.”
Key point:
- Audit evidence should be complete and traceable.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- 2 CFR Part 200 Subpart D
93. Q: How should donor-funded assets be tracked?
A: Donor-funded assets should be tracked from purchase through custody and eventual disposal, with the donor source visible at every stage. The asset register should show the item description, serial number if available, purchase date, funding source, location, custodian, and any special use restriction. This makes it possible to show not only that the asset was bought, but that it was received, tagged, safeguarded, and used in line with the donor terms. Asset tracking is especially important where equipment moves between sites or is used by field staff over time.
Send to the team:
- “Please tag and register every donor-funded asset.”
Key point:
- Donor-funded assets need traceable custody.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- FASB ASU 2020-07
94. Q: What is the connection between grant management and asset control?
A: Grant management determines whether an asset purchase is allowed, while asset control determines what happens after the purchase is approved. The first side asks whether the donor permits the item; the second side asks whether the organization received it, recorded it, tagged it, safeguarded it, and can still account for it later. Both are needed. If the grant side is weak, the item may be ineligible; if the asset side is weak, the organization may not be able to prove where the item went or whether it was used properly.
Send to the team:
- “Please link asset tags to the donor grant that funded the item.”
Key point:
- Funding approval and asset control must match.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- 2 CFR Part 200
95. Q: How should subcontracted donor funds be monitored?
A: Subcontracted donor funds should be monitored with the same seriousness as direct donor funds. The organization should review the subagreement terms, confirm deliverables, check reporting dates, verify payment conditions, and request supporting evidence for the work claimed. If the subcontractor is late or incomplete, the issue may affect not just that subaward but also the prime donor report. Clear monitoring makes downstream spending visible instead of assuming that once the money has been passed out, the responsibility has ended.
Send to the team:
- “Please monitor subrecipients and subcontractors with the same discipline as direct grants.”
Key point:
- Downstream funds still need control.
Official links:
- 2 CFR Part 200
- 2 CFR Part 200 Subpart D
96. Q: What is pass-through funding?
A: Pass-through funding is money that one organization receives and then passes to another organization so the downstream organization can carry out part of the donor-funded work. In this arrangement, the prime recipient remains responsible for oversight, reporting, and ensuring that the funds are used as agreed. That means the transfer is not just a cash movement; it is also a compliance relationship that needs clear terms and documentation. If the agreement is not precise, both organizations may be left unclear about reporting and accountability responsibilities.
Send to the team:
- “Please document any pass-through arrangement clearly in the agreement.”
Key point:
- Pass-through funds need downstream accountability.
Official links:
- 2 CFR Part 200
- 2 CFR Part 200 Subpart D
97. Q: How should pass-through donor obligations be handled?
A: Pass-through donor obligations should be handled in writing from the start. The agreement should define who is responsible for which deliverables, what reports are due, when payments are released, what evidence is required, and how issues will be escalated. Monitoring should then follow the agreement instead of relying on informal trust or verbal updates. This makes the arrangement easier to manage, easier to audit, and easier to explain if something goes wrong downstream.
Send to the team:
- “Please put pass-through responsibilities in the signed agreement and monitor them monthly.”
Key point:
- Pass-through obligations must be explicit and monitored.
Official links:
- 2 CFR Part 200 Subpart D
- GAO Green Book
98. Q: What are the most common mistakes in grant management?
A: The most common mistakes are usually basic process failures rather than rare technical issues. They include late reporting, weak coding, missing support, poor reconciliations, weak tracking of restrictions, and charging costs outside the grant rules. These errors often happen because no single person owns the full process from budget setup through final reporting. The practical lesson is that grant management needs routine discipline, not just an end-of-period review.
Send to the team:
- “Please review grant coding, documentation, and deadlines every month.”
Key point:
- Most grant problems come from weak process discipline.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- 2 CFR Part 200
99. Q: How can stronger donor tracking improve nonprofit accountability?
A: Stronger donor tracking improves accountability by making every major movement of funds easier to trace, explain, and verify. When donor balances, restrictions, deadlines, and communication history are kept clean, management can respond more quickly and confidently to questions from donors, auditors, and the board. It also improves internal decision-making because the team can see where funding is tight, where reporting is overdue, and where correction is needed. In short, better tracking creates better control, better reporting, and better trust.
Send to the team:
- “Please keep donor balances and activity traceable at all times.”
Key point:
- Better tracking improves trust and control.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- FASB ASU 2016-14
100. Q: What does good fund, grant, and donor management look like in practice?
A: Good fund, grant, and donor management looks orderly from the moment the award is accepted to the point the file is closed. The organization sets up the grant correctly, codes transactions consistently, maintains a reporting calendar, collects complete support, reconciles balances regularly, logs communications, and reviews exceptions before they become problems. It also means finance and program staff understand their roles, management reviews the high-risk items, and the board sees the material issues that matter most. When all of those pieces work together, the organization can show that it used donor resources responsibly and can defend the numbers with confidence.
Send to the team:
- “Please keep grant setup, coding, support, reconciliations, and reporting under one controlled process.”
Key point:
- Good management is disciplined, documented, and traceable.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- 2 CFR Part 200
- FASB ASU 2018-08
- FASB ASU 2016-14

Table 5
Q&A NGO Finance and Accounting
1. Q: What is cost allocation in NGO finance?
A: Cost allocation is the disciplined process of assigning shared costs to the programs, projects, grants, cost centers, or functions that benefited from them. In practice, it turns one expense that sits at the organization level into meaningful slices that reflect the way services are actually delivered. A strong allocation process makes the finance records more honest, the program reports more complete, and the management discussion more useful because it shows the true cost of running the mission rather than only the direct spend on the activity itself. In practice, this means the finance team should be able to show the logic from source record to ledger entry to report without relying on memory. This is especially important where donor restrictions, grant conditions, and program budgets all overlap in the same reporting period. It also keeps the reporting line between budget owners and finance staff clear.
Send to the team:
- “Please allocate shared costs using a documented method that reflects actual benefit.”
Key point:
- Cost allocation spreads shared costs fairly.
Official links:
- 2 CFR Part 200
- GAO Green Book
2. Q: Why is cost allocation important for nonprofits?
A: It is important because nonprofits rarely spend money in clean, isolated silos. One finance team supports many grants; one office supports multiple programs; one director may supervise several projects. Allocation makes those common costs visible instead of hiding them inside general overhead. That matters for donor reporting, grant budgeting, board oversight, and internal management because each audience needs a picture of the full cost of service delivery, not just the easiest-to-track direct charges. In practice, that usually means the same schedule must work for month-end close, donor review, and later audit testing. That is why the same expense can look small in the ledger but large in management terms once the full support structure is included. It also prevents support costs from disappearing into overly simple direct expense lines.
Send to the team:
- “Please track shared costs so each program shows its true cost.”
Key point:
- Allocation makes nonprofit reporting more complete.
Official links:
- 2 CFR Part 200
- IRS Publication 557
3. Q: What is the difference between direct costs and indirect costs?
A: Direct costs can be linked to one program or project with little judgment, such as a project-specific consultant, field transport for a single activity, or materials bought only for one grant. Indirect costs support more than one activity and therefore need a reasonable split method. The distinction matters because direct costs can usually be charged straight to the beneficiary, while indirect costs require a policy-based approach that is consistent and defensible. In practice, the test is whether a reviewer can see the connection without guessing. A clean distinction here also helps the organization avoid confusion between accounting classification and operational responsibility. The file should show why one charge was direct and another had to be split.
Send to the team:
- “Please charge direct costs to the project and allocate indirect costs separately.”
Key point:
- Direct costs are traceable; indirect costs are shared.
Official links:
- 2 CFR Part 200
- GAO Green Book
4. Q: What is the difference between program costs and support costs?
A: Program costs are the costs that exist because the organization is delivering services, running field activities, or producing mission outputs. Support costs are the background functions that make those services possible, such as finance, HR, admin, leadership, and procurement. The line between them matters because program costs show mission execution, while support costs show the infrastructure that enables the mission. Both are necessary, but they tell different stories in the reports. In practice, the report should still make sense if a program manager, finance officer, or donor reviewer reads it on its own. This separation also makes it easier to explain results to non-finance staff without losing the accounting logic. It also makes the reports easier to explain to program teams who do not live in the ledger.
Send to the team:
- “Please separate program delivery costs from support costs in reporting.”
Key point:
- Program costs serve the mission directly; support costs enable it.
Official links:
- IRS Publication 557
- GAO Green Book
5. Q: What is the difference between shared costs and project-specific costs?
A: Project-specific costs belong to one grant, one activity, or one workstream and should be charged there directly whenever evidence supports it. Shared costs are used by more than one project or department, so they need allocation. In a real NGO, the same vehicle, office, staff member, or software license may support several activities at once. The accounting question is not only who used it first, but who benefited from it and in what proportion. In practice, the file should show why one charge was direct and another had to be split. A disciplined file trail is what keeps the organization from having to rebuild the story during audit or donor review. It also reduces later disputes about what should have been charged where.
Send to the team:
- “Please identify whether each cost is project-specific or shared before posting it.”
Key point:
- Shared costs need allocation; project-specific costs do not.
Official links:
- 2 CFR Part 200
- GAO Green Book
6. Q: Why do NGOs need a cost allocation policy?
A: An allocation policy creates a stable rulebook for deciding how shared costs are distributed. Without it, each month can become a separate negotiation, and similar expenses may be treated differently depending on who prepared them. A policy also helps train staff, reduces disputes, supports audit testing, and shows donors that cost sharing is based on consistent logic rather than convenience. In a well-run organization, policy is what prevents the finance team from improvising the same issue over and over again. In practice, the policy becomes the reference point when staff disagree or when the team changes over time. Over time, the policy becomes part of the organization’s control environment and reduces the need for case-by-case judgment. It also gives the finance team one stable reference when the organization grows or changes.
Send to the team:
- “Please follow the approved allocation policy for all shared costs.”
Key point:
- A policy creates consistency and control.
Official links:
- 2 CFR Part 200
- GAO Green Book
7. Q: What should a cost allocation policy cover?
A: It should explain the purpose of allocation, the scope of costs covered, the approved cost drivers, the formula or method used, the frequency of recalculation, the documents needed to support the split, the review and approval roles, the exception process, and the date when the policy is next reviewed. The clearer the policy, the less room there is for guesswork when staff post expenses, close the books, or prepare donor reports. In practice, the policy should be detailed enough to keep people from inventing new rules in the middle of a close. It should also be written in a way that field teams, program staff, and finance staff can all apply it consistently. The policy also helps when field operations need a practical method rather than a perfect one.
Send to the team:
- “Please document the allocation method, reviewer, and approval process.”
Key point:
- The policy should define the full allocation process.
Official links:
- 2 CFR Part 200
- GAO Green Book
8. Q: Who should approve the allocation method used by an NGO?
A: The method should be approved by management with finance oversight because allocation affects both financial reporting and operational decisions. Where a change is material, it should also pass through the organization’s governance or policy approval route so the new method is visible, reviewed, and documented. That is important because changing an allocation basis changes how programs look on paper, even if the underlying activities have not changed at all. In practice, approval creates ownership and makes later review much easier. Formal approval also protects staff because it reduces the chance that one person’s preference becomes a hidden rule. It also creates a stronger record of who accepted the method and when.
Send to the team:
- “Please have finance and management approve any allocation method changes.”
Key point:
- Allocation methods need formal approval.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- 2 CFR Part 200
9. Q: What is a cost driver?
A: A cost driver is the measurable factor used to split a shared cost. It is the logic that connects the expense to the benefit received. For example, headcount can drive HR costs, square footage can drive rent, mileage can drive fuel, and time usage can drive staff cost allocations. The goal is not mathematical elegance; the goal is a sensible and supportable link between the cost and the activity that caused it. In practice, the best driver is usually the simplest one that still reflects how the cost is actually consumed. The best driver is the one that reflects the cost behavior without creating unnecessary administrative work. It also makes it easier to apply the same logic to similar costs later.
Send to the team:
- “Please use a measurable cost driver for each shared cost pool.”
Key point:
- A cost driver is the basis for splitting a shared cost.
Official links:
- 2 CFR Part 200
- GAO Green Book
10. Q: Why should allocation be based on a logical cost driver?
A: Because the driver should reflect actual use or actual benefit as closely as possible. If the basis is disconnected from the real activity pattern, the allocation becomes arbitrary and can mislead management, donors, and auditors. A logical driver gives the finance team a defensible story: this pool was split this way because that is the best available proxy for who used the resource. That is what makes the allocation credible rather than merely convenient. In practice, the stronger the link between benefit and basis, the easier the allocation is to defend in review meetings. The aim is to avoid a split that is technically neat but operationally misleading. It also protects the report from becoming a judgment call every time a new charge appears.
Send to the team:
- “Please choose an allocation base that reflects real use.”
Key point:
- The allocation driver should match the benefit received.
Official links:
- 2 CFR Part 200
- GAO Green Book
11. Q: What makes an allocation basis fair?
A: A fair basis distributes cost in a way that reasonably reflects who benefited and by how much. It should not overburden one grant simply because it is easiest to code or because that grant has more budget room. Fairness in NGO allocation is less about perfect precision and more about a reasonable, repeatable, and documented approximation of benefit. The process should be understandable enough that the same logic can be applied again next month. In practice, fairness is measured by whether the outcome feels reasonable when explained aloud. This is what keeps shared-cost reporting credible when different managers review the same numbers. It also helps leadership see the real cost of the mission, not only the invoice total.
Send to the team:
- “Please use a basis that treats similar benefit levels similarly.”
Key point:
- Fair allocation matches benefit and burden.
Official links:
- 2 CFR Part 200
- GAO Green Book
12. Q: What makes an allocation basis consistent?
A: A consistent basis is applied the same way across periods and across similar cost pools unless a documented operational change justifies a revision. If one month rent is split by headcount, the next month by room count, and the next month by budget size, the reports lose comparability. Consistency allows trend analysis, budget comparison, and audit review to work properly because people can see the effect of the underlying activity rather than the effect of a changing formula. In practice, consistency is what allows comparison from one month to the next without reinterpreting the numbers. The real value of consistency is that it makes variance analysis and period comparisons meaningful. It also improves the quality of resource planning for the next period.
Send to the team:
- “Please apply the same allocation rule each month unless approved otherwise.”
Key point:
- Consistency improves comparability.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- 2 CFR Part 200
13. Q: What makes an allocation basis reasonable?
A: A reasonable basis is practical to operate, supported by available records, and proportionate to the size of the organization. A small NGO may not need a complex model if a simpler measure gives a defensible result and can be maintained accurately. Reasonable also means the method can be explained to staff, management, and donors without requiring a long technical debate every time it is used. In practice, reasonableness means the method can be used repeatedly without becoming a burden on the team. A method is not reasonable if it consumes more time than the cost it is trying to allocate. Practicality matters because NGOs need methods that survive turnover, pressure, and close deadlines.
Send to the team:
- “Please use a method that can be explained and supported with evidence.”
Key point:
- Reasonable means supportable and practical.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- 2 CFR Part 200
14. Q: What are common allocation bases used in NGOs?
A: Common bases include headcount, staff time, floor space, vehicle mileage, transaction volume, unit counts, service usage, and direct consumption logs. The best base depends on what is being allocated and how the cost behaves. Rent usually follows space; staff support often follows time; transport often follows mileage; IT support may follow user count or device count. The right basis is the one that most closely tracks the real pattern of benefit. In practice, the method should fit the cost, not force the cost into a one-size-fits-all split. That also keeps the cost basis understandable for non-finance users. It also ensures the method can live inside the organization’s real capacity.
Send to the team:
- “Please match each shared cost to the most relevant allocation base.”
Key point:
- The right base depends on the cost being allocated.
Official links:
- 2 CFR Part 200
- GAO Green Book
15. Q: How should shared rent be allocated across programs?
A: Shared rent is usually allocated using square footage, desk count, room usage, or another space measure that reflects how much of the facility each program occupies. If one program uses a storage room, another uses open office space, and a third uses only occasional desk access, the split should reflect that pattern rather than forcing an equal share. The file should keep the floor plan or space schedule so the basis can be checked later. In practice, a floor plan or occupancy record is often enough if it is current and well maintained. A current space schedule makes it much easier to justify the split if questions arise later. It also gives the organization a practical method it can keep using without strain.
Send to the team:
- “Please allocate rent using a documented space basis.”
Key point:
- Rent is often best allocated by space used.
Official links:
- 2 CFR Part 200
- GAO Green Book
16. Q: How should shared utilities be allocated across programs?
A: Utilities can be allocated using floor space, headcount, equipment load, metered usage, or another logical proxy when direct metering is not available. Electricity, water, internet, and similar costs are often driven by a mix of people, space, and equipment, so the team should choose the measure that best matches the actual consumption pattern. The important part is to avoid a vague split that cannot be explained or reproduced. In practice, the team should prefer the best available proxy instead of inventing precision that the records cannot support. The team should also check whether a more direct usage measure exists before settling for a proxy. It also helps when field operations need a practical method rather than a perfect one.
Send to the team:
- “Please use the most practical usage-based measure for utilities.”
Key point:
- Utilities should follow the use pattern as closely as possible.
Official links:
- 2 CFR Part 200
- GAO Green Book
17. Q: How should shared staff time be allocated across projects?
A: Shared staff time should be allocated using timesheets, activity logs, supervisor-certified estimates, or another approved record of actual effort. In an NGO, the same officer may support proposal writing, grant management, reporting, and field follow-up in the same week. Time allocation makes that mixed reality visible in the books. The more direct the evidence, the stronger the allocation and the easier it is to defend during review. In practice, timesheets become most useful when they are completed close to the time worked, not reconstructed at period end. Regularly completed timesheets help prevent month-end estimates from becoming the default source of truth. They also reduce the need for after-the-fact reconstruction during close.
Send to the team:
- “Please record staff time by project using an approved method.”
Key point:
- Staff time allocation should be based on evidence of work done.
Official links:
- 2 CFR Part 200
- GAO Green Book
18. Q: How should shared office supplies be allocated?
A: Office supplies should be charged directly when they are clearly purchased for one project. When they are general shared supplies, they can be allocated using a simple basis such as headcount, desk usage, or consumption by department. The key is to keep the method proportionate to the item. A box of printer paper does not need the same complexity as a large rent allocation, but it still should not vanish into an unexplained overhead bucket. In practice, even small supply costs matter when donor reports need a complete picture of shared spending. Even modest supply costs can become important when several grants rely on the same stock. It also avoids hiding small shared items inside broad overhead without review.
Send to the team:
- “Please separate project-specific supplies from general shared supplies.”
Key point:
- Use direct charge when possible; allocate only when needed.
Official links:
- 2 CFR Part 200
- GAO Green Book
19. Q: How should shared vehicle costs be allocated?
A: Shared vehicle costs are often best allocated using mileage, trip logs, route records, fuel usage, or trip purpose records. A vehicle that carries staff to multiple projects should not be assigned to only one grant unless the logbooks truly support that treatment. Allocation based on use is especially important where transport is a visible donor cost and where field work is spread across many activities. In practice, transport logs are often the difference between a believable allocation and a weak estimate. Mileage records are especially useful when travel routes vary by project or location. It also helps the team explain transport splits using visible records.
Send to the team:
- “Please support vehicle allocations with trip or mileage records.”
Key point:
- Vehicle costs should follow actual use.
Official links:
- 2 CFR Part 200
- GAO Green Book
20. Q: How should shared internet and communications costs be allocated?
A: These costs can be allocated using headcount, user access, department usage, device count, or activity-based usage where available. Communications costs often support the whole organization, so the basis should be simple enough to maintain but still reflect the likely beneficiary group. A reasonable approach is to choose the proxy that best matches actual usage and to apply it consistently so the monthly trend is not distorted by random judgment. In practice, communications cost drivers should be chosen once and then used steadily unless the operation changes. Once chosen, the basis should be documented so staff do not reinvent the logic each month. It also keeps the cost basis understandable for non-finance users.
Send to the team:
- “Please use a simple usage-based basis for communications costs.”
Key point:
- Communications costs should follow the likely users.
Official links:
- 2 CFR Part 200
- GAO Green Book
21. Q: What is program costing?
A: Program costing is the process of calculating the full cost of delivering a program, not only the direct out-of-pocket expense. It includes direct spend plus a share of shared support and overhead where appropriate. That makes it more useful than raw expenditure because it tells management what the program truly consumed from the organization, which is essential for planning, pricing, fundraising, and sustainability decisions. In practice, full cost includes the support structure that allows the activity to exist at all. Full cost is what the activity really consumes, not only what the invoice shows. It also helps management see which programs are genuinely economical, which are being subsidized, and which consume more support than expected.
Send to the team:
- “Please include both direct and allocated costs when calculating program cost.”
Key point:
- Program costing shows the total cost of delivery.
Official links:
- 2 CFR Part 200
- IRS Publication 557
22. Q: Why is program costing important for nonprofit management?
A: It helps management see which programs are genuinely economical, which are being subsidized, and which consume more support than expected. Without program costing, decisions are often based on direct spend alone, which can make a program look inexpensive even when it depends heavily on central support. Proper costing also helps leadership explain the true resource requirement to donors and boards in a realistic way. In practice, management uses this information to avoid decisions based only on visible direct spend. The difference matters because project design and resource planning depend on the correct view of cost. Program costing also helps set realistic expectations for future grant proposals.
Send to the team:
- “Please review program cost data when making planning decisions.”
Key point:
- Program costing supports better management decisions.
Official links:
- 2 CFR Part 200
- GAO Green Book
23. Q: What is the difference between program costing and budgeting?
A: Budgeting sets the plan before the period begins. Program costing measures or estimates the actual full cost of delivery. A budget may say what management intends to spend, while program costing shows what the mission really required once support costs are included. Both are useful, but they answer different questions: one is about planning, the other is about the cost of execution. In practice, the budget for planning and program costing for actual cost analysis should be used together. Reminder: direct spend is only part of the story. The difference can change how a grant is presented and how a program is judged.
Send to the team:
- “Please use the budget for planning and program costing for actual cost analysis.”
Key point:
- Budgeting is forward-looking; costing measures delivery cost.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- 2 CFR Part 200
24. Q: How does cost allocation affect program costing?
A: Allocation determines how much of the shared organization cost appears in each program’s full cost. If allocation is too low, a program looks artificially cheap; if too high, it looks inflated and may appear unsustainable. That is why the allocation logic is not a side issue. It directly shapes the management view of performance, efficiency, and sustainability across the portfolio. In practice, allocation is what makes a program cost report credible rather than partial. The same rule also protects internal comparisons from being skewed by one-off coding choices. It also makes donor conversations more honest and precise.
Send to the team:
- “Please include allocated support costs in program cost reports.”
Key point:
- Allocation changes the reported full cost of a program.
Official links:
- 2 CFR Part 200
- GAO Green Book
25. Q: How does program costing support donor reporting?
A: It gives donors a fuller picture of what their support actually enabled. Many donors want more than a simple list of direct expenditures; they want to understand the real cost of delivery, including the support infrastructure that makes the project work. Program costing helps the organization explain that reality clearly, show whether the funding covered the full need, and open honest conversations about sustainability and overhead recovery. In practice, donors generally understand better when the organization can show a complete and coherent cost story. That information often becomes central to reserve planning and fundraising strategy. It also shows when one project is depending too heavily on central support.
Send to the team:
- “Please report program costs in a way donors can trace and understand.”
Key point:
- Donors need the full cost of the funded work.
Official links:
- 2 CFR Part 200
- IRS Publication 557
26. Q: How does program costing support management decisions?
A: It helps leadership compare programs, assess efficiency, test whether scale improves cost effectiveness, and decide whether a program should expand, redesign, or wind down. It also helps with staffing and fundraising because management can estimate the resource requirement for the next phase more credibly. Good program costing turns finance from a recorder of history into a decision support tool for the next operating cycle. In practice, the leadership team needs this information before the numbers turn into a sustainability problem. Sustainability improves when the organization knows what each program truly requires. Program costing is one of the clearest tests of long-term viability.
Send to the team:
- “Please use program cost data when reviewing performance and sustainability.”
Key point:
- Program costing improves decision-making.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- 2 CFR Part 200
27. Q: What is full cost recovery?
A: Full cost recovery means recovering not only the direct costs of a grant or activity but also the fair share of indirect and support costs needed to run it properly. In practical terms, it means the organization tries to secure enough funding so the work does not silently drain unrestricted reserves. This is one of the most important sustainability questions in nonprofit finance because under-recovered support costs often accumulate invisibly over time. In practice, full cost recovery is one of the clearest tests of long-term viability. Hidden support gaps often surface only when budgets tighten or a major grant ends. It also protects the organization from chronic underfunding.
Send to the team:
- “Please include indirect and support cost recovery in funding discussions.”
Key point:
- Full cost recovery covers the whole cost of delivery.
Official links:
- 2 CFR Part 200
- IRS Publication 557
28. Q: Why is full cost recovery important for NGOs?
A: It protects the organization from chronic underfunding, improves operational resilience, and reduces the risk that unrestricted income has to subsidize every restricted grant. Full cost recovery also helps leadership negotiate more honestly with donors because it clarifies what the work actually costs. In a long-running NGO, the failure to recover support costs often becomes a strategic problem long before it becomes a bookkeeping problem. In practice, underfunding support costs can quietly weaken systems long before the organization notices a crisis. The risk is not only underreporting but also underfunding the organization itself. That is why full recovery supports sustainability.
Send to the team:
- “Please consider full cost when negotiating grants and budgets.”
Key point:
- Full recovery supports sustainability.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- 2 CFR Part 200
29. Q: What is the risk of underallocating support costs?
A: Underallocating support costs makes programs look cheaper than they really are. That can lead to bad decisions, unrealistic donor discussions, and hidden pressure on unrestricted funds. It may also distort internal comparisons between projects, because one program may appear lean simply because part of its real support burden is parked elsewhere or left unassigned. In practice, hidden support gaps often show up only when budgets tighten or a major grant ends. Proper allocation is one of the main ways to keep cost reporting honest. Underallocation hides real program cost and can push pressure into the next period.
Send to the team:
- “Please make sure support costs are not omitted from program costing.”
Key point:
- Underallocation hides real program cost.
Official links:
- 2 CFR Part 200
- GAO Green Book
30. Q: What is the risk of overallocating support costs?
A: Overallocating support costs can make a program seem more expensive than it truly is and can unfairly influence whether management keeps, scales, or closes it. It can also create donor reporting tension if the allocated cost is not well supported. The problem is not only accounting error; it is strategic distortion. A budget that shifts too much overhead into one project can mislead the whole organization about performance. In practice, overallocation can be as damaging as omission because it distorts comparative analysis. The same rule also protects internal comparisons from being skewed by one-off coding choices. It keeps the organization from absorbing support costs without realizing it.
Send to the team:
- “Please avoid shifting more support cost to a program than the evidence supports.”
Key point:
- Overallocation distorts program results.
Official links:
- 2 CFR Part 200
- GAO Green Book
31. Q: How should an NGO treat headquarters costs?
A: Headquarters costs should be classified according to their actual function, not just by where the invoice arrived. Some HQ costs are clearly direct to a function, while others support the whole organization and need allocation across programs or grants. The file should distinguish between direct HQ activity and general headquarters infrastructure so that the reports do not bury central support inside a vague admin line. In practice, HQ charges should not be treated as a dumping ground for anything difficult to classify. HQ should be treated like any other cost center: useful, necessary, and still subject to discipline. It also keeps headquarters from becoming a catch-all for hard-to-classify expenses.
Send to the team:
- “Please identify which HQ costs are shared and which are program-specific.”
Key point:
- HQ costs are often indirect and need allocation.
Official links:
- 2 CFR Part 200
- GAO Green Book
32. Q: How should an NGO treat field office costs?
A: Field office costs should be charged directly when they belong to one project or one location. If the office supports several grants, the shared portion should be allocated using a local basis such as headcount, room use, or field activity volume. Field offices often operate in limited conditions, so the method should be simple enough to maintain and robust enough to survive staff turnover and changing local conditions. In practice, local conditions matter, so the allocation method should be easy for field staff to maintain. The file should also be able to survive staff turnover and still make sense. It also acknowledges that field reality often requires flexible but documented methods.
Send to the team:
- “Please separate direct field costs from shared field office costs.”
Key point:
- Field office costs may be direct or shared depending on use.
Official links:
- 2 CFR Part 200
- GAO Green Book
33. Q: What is overhead?
A: Overhead is the collection of support costs that keep the organization running but cannot be traced neatly to one project. It usually includes finance, administration, HR, executive oversight, and parts of procurement or IT. Overhead is not wasted money; it is the structure that allows the mission to function. The real question is whether it is appropriately sized, transparently reported, and fairly recovered. In practice, overhead should be transparent, not treated as an embarrassment. Separating indirect recovery helps management understand whether growth is actually financed. It also helps leadership see whether indirect recovery is keeping pace with growth.
Send to the team:
- “Please track overhead separately from direct project costs.”
Key point:
- Overhead supports multiple activities.
Official links:
- IRS Publication 557
- GAO Green Book
34. Q: What is indirect cost recovery?
A: Indirect cost recovery is the portion of funding an NGO receives to pay for shared support costs that are necessary but not directly attributable to one project. It is often the financial bridge between restricted program funding and the cost of keeping the organization functioning as a whole. When recovery is weak, the finance team may need to explain why a busy program portfolio still leaves the organization short of general operating capacity. In practice, separate tracking helps management see whether the organization is living within its means. This separate tracking often reveals gaps that were hidden inside one blended admin number. It also makes funding gaps easier to explain in board papers.
Send to the team:
- “Please recover indirect costs where donor rules allow.”
Key point:
- Indirect recovery helps fund shared support functions.
Official links:
- 2 CFR Part 200
- IRS Publication 557
35. Q: Why should indirect costs be tracked separately?
A: They should be tracked separately so the organization can see how much support cost is recovered, how much is absorbed, and how much is left unfunded. Without that visibility, leadership may think programs are fully covered when the central office is quietly draining reserves to keep them going. Separate tracking makes the gap visible and creates a basis for better fundraising and pricing decisions. In practice, separating indirect recovery helps management understand whether growth is actually financed. That visibility is essential for boards and leadership when they assess long-term financial health. It also lets reviewers trace the accounting treatment back to the file evidence.
Send to the team:
- “Please report indirect cost recovery separately from direct project income.”
Key point:
- Separate tracking shows the true support gap.
Official links:
- 2 CFR Part 200
- GAO Green Book
36. Q: How should an NGO identify which costs are directly traceable?
A: A cost is directly traceable when source documents, approvals, and coding show that it belongs to one project, one donor, or one activity without needing a split formula. The evidence should be strong enough that another reviewer could follow the same trail and reach the same conclusion. If the answer depends on judgment or estimates, then the cost probably needs allocation instead of a direct charge. In practice, direct traceability is what makes coding rules auditable. The stronger the trail, the fewer questions the team will face later. It also reduces the risk that the same cost is treated differently by different staff.
Send to the team:
- “Please attach each directly traceable cost to the right project code.”
Key point:
- Direct traceability removes the need for allocation.
Official links:
- 2 CFR Part 200
- GAO Green Book
37. Q: What is a direct charge?
A: A direct charge is a posting made straight to the beneficiary project or program because the supporting evidence shows that the cost belongs there. It is the cleanest way to account for an expense when the connection is obvious and documented. Good direct charging reduces later cleanup work, improves donor reporting accuracy, and prevents shared costs from being pushed into the wrong pool by default. In practice, direct charging reduces clean-up work at close if the support file is strong from the start. A direct posting should always be supported by source documents that identify the beneficiary. It also makes direct charging faster when the evidence is already clear.
Send to the team:
- “Please direct-charge costs only when the project beneficiary is clear.”
Key point:
- Direct charges avoid unnecessary allocation.
Official links:
- 2 CFR Part 200
- IRS Publication 557
38. Q: When should a cost be charged directly to a project?
A: A cost should be charged directly when it was purchased exclusively for that project and the source evidence identifies that project as the sole beneficiary. The item should not be shared, speculative, or dependent on an after-the-fact explanation. If the support file is weak, the safer approach is often to hold the charge until the correct allocation or coding is confirmed. In practice, project-only items are simplest when they are coded at the point of purchase or commitment. Direct posting saves time at month end and improves confidence in the books. It also prevents project-only items from being mixed into shared pools.
Send to the team:
- “Please use direct charging for project-only expenses.”
Key point:
- Project-only costs should be directly charged.
Official links:
- 2 CFR Part 200
- GAO Green Book
39. Q: When should a cost be allocated rather than directly charged?
A: A cost should be allocated whenever it benefits more than one project, more than one donor, or more than one department. The presence of shared benefit is the signal that a split is needed. Allocation is also appropriate when no single project can be identified as the sole beneficiary and the best available evidence shows mixed use or mixed service delivery. In practice, mixed-use items are easier to handle when the organization accepts allocation instead of forcing certainty. The schedule should be something a reviewer can follow without needing the preparer to explain each step. It also helps the team avoid forcing a cost into a single project when it is truly shared.
Send to the team:
- “Please allocate any cost that supports more than one activity.”
Key point:
- Shared benefit means allocation is needed.
Official links:
- 2 CFR Part 200
- GAO Green Book
40. Q: What is a cost allocation schedule?
A: It is the working paper that shows the cost pool, the allocation basis, the percentage or formula applied, and the amount assigned to each program or project. A good schedule is more than a math sheet; it is the evidence file that lets reviewers understand how the split was produced. It should be easy to follow from source data to final posting. In practice, the schedule is also a management tool, not just an audit file. Reusable schedules also help standardize monthly close work across teams. It also turns the schedule into a working document rather than a static spreadsheet.
Send to the team:
- “Please maintain a schedule for each shared cost category.”
Key point:
- The schedule shows how costs were split.
Official links:
- 2 CFR Part 200
- GAO Green Book
41. Q: What information should appear in a cost allocation schedule?
A: It should show the cost pool, the driver used, the source records, the calculation logic, the period covered, the preparer, the reviewer, the approver, and the final amounts posted. That way the schedule can survive audit testing, management questions, and later replication. If any of those pieces are missing, the schedule may still be mathematically correct but operationally weak. In practice, a good schedule can be reused as the basis for next month’s posting. The schedule should be able to survive review without needing a long verbal explanation. It also makes repeat posting smoother for the next close cycle.
Send to the team:
- “Please include the source data and the calculation logic in every schedule.”
Key point:
- The schedule must be traceable and reviewable.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- 2 CFR Part 200
42. Q: How often should allocation schedules be reviewed?
A: They should be reviewed monthly or at each reporting close, and again whenever the business pattern changes in a meaningful way. If a new project starts, a field office is added, staffing shifts, or the use of a shared resource changes, the allocation basis may need revision. Review is what keeps a fair method from becoming stale and misleading. In practice, review should be routine rather than a one-time correction exercise. Regular review prevents old assumptions from staying in place after the work has changed. It also keeps assumptions from outliving the operating reality they were built for.
Send to the team:
- “Please review allocations at each close and after any major operational change.”
Key point:
- Allocation should be reviewed on a recurring basis.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- 2 CFR Part 200
43. Q: Who should review allocation calculations?
A: Finance should prepare the calculation, and someone independent of the preparer, such as a finance manager, controller, or director, should review the assumptions, math, and resulting postings before the allocation is finalized. Independence matters because allocation affects reported program performance and can be tempting to bend toward convenience. A second set of eyes is one of the simplest ways to prevent quiet error. In practice, an independent reviewer should ask whether the basis still fits the actual pattern of use. A good reviewer looks for whether the basis still matches the current operating reality. It also provides a natural point for asking whether the basis still fits.
Send to the team:
- “Please use independent review for allocation calculations.”
Key point:
- Allocation should be checked by someone independent of the preparer.

- GAO Green Book

44. Q: What documents support allocation calculations?
A: Support can include timesheets, attendance records, floor plans, mileage logs, usage reports, headcount schedules, vendor invoices, service tickets, and approved policy references. The point is not to create a paper pile; it is to keep enough evidence to show why the split was made. The stronger and more specific the underlying document, the easier the allocation is to explain later. In practice, evidence quality matters more than the number of attachments. The file should also be able to survive review and audit without being rebuilt. It also improves the quality of support files for review and audit.
Send to the team:
- “Please keep the source evidence with each allocation schedule.”

45. Q: What is the role of timesheets in staff cost allocation?
A: Timesheets show how staff effort was distributed across projects or functions. They are especially useful where one person supports multiple grants and the organization needs to know who received the benefit of each portion of time. Timesheets work best when they are completed regularly, reviewed by a supervisor, and aligned to real activity rather than retroactive memory. In practice, time records should reflect actual work patterns rather than broad estimates after the fact. Timesheets should be completed close to the time worked, not reconstructed later. They also distinguish attendance from actual work allocation.
Send to the team:
- “Please complete timesheets accurately and on time.”
Key point:
- Timesheets provide direct evidence of labor use.
Official links:
- 2 CFR Part 200
- GAO Green Book
46. Q: What is the role of attendance records in allocation?
A: Attendance records can support whether a person was present, but they do not by themselves show how the person’s time was used across different activities. For that reason, attendance is weaker evidence than a timesheet for allocation purposes. It can still be helpful as a secondary check, especially where field work or shift patterns make simple presence important. In practice, attendance shows presence, not detailed effort. The record should not be overstated as a labor allocation tool. It also keeps the organization from relying on a weak proxy where better evidence exists.
Send to the team:
- “Please use attendance records only as a secondary support unless time records exist.”
Key point:
- Attendance shows presence, not detailed effort.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- 2 CFR Part 200
47. Q: What is the role of square footage in rent allocation?
A: Square footage is a practical driver when rent and facility costs are tied to physical space. It helps show which program uses how much of the building, whether by office, storage, meeting area, or field room. The organization should keep a current space schedule or floor plan so the calculation can be checked and updated when the layout changes. In practice, updated floor plans prevent old occupancy assumptions from lingering in the books. Space records should be checked whenever offices change. It also keeps the floor-space basis tied to current occupancy.

48. Q: What is the role of headcount in allocation?
A: Headcount is often used when a shared cost is driven mainly by the number of people supported, such as HR, office administration, supervision, or basic staff services. It is simple and easy to maintain, which makes it useful for recurring support allocations. It should still be chosen thoughtfully because not every people-related cost is truly driven by simple headcount alone. In practice, headcount is useful, but it should not override better evidence when better evidence exists. The model should be grounded in a reasonable proxy rather than guesswork. It also ensures the model is grounded in a reasonable proxy rather than guesswork.

49. Q: What is the role of usage data in allocation?
A: Usage data shows actual consumption or activity, such as mileage, minutes, machine time, units produced, support tickets, or room occupancy. It is often the strongest allocation evidence because it comes closest to the real pattern of benefit. Where usage data exists, the finance team should usually prefer it over rough proxies because it improves both fairness and auditability. In practice, actual usage is the closest thing to direct evidence when allocation is shared. A practical method is usually the one staff can execute correctly every month. It also reduces the need for broad assumptions.
Send to the team:
- “Please use actual usage data where it is available.”
Key point:
- Usage data gives the strongest allocation support.
Official links:
- 2 CFR Part 200

50. Q: Why should allocation methods match actual usage?
A: Allocation methods should match actual usage so that cost ends up where the benefit really flowed. When the basis is close to usage, the resulting reports are more accurate, more credible, and more useful for decision-making. That does not mean perfection is required. It means the organization should choose the best available measure and explain its logic clearly so the allocation can be trusted. In practice, the best method is the one the team can maintain accurately over time. Matching usage improves fairness, reporting quality, and confidence in the numbers. It also reduces the need for complicated explanations when the numbers are reviewed.
Send to the team:
- “Please use the method that best reflects actual benefit received.”
Key point:
- Matching usage improves fairness and reporting quality.
Official links:
- 2 CFR Part 200
- GAO Green Book
51. Q: What is a cost center?
A: A cost center is an internal reporting unit used to collect, monitor, and explain costs without creating a separate legal entity. In an NGO, it may represent a department, branch, field office, program team, or support function such as finance or HR. The practical value of a cost center is that it gives management a clean view of where spending happens and who is responsible for it, which is essential when resources must be tracked carefully across missions, projects, and locations.

When a nonprofit uses cost centers well, it can compare actual spending against budgets, identify cost pressure early, and explain results to leadership and donors more clearly. It also helps prevent the common problem of all expenses being mixed into one broad category where nobody can tell which team used what. A cost center is therefore not just an accounting label; it is a management tool that supports accountability, planning, and control.
Send to the team:
- “Please code expenses to the correct cost center every time.”
Key point:
- A cost center helps track spending by responsibility area.
Official links:
- IRS Managerial Costing
- GAO Green Book
52. Q: How do cost centers help with program costing?
A: Cost centers help program costing by separating costs into meaningful internal buckets that reflect actual activity and responsibility. When costs are recorded by cost center, finance can see which parts of the organization are driving spending, which functions are shared, and which costs should later be allocated into programs. That makes it easier to build a realistic picture of the true cost of delivering services, not just the direct costs that are obvious at first glance.

In practice, a cost center can hold payroll, travel, office, or support costs that relate to a specific team or field location. From there, management can review whether the program is operating within budget, whether overhead is rising too fast, and whether shared services are being distributed fairly. This improves not only reporting but also decision-making, because leaders can stop guessing and start seeing where resources are actually consumed.
Send to the team:
- “Please use cost centers consistently for program costing.”
Key point:
- Cost centers make program cost tracking more visible.
Official links:
- IRS Managerial Costing
- HHS Cost Allocation Services
53. Q: What is a project code?
A: A project code is a transaction identifier used to group accounting activity for a specific grant, campaign, initiative, activity, or workstream. In a nonprofit setting, it is one of the most important tools for linking income and expenditure to the correct purpose. Without it, grant spending can become mixed together, and reporting can quickly become unreliable.

Project codes help finance trace the full lifecycle of a funded activity: the budget, the commitments, the actual expenditure, and the final reporting. They also support monitoring during implementation because staff can compare actual costs against the project budget and identify overspending or underspending while there is still time to act. A well-designed project code structure also makes closeout simpler because supporting documents, reports, and balances can be reviewed against one clear reference point.
Send to the team:
- “Please assign the correct project code before posting.”
Key point:
- Project codes identify the purpose of spending.
Official links:
- IRS Form 990 Instructions
- GAO Green Book
54. Q: How do project codes help track cost by grant?
A: Project codes connect each cost to the correct grant or donor-funded activity, which is essential when one organization manages multiple awards at the same time. By assigning the code at the transaction level, finance creates a reliable audit trail from the source document to the donor report. This reduces the risk of charging the wrong grant, double-counting costs, or leaving shared items unassigned.

They also make it easier to answer donor questions because the organization can show exactly what was spent, when it was spent, and under which funded activity. During grant closeout, project codes help confirm whether any balance remains, whether all deliverables were supported by the correct costs, and whether the award is ready to be formally closed. In that sense, the code is not just a number; it is the backbone of grant-level traceability.
Send to the team:
- “Please code every grant-related cost to the right project.”
Key point:
- Project codes protect grant-level traceability.
Official links:
- HHS Cost Allocation Services
- IRS Form 990 Instructions
55. Q: What is a department code?
A: A department code is an internal classification used to identify the function or unit responsible for a cost. Typical examples include finance, HR, operations, fundraising, logistics, or a field office. It allows the organization to understand which internal area consumed the resource, even when the spending is not tied to one single project.

Department codes are especially useful for overhead analysis because they separate support functions from direct program work. They also help managers review staffing, office, and administrative spending by area rather than treating everything as one generic pool. When used consistently, department codes make budgeting easier, because each department can carry its own plan and its own performance review.
Send to the team:
- “Please use department codes for internal responsibility tracking.”
Key point:
- Department codes organize costs by function.
Official links:
- IRS Managerial Costing
- GAO Green Book
56. Q: How do department codes support overhead tracking?
A: Department codes help management separate direct program spending from overhead or support spending, which is one of the most important distinctions in nonprofit finance. If all expenses are coded only by account type, it may be impossible to see how much the organization spends on administration, fundraising, or shared services. Department codes solve that problem by showing where the cost belongs operationally.

This matters because overhead is often scrutinized by donors, boards, and senior management. With good department coding, the organization can explain support costs transparently and defend them with evidence rather than impressions. It also makes it possible to compare departments against budget and identify where staffing, office costs, or operational expenses are becoming too heavy.
Send to the team:
- “Please tag overhead costs with the correct department code.”
Key point:
- Department codes make overhead visible.
Official links:
- FASB ASU 2016-14
