57. Q: What is a chart of accounts in relation to cost allocation?
A: The chart of accounts is the core coding structure that classifies transactions into accounts such as salaries, rent, supplies, travel, depreciation, and other expense or income types. In relation to cost allocation, it must be detailed enough to allow finance to identify which costs are direct, which are shared, and which need later distribution. If it is too broad, allocation becomes messy and subjective; if it is too detailed, staff may struggle to use it consistently.

A strong chart of accounts supports clean analysis because every transaction has a logical place in the books from the start. It also improves donor reporting, because allocated and direct costs can be separated more reliably. In effect, the chart of accounts is where the accounting structure and the operational structure meet, so it should be designed with reporting and allocation in mind, not only with posting convenience.
Send to the team:
- “Please keep the chart of accounts detailed enough for allocation.”
Key point:
- The chart of accounts drives clean cost classification.
Official links:
- IRS Publication 557
- FASB ASU 2016-14
58. Q: How should the chart of accounts support allocation tracking?
A: It should separate cost type, cost center, project, and support category so that shared costs can be identified immediately and allocated in a consistent way. That means the coding structure should not force finance staff to guess later whether a cost is direct, shared, restricted, or general overhead. The more the code tells the story at the point of entry, the less rework is needed at month end.

Good design also means the chart of accounts should align with the organization’s reporting needs. If leadership wants to see payroll by project, rent by location, and office support by department, the coding structure should make those views possible without excessive manual extraction. When the chart of accounts is well designed, allocation becomes a controlled process rather than an emergency cleanup exercise.
Send to the team:
- “Please design the coding structure to support allocation at source.”
Key point:
- Good coding reduces allocation errors.
Official links:
- IRS Managerial Costing
- GAO Green Book
59. Q: What is the difference between allocation at source and allocation at month end?
A: Allocation at source means the cost is split correctly when the transaction is first entered into the accounting system. Allocation at month end means the cost is accumulated first and then redistributed later through a journal entry after the period has closed or nearly closed. Both methods can work, but they solve different operational problems.

Source allocation is stronger when the split is known in advance and the organization wants cleaner reporting throughout the month. Month-end allocation is useful when the true cost pattern is not known until all activity has been reviewed, such as shared payroll, utilities, or support costs. The key is not which method is used, but whether the method is documented, consistent, and supported by a reasonable basis.
Send to the team:
- “Please choose one allocation timing and apply it consistently.”
Key point:
- Source allocation happens immediately; month-end allocation happens later.
Official links:
- IRS Managerial Costing
- HHS Cost Allocation Services
60. Q: What are the advantages of allocating costs at source?
A: Allocating costs at source improves accuracy because the cost is coded correctly before it is buried in a general pool. It also reduces the volume of month-end adjustment journals, which saves time and lowers the risk of posting errors. Perhaps most importantly, it allows reports to be available sooner because less reclassification is needed later.

This approach also improves discipline among staff because the correct coding becomes part of the transaction flow instead of a later cleanup step. If an NGO is handling grants, donor restrictions, or multiple programs, source allocation can dramatically improve transparency. The process becomes simpler to review, easier to explain, and more reliable for audit purposes.
Send to the team:
- “Please allocate shared costs at the time of entry where possible.”
Key point:
- Source allocation is faster and cleaner.
Official links:
- IRS Managerial Costing
- GAO Green Book
61. Q: What are the advantages of allocating costs at month end?
A: Month-end allocation is useful when the final split cannot be known at the time the cost is first entered. This is common for shared staff time, pooled office costs, or support services where the month’s final usage pattern only becomes clear after all activity is recorded. It gives management a chance to review the full picture before making the final allocation.

It can also be a practical solution when the organization has limited systems and needs a single controlled month-end routine. In that case, the allocation worksheet becomes part of the close process and should be reviewed like any other major journal entry. The strength of month-end allocation is that it allows a more informed split, but only if the process is documented and repeated consistently.
Send to the team:
- “Please document the month-end allocation method and support.”
Key point:
- Month-end allocation allows fuller review.
Official links:
- IRS Managerial Costing
- HHS Cost Allocation Services
62. Q: What is a shared staff cost allocation?
A: Shared staff cost allocation is the process of splitting one employee’s salary and related employment costs across multiple projects, funds, departments, or functions based on a reasonable and supported basis. It is used when the employee’s time or output benefits more than one activity. This is common in NGOs where technical staff, managers, finance staff, and field staff often support several workstreams at once.

The allocation should reflect actual work performed, not just convenience or budget pressure. If the employee’s effort changes during the month, the split should change too, or the organization should use a documented estimate that is later reviewed against reality. When done well, shared staff allocation gives a more honest view of what each project truly costs and prevents one donor from subsidizing another without anyone noticing.
Send to the team:
- “Please split shared staff costs using a documented basis.”
Key point:
- Shared staff costs must be split fairly.
Official links:
- IRS Managerial Costing
- GAO Green Book
63. Q: How should payroll costs be allocated across projects?
A: Payroll costs should be allocated on the basis of time records, approved work plans, activity logs, or another reasonable method that reflects the employee’s actual contribution to each project. The best method is the one that is practical, documented, and subject to review. For project-based organizations, payroll is often the largest shared cost, so getting this right matters a great deal.

It is important that payroll allocation is not treated as a guess at month end. Even where formal timesheets are not used, there should be some evidence supporting the split, such as supervisor confirmation, activity schedules, or agreed percentage allocations that are periodically tested. If the underlying work changes significantly, the allocation should be revised so the books continue to reflect reality rather than assumptions that have become stale.
Send to the team:
- “Please keep payroll allocations backed by time or activity evidence.”
Key point:
- Payroll allocation needs support and review.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- HHS Cost Allocation Services
64. Q: How should benefits and allowances be allocated?
A: Benefits and allowances should normally follow the same allocation basis as the related salary unless there is a documented reason to use a different split. That keeps total employee cost aligned with the same projects or departments that received the person’s working time. In practice, this means the organization should think of salary, benefits, allowances, and related employment taxes as one connected cost package.

When benefits are allocated consistently with salary, the numbers become easier to understand and explain. It prevents a situation where the salary is charged correctly but the related costs are left in a general pool, which distorts project cost. A well-run nonprofit treats related employee costs as part of the same story rather than as separate fragments scattered across the ledger.
Send to the team:
- “Please allocate related employee costs on the same basis as salary.”
Key point:
- Related staff costs should move together.
Official links:
- IRS Managerial Costing
- HHS Cost Allocation Services
65. Q: How should employer taxes be allocated?
A: Employer taxes should usually follow the same split as the underlying salary and wages because they arise directly from the employment relationship. If one employee is allocated 60 percent to one project and 40 percent to another, the related employer tax cost should normally follow the same pattern unless the organization has a documented policy saying otherwise. This keeps the total employment cost consistent and avoids hidden distortions in project costing.

Allocating employer taxes this way makes the true cost of labor more visible. It also prevents the misleading situation where salary is allocated properly but tax costs are left behind in administration or another broad overhead pool. For donor reporting and management analysis, that kind of mismatch creates confusion and can understate the full cost of the funded activity.
Send to the team:
- “Please allocate employer taxes using the related payroll split.”
Key point:
- Employer taxes should follow payroll allocation.
Official links:
- IRS Managerial Costing
- GAO Green Book
66. Q: How should leave costs be allocated?
A: Leave costs should generally be allocated on the same basis as the employee’s normal payroll split unless the organization has a stronger approved method. Leave is part of the employment cost of the same person, so it usually belongs wherever that person’s regular work is charged. This keeps payroll cost patterns stable and prevents leave from being treated as an orphan expense that sits somewhere unrelated.

In practice, the policy should explain how annual leave, sick leave, and similar absences are treated so staff do not improvise different approaches each month. If leave is not allocated consistently, project totals can become distorted and the actual employment cost can be understated in key grants. A clear rule keeps both finance and program managers from debating the treatment every closing cycle.
Send to the team:
- “Please apply the approved leave-cost allocation method consistently.”
Key point:
- Leave costs should match the employee’s usual cost pattern.
Official links:
- IRS Managerial Costing
- GAO Green Book
67. Q: How should consultant costs be allocated when they serve multiple projects?
A: Consultant costs should be allocated using the contract terms, timesheets, deliverables, meeting logs, or another documented basis that shows how the work benefited each project. The split should ideally be agreed before the work starts or during the assignment, not invented after the invoice has arrived. That discipline protects the organization from charging the wrong project just because the final bill needs somewhere to go.

Consultants often support more than one activity, especially in NGOs that use technical specialists or short-term experts. In those cases, the organization should keep enough evidence to show why each part of the fee was assigned the way it was. Good consultant allocation is therefore about both commercial fairness and accounting defense: the books should reflect reality, and the file should explain why the split was reasonable.
Send to the team:
- “Please document how each consultant cost is split across projects.”
Key point:
- Consultant costs need clear service evidence.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- IRS Publication 557
68. Q: How should travel costs be allocated when trips support more than one activity?
A: Travel costs should be split using a reasonable and documented basis such as the agenda, the number of days spent on each activity, the purpose of the meeting, or the deliverable supported by the trip. Mixed-purpose travel is common in nonprofit work, especially when one visit includes field monitoring, partner supervision, and coordination meetings. The organization should not simply charge the whole trip to whichever grant has available budget.

The support for the allocation should be kept with the travel claim, ticket, boarding pass, or trip report. That way, anyone reviewing the transaction can understand why the split was chosen and whether it makes sense. Over time, this habit reduces argument, improves donor confidence, and makes travel reporting much easier to defend.
Send to the team:
- “Please record the allocation basis for every mixed-purpose trip.”
Key point:
- Travel should be split using a documented logic.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- IRS Managerial Costing
69. Q: How should training costs be allocated when multiple programs benefit?
A: Training costs should be allocated based on who attended, which teams benefited, or which programs directly used the training output. If one training event supported several projects or offices, the split should follow the benefit pattern rather than a convenient guess. The goal is to match the cost to the people and activities that genuinely gained value from it.

This matters because training often looks like a shared overhead cost, yet it may actually support implementation quality in several donor-funded activities. A well-documented allocation helps management see whether learning and capacity-building are being invested in proportion to need. It also helps avoid the false impression that a training event belongs entirely to one project when its effect was broader.
Send to the team:
- “Please allocate training costs based on actual program benefit.”
Key point:
- Training costs should follow the beneficiaries.
Official links:
- IRS Managerial Costing
- HHS Cost Allocation Services
70. Q: How should depreciation be allocated?
A: Depreciation should be allocated to the projects, departments, or functions that actually use the asset, based on a reasonable method such as square footage, usage hours, location, or service capacity. The asset’s benefit should drive the allocation, because depreciation represents the cost of consuming that asset over time. If the building, vehicle, or equipment is used by several parts of the NGO, the cost should be shared in line with that use.

Good depreciation allocation makes full-cost reporting much more realistic. It prevents a situation where program costs ignore the underlying assets used to deliver them, which can make programs appear cheaper than they really are. It also improves sustainability analysis because leadership can see the long-term cost of maintaining the operational base that supports the mission.
Send to the team:
- “Please allocate depreciation using the asset’s actual benefit pattern.”
Key point:
- Depreciation should follow asset use.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- FASB ASU 2016-14
71. Q: What is a reasonable basis for allocating asset depreciation?
A: A reasonable basis is one that reflects how the asset actually benefits the organization over time. For example, office space may be allocated by square footage, a vehicle by usage miles, IT equipment by user count, and a warehouse by operational function. The important thing is that the basis matches the nature of the asset and can be explained to management or an auditor without forcing a complicated story.

Once a basis is chosen, it should be documented and applied consistently unless the asset’s usage pattern changes significantly. That consistency helps avoid disputes and gives the organization stable reporting. If the basis becomes outdated, however, the allocation should be revised so the books continue to represent reality rather than a historical assumption that no longer fits.
Send to the team:
- “Please document the logic used for depreciation allocation.”
Key point:
- The basis must reflect actual benefit.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- IRS Publication 557
72. Q: What is a cost allocation memo?
A: A cost allocation memo is a written record that explains how a shared cost was split, why the chosen basis was appropriate, who approved the method, and when it should be reviewed again. It serves as the reference document for both staff and auditors, especially when the allocation is significant or recurring. In a well-managed nonprofit, the memo turns an abstract rule into a practical and traceable control.

The memo is helpful because memory fades, staff change, and recurring allocations can become automatic without anyone remembering the original logic. By writing down the basis, the organization protects itself from inconsistency later. It also gives the finance team a stable explanation to use when questions arise from budget holders, donors, or audit reviewers.
Send to the team:
- “Please keep a signed memo for every major allocation method.”
Key point:
- A memo explains and supports the split.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- HHS Cost Allocation Services
73. Q: Why should allocations be documented in writing?
A: Written documentation preserves the logic behind the allocation so that the method can be repeated, reviewed, and defended later. Without writing, the split can depend on memory, personal habit, or staff continuity, all of which are fragile in a busy NGO environment. A written record also makes it much easier for auditors and management to understand whether the basis was fair and whether it was applied consistently.

Documentation is especially important for shared costs that are material or sensitive, such as payroll, rent, utilities, or technical support. When the support sits in the file, the organization can respond to questions quickly instead of reconstructing the story months later. In practice, writing down the rule is one of the simplest and strongest control measures available.
Send to the team:
- “Please write down the allocation basis before posting it.”
Key point:
- Written support makes allocations defensible.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- IRS Publication 557
74. Q: What is the difference between allocation and apportionment?
A: Allocation usually refers to assigning shared costs using a chosen basis, such as time, headcount, space, or usage. Apportionment is a broader word that can describe dividing costs or resources among users or activities. In practice, the two terms are often used in similar ways, but the organization should still define the word it uses so staff apply the same logic consistently.

The important point is not the label but the discipline behind it. Whether the organization calls the process allocation or apportionment, the method should be documented, reasonable, and repeatable. If the terminology is unclear, the finance team should standardize it in policy so the same transaction is not handled differently by different people.
Send to the team:
- “Please define the term used in our policy and apply it consistently.”
Key point:
- The label matters less than the documented method.
Official links:
- IRS Managerial Costing
- GAO Green Book
75. Q: How should an NGO avoid arbitrary allocations?
A: It should use a written policy, a consistent cost driver, supporting evidence, and periodic review so the split is based on reality rather than convenience. Arbitrary allocation usually happens when staff are under pressure to finish quickly and choose percentages by instinct instead of logic. That approach may be fast, but it makes the numbers weak, hard to defend, and easy to challenge.

To avoid this, the organization should define the driver in advance and keep the supporting calculation with the accounting file. It should also review whether the driver still reflects how resources are being used, because a once-reasonable split can become outdated over time. The goal is not perfection; it is reasonableness, consistency, and a clear audit trail.
Send to the team:
- “Please do not guess the split; use a documented basis.”
Key point:
- Arbitrary allocation weakens trust and accuracy.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- HHS Cost Allocation Services
76. Q: What is a recurring allocation journal entry?
A: A recurring allocation journal entry is a regular journal used to post the same or similar shared-cost split each period when the pattern is stable. It is commonly used for expenses such as rent, utilities, shared staff costs, or fixed support charges that follow a predictable allocation basis. When the process is stable, it can save time and reduce manual error during the close cycle.

The recurring entry should still be reviewed before posting because patterns can change even when the journal looks familiar. If the organization expands, changes staffing, or moves offices, the allocation assumptions may need to be updated. So the recurring entry is a convenience tool, not a substitute for control or judgment.
Send to the team:
- “Please review recurring allocation journals before posting each period.”
Key point:
- Recurring entries automate stable splits.
Official links:
- IRS Managerial Costing
- GAO Green Book
77. Q: Why should recurring allocations be reviewed periodically?
A: Recurring allocations should be reviewed because staffing, usage, budgets, funding, and operational patterns change over time. A split that made sense six months ago may no longer be fair if a team has grown, a program has moved, or a support service is now being used differently. If the organization keeps applying the same percentage blindly, the numbers may become inaccurate even though the journal still looks familiar.

Periodic review helps keep the allocation basis aligned with current reality. It also gives management a chance to confirm whether the split continues to support donor reporting and program costing. A recurring entry becomes truly useful only when the underlying logic is still valid and the file shows that it was checked rather than assumed.
Send to the team:
- “Please recheck recurring allocations at least quarterly or at year end.”
Key point:
- Stable methods still need periodic review.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- HHS Cost Allocation Services
78. Q: What controls are needed over cost allocation?
A: Strong cost allocation controls should include an approved policy, clear drivers, documented calculations, management review, and evidence retention. Where possible, the people preparing the allocation should not be the only ones approving it. Large, unusual, or sensitive allocations deserve extra attention because they can have a major effect on program results and donor reports.

A variance check after posting is also helpful because it confirms whether the allocation produced a reasonable result. If the split changed dramatically, finance should understand why before closing the period. In practice, allocation control is about making sure the method is not only mathematically correct but also operationally sensible and properly signed off.
Send to the team:
- “Please review and sign off on all major allocations.”
Key point:
- Allocation needs policy, review, and evidence.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- IRS Publication 557
79. Q: What is the risk of manipulating allocations to improve reported results?
A: Manipulating allocations can make one program appear healthier and another appear weaker than reality, which distorts both management decisions and external reporting. It can also make donor reports misleading, because costs are not being assigned based on actual benefit or use. In the short term, manipulation may make a budget or program line look better, but in the long run it damages trust and can create serious audit concerns.

When allocation is used to shape an outcome rather than reflect reality, the numbers stop being a management tool and become a presentation tool. That can influence resource decisions, funding conversations, and even strategic planning in the wrong direction. A disciplined NGO treats allocation as a fair distribution process, not as a way to force results to look more attractive.
Send to the team:
- “Please keep allocations objective and reviewable.”
Key point:
- Manipulated allocation distorts performance.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- FASB ASU 2016-14
80. Q: How can cost allocation create audit risk?
A: Cost allocation creates audit risk when the basis is unclear, unsupported, inconsistent, or changed without approval. In those situations, auditors may question whether expenses were assigned fairly and whether the financial statements or donor reports truly reflect the organization’s activity. The issue is not only the math; it is whether the process can be defended with evidence.

Audit risk rises further when allocations are material, repeated, or linked to restricted funding. If the support file is weak, the auditor may request alternative evidence or challenge the charge entirely. Good documentation and a stable policy reduce that risk because they show that the organization is not improvising each month.
Send to the team:
- “Please keep allocation support ready for audit review.”
Key point:
- Weak allocation support invites audit findings.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- IRS Publication 557
81. Q: What evidence do auditors usually expect for allocations?
A: Auditors usually expect to see the policy, the calculation, the source data, the approval, and evidence that the method was applied consistently. Depending on the type of allocation, they may also want timesheets, headcount reports, floor space data, service records, or other operational drivers. The overall question the auditor is asking is simple: does the allocation reflect a reasonable and supported method?

The file should therefore tell the complete story from beginning to end. If the calculation is strong but the source data is missing, the allocation can still be challenged. Likewise, if the rule exists but nobody approved it, the process may be seen as weak even if the numbers look sensible.
Send to the team:
- “Please keep the allocation worksheet and support with the file.”
Key point:
- Auditors need the rule and the support behind it.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- IRS Publication 557
82. Q: How should allocation assumptions be tested?
A: Allocation assumptions should be tested by comparing them against actual usage, staffing levels, time records, floor space, transaction counts, or other operational evidence. The idea is to check whether the chosen driver still reflects how the organization is actually working. If the assumed split is far from reality, it should be revised rather than repeated automatically.

Testing assumptions is especially important when an allocation has been in place for a long time and no one has questioned it. A simple review can reveal that the original logic no longer fits the current structure. This makes the organization more accurate and reduces the chance of carrying forward hidden distortions for years.
Send to the team:
- “Please test the allocation basis against actual usage.”
Key point:
- Assumptions should be checked against real activity.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- HHS Cost Allocation Services
83. Q: What is a variance in allocated cost?
A: A variance in allocated cost is the difference between the amount allocated and the amount that should have been allocated based on actual activity or the approved formula. It may show that the driver was misapplied, that the assumption was outdated, or that the underlying usage changed. Variance is therefore a useful signal, not just an accounting nuisance.

When the variance is small, it may simply confirm that the method is working reasonably well. When it is large, it may indicate that the organization needs to update the basis or improve the source data. In either case, the variance should be explained so the numbers remain understandable and defensible.
Send to the team:
- “Please explain any material allocation variance.”
Key point:
- Variance shows where the split may be off.
Official links:
- IRS Managerial Costing
- GAO Green Book
84. Q: Why should allocated amounts be checked against actual usage?
A: They should be checked against actual usage because assumptions can drift away from reality as the organization changes. A department may grow, a project may end, or a service may be used more heavily than before, and the original split may no longer be fair. By comparing allocated amounts with actual use, finance can see whether the method is still reasonable.

This check protects both accuracy and fairness. It also gives management confidence that the reported costs really reflect operational patterns rather than old estimates that have been left untouched. In a nonprofit, this is especially important because resource use is closely tied to donor trust and program accountability.
Send to the team:
- “Please compare allocations with actual usage every review cycle.”
Key point:
- Actual usage is the best reality check.
Official links:
- HHS Cost Allocation Services
- GAO Green Book
85. Q: What is a cross-charge?
A: A cross-charge is a charge from one department, project, or entity to another for a service, item, or resource used. It is commonly used when one part of the organization provides a service that benefits another part, such as IT support, vehicle use, printing, or staff services. The cross-charge should reflect a real transfer of benefit, not just a convenient book entry.

It is important to support the charge with a service record or calculation so the receiving unit understands what it is paying for. This helps preserve fairness and avoids hidden subsidies between programs or offices. When used properly, a cross-charge makes internal relationships more transparent and prevents costs from lingering in the wrong place.
Send to the team:
- “Please support every cross-charge with a service record.”
Key point:
- A cross-charge records a charged service between users.
Official links:
- IRS Managerial Costing
- GAO Green Book
86. Q: How is a cross-charge different from a reallocation?
A: A cross-charge usually represents the transfer of a specific service or resource from one user to another. A reallocation, by contrast, usually redistributes costs that are already recorded in the books, using a new or corrected basis for sharing them across activities. The two concepts are related, but they solve different problems.

Cross-charges are often tied to a direct internal service or supply. Reallocations are more often used to correct or distribute pooled costs like shared payroll, rent, or support expenses. The organization should label the transaction correctly because the support and approval logic may differ depending on which method is being used.
Send to the team:
- “Please label the transaction correctly: cross-charge or reallocation.”
Key point:
- Cross-charge is a service charge; reallocation is a split correction.
Official links:
- IRS Managerial Costing
- GAO Green Book
87. Q: What is a service department cost?
A: A service department cost is the cost of a support function that serves other departments or programs, such as finance, HR, IT, procurement, or logistics. These costs are not usually direct program costs because they support the organization as a whole rather than delivering one specific activity. Still, they are real costs and should be tracked carefully because they are part of the organization’s full operating structure.

Service department costs are important because they often become the basis for allocations to programs, projects, or locations. If they are not identified clearly, shared services can disappear into general overhead and become difficult to analyze. By naming them correctly, the organization creates a cleaner foundation for allocation, budgeting, and cost recovery decisions.
Send to the team:
- “Please classify support-unit costs as service department costs.”
Key point:
- Service departments support other units.
Official links:
- IRS Managerial Costing
- HHS Cost Allocation Services
88. Q: How should service department costs be spread across beneficiaries?
A: They should be spread using a reasonable cost driver that reflects how the service is actually consumed, such as headcount, tickets handled, floor space, usage hours, or transaction volume. The chosen driver should match the nature of the service, because the best method for IT support may not be the best method for office rent or HR work. The point is to align the cost with the benefit received as closely as practical.

For example, if a finance team supports three programs equally, the split might use the number of transactions processed for each program rather than a simple equal percentage. That makes the allocation more meaningful and easier to explain. The driver should also be reviewed periodically so it does not become stale or disconnected from current service patterns.
Send to the team:
- “Please allocate service department costs using a documented driver.”
Key point:
- The driver should match the service delivered.
Official links:
- HHS Cost Allocation Services
- IRS Managerial Costing
89. Q: What is the purpose of an allocation driver matrix?
A: An allocation driver matrix lists each shared cost and the basis used to split it, creating one central reference for recurring allocations. It helps staff apply the same logic each period, reduces confusion, and makes the method easier to review during close or audit. In a nonprofit with several programs or offices, this can prevent a great deal of time being wasted on repeated explanations.

The matrix is also valuable when staff change, because the next person can see the logic immediately instead of trying to reconstruct it from old journals. It becomes a practical control document that links policy to daily accounting work. If maintained properly, it can also support training because new staff can learn the costing model more quickly.
Send to the team:
- “Please keep one driver matrix for all recurring shared costs.”
Key point:
- A driver matrix standardizes allocation choices.
Official links:
- HHS Cost Allocation Services
- GAO Green Book
90. Q: How should an NGO choose between different allocation bases?
A: It should choose the basis that best reflects who benefited from the cost, is practical to collect, and can be applied consistently over time. The strongest allocation base is usually the one that balances fairness, simplicity, and evidence. If the basis is too complex, staff may not use it correctly; if it is too simple, it may not reflect reality well enough.

For example, headcount may work well for some HR or administrative costs, while usage hours may be better for a vehicle or equipment cost. The organization should not choose a basis simply because it is easy or because it makes a donor report look better. A good allocation basis is one that a reasonable reviewer could understand and accept without needing a long explanation.
Send to the team:
- “Please choose the allocation base that best matches actual benefit.”
Key point:
- The best basis is the one that matches benefit and evidence.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- HHS Cost Allocation Services
91. Q: What happens if an allocation basis changes mid-year?
A: If an allocation basis changes mid-year, the organization should document the reason for the change, get the proper approval, and apply the new method consistently from the approved date forward. It should also explain the effect on reports so users understand why current period figures are not exactly comparable with earlier months. Sudden unexplained changes create confusion and can make trends look artificial.

The change may be necessary if the organization has grown, the service pattern has changed, or the previous driver no longer reflects reality. In that case, the best practice is to show the old method, the new method, and the reason for the transition clearly in the file. Transparency is more important than trying to hide the change and pretend nothing happened.
Send to the team:
- “Please document and approve any mid-year allocation change.”
Key point:
- Mid-year changes need approval and disclosure.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- FASB ASU 2016-14
92. Q: How should an NGO handle a new program with shared costs?
A: It should set up the project code, define the direct and shared costs, choose the allocation driver, and document the method before major spending begins. That planning step is important because new programs often start with incomplete structures, and finance later has to clean up the mess if costing rules were not set early. Good setup saves time and prevents arguments about where costs should go.

The organization should also think about reporting from the start: what the donor needs, what management needs, and what the closeout file will need later. If the program is shared across departments or offices, the support logic should be agreed in advance so staff know how to code the activity correctly. Early clarity is far easier than retroactive correction.
Send to the team:
- “Please define the costing method before the new program starts.”
Key point:
- New programs need costing rules from the start.
Official links:
- HHS Cost Allocation Services
- GAO Green Book
93. Q: How should an NGO handle donor limits on indirect cost recovery?
A: It should read the donor terms carefully, apply the allowed indirect rate or cap, and track any shortfall or excess separately so management understands the funding impact. Donor rules override internal preference, so the organization cannot simply decide to recover more overhead than the award allows. If the limit is restrictive, finance should make sure leadership sees the effect on sustainability and support-cost recovery.

This is not just a compliance issue; it is a planning issue. If indirect recovery is limited, the organization may need to adjust its reserves, reforecast its overhead gap, or seek unrestricted support elsewhere. The key is to know the donor rule early and reflect it honestly in both the accounting and the management view.
Send to the team:
- “Please check donor rules before applying indirect costs.”
Key point:
- Donor limits override internal preferences.
Official links:
- HHS Cost Allocation Services
- IRS Publication 557
- FASB ASU 2016-14
94. Q: What is the relationship between allocation and donor compliance?
A: Allocation is a major part of donor compliance because donor reports, budget-versus-actual statements, and indirect cost claims all depend on expenses being assigned correctly. If shared costs are not allocated properly, a donor may see an inaccurate picture of how its funds were used. That can create questioned costs, reporting corrections, or even relationship damage if the error is material.

Good allocation supports compliance by ensuring that costs land in the right place based on the donor agreement and the organization’s written method. This is why allocation should never be treated as a purely internal accounting issue. It is part of the compliance framework that links finance, operations, and donor accountability together.
Send to the team:
- “Please make sure allocation rules match donor conditions.”
Key point:
- Allocation is part of donor compliance.
Official links:
- HHS Cost Allocation Services
- IRS Publication 557
95. Q: How should allocation support accurate financial reporting?
A: Allocation should place each cost in the right place so that the reports show the true cost of programs, support functions, and donor-funded activities. When shared costs are allocated well, management can trust the numbers for budgeting, forecasting, and decision-making. When they are allocated poorly, the report may still balance mathematically but fail to tell the truth operationally.

This matters because many nonprofit decisions depend on understanding full cost rather than only direct cost. Accurate allocation also improves trend analysis, because expenses are compared on a consistent basis from period to period. In this way, allocation becomes a foundation for reliable financial reporting rather than just a month-end adjustment routine.
Send to the team:
- “Please review allocations before the month-end close.”
Key point:
- Accurate allocation improves report quality.
Official links:
- FASB ASU 2016-14
- GAO Green Book
96. Q: How should allocation support realistic program costing?
A: It should assign a fair share of shared costs to each program so managers can see the true total cost of delivery, not just the visible direct expense. Realistic program costing includes direct costs, shared support, and sometimes asset or service costs that are easy to overlook if no allocation system exists. That fuller picture helps managers understand what it really takes to run the activity.

This is important for sustainability, because a program that looks cheap on a direct-cost basis may be much more expensive once support is added. It is also useful for donor discussions, because some donors want to know the full cost of impact rather than a narrow slice of direct spending. A realistic costing model gives leadership a better basis for planning, pricing, and fundraising.
Send to the team:
- “Please include allocated support costs in program costing.”
Key point:
- Program costing should show full cost, not only direct cost.
Official links:
- HHS Cost Allocation Services
- IRS Publication 557
97. Q: What are common mistakes in NGO cost allocation?
A: Common mistakes include using arbitrary splits, failing to update the driver, ignoring shared staff time, leaving out support costs, and not documenting the basis. Another frequent error is treating all overhead as if it were a generic pool that does not need analysis, when in reality different support costs often need different drivers. These mistakes may seem small at first, but they distort project costing and can build up over time.

One of the biggest risks is that people become used to the same number and stop asking whether it still makes sense. When that happens, the allocation method may continue for years even though operations have changed. A good nonprofit reviews its costing logic regularly so it does not accidentally create a false picture of program efficiency.
Send to the team:
- “Please avoid guesswork and keep allocation support current.”
Key point:
- Most allocation errors come from weak support and stale methods.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- HHS Cost Allocation Services
98. Q: What are common mistakes in program costing?
A: Common mistakes include leaving out support costs, double counting shared expenses, using the wrong project code, and relying on estimates that are never checked. Another problem is charging only the visible direct costs and forgetting that the program also consumes finance, HR, office space, logistics, and management support. That creates a program cost figure that looks cleaner than reality but is far less useful for decisions.

Program costing also goes wrong when the costing model is not aligned with the actual structure of operations. If a program uses shared vehicles, shared staff, or shared facilities, those costs must be accounted for somewhere. Otherwise, management may believe a program is cheaper than it really is and make funding or expansion decisions based on incomplete information.
Send to the team:
- “Please review program costing for missing and duplicated costs.”
Key point:
- Bad costing usually comes from missing or duplicated shared costs.
Official links:
- FASB ASU 2016-14
- IRS Form 990 Instructions
99. Q: How can strong allocation practices improve transparency?
A: Strong allocation practices make it easier to explain where money went, how shared costs were handled, and why each program shows the cost level it does. They show leadership, donors, auditors, and staff that the organization is not hiding support costs or shifting expenses without logic. Transparency is not just about publishing numbers; it is about making those numbers understandable and defensible.

When the allocation method is simple enough to explain and consistent enough to repeat, the whole finance system becomes more trustworthy. People may still ask questions, but the answers can be supported rather than improvised. That is one of the clearest signs that the organization is moving from basic bookkeeping toward mature financial management.
Send to the team:
- “Please keep allocation methods simple enough to explain clearly.”
Key point:
- Transparent allocation builds trust in the numbers.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- FASB ASU 2016-14
100. Q: What does a good NGO cost allocation and program costing system look like in practice?
A: A good system has clear codes, a written policy, approved drivers, source documentation, regular review, and reports that show both direct and shared costs by program. It should be simple enough for staff to use in day-to-day work and strong enough to stand up to donor scrutiny, board questions, and audit review. In practice, that means the system is not just technically correct; it is actually usable.

The best systems also make the organization smarter over time. They help leadership understand the full cost of programs, compare activities fairly, and make better sustainability decisions. When allocation and program costing are done well, finance stops being only a reporting function and becomes a management tool that improves planning, accountability, and mission delivery.
Send to the team:
- “Please keep the system simple, documented, and reviewed every period.”
Key point:
- A good system is clear, consistent, and defensible.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- HHS Cost Allocation Services
- FASB ASU 2016-14

Table 6
Q&A NGO Finance and Accounting
1. Q: What is cash management in NGO finance?
A: Cash management is the discipline of making sure the NGO always knows how much usable cash it has, where that cash is held, when it will arrive, and when it will be needed. In practice, it covers daily monitoring, payment timing, bank oversight, and forward planning so the organization can continue delivering programs without interruption. A strong cash management process helps the NGO avoid missed payroll, delayed supplier payments, and avoidable borrowing or emergency transfers. It is not only about counting money; it is about protecting liquidity, matching spending to available resources, and making sure restricted and unrestricted money are not mixed in a way that creates compliance problems. Good cash management also gives leadership a realistic view of whether current operations are sustainable in the short term.
Send to the team:
- “Please track all expected cash inflows and outflows in one cash plan and update it regularly.”
Key point:
- Cash management protects liquidity, payment timing, and operational continuity.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
- IRS Publication 557
2. Q: Why is cash management important for a nonprofit?
A: Cash management is important for a nonprofit because the timing of donor receipts, grant disbursements, and program spending rarely matches perfectly. An NGO may have a strong budget on paper but still face cash stress if payments arrive late or large costs fall due before funding is received. Without good cash management, even a healthy organization can struggle to meet obligations, which can disrupt services, damage staff morale, and create compliance issues with vendors, employees, or donors. In an NGO, the consequences are not just financial. Cash shortages can interrupt field delivery, delay reporting, weaken trust, and force management into reactive decisions that reduce control. Careful cash management helps the organization stay mission-focused while still protecting financial discipline.
Send to the team:
- “Please review cash availability before committing new spending or releasing payments.”
Key point:
- Cash control supports mission delivery and compliance.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
- IRS Publication 557
3. Q: What is the difference between cash and bank balance?
A: Cash is the money the NGO controls in physical form or immediately available payment form, while bank balance is the amount recorded by the bank for a particular account at a specific time. Those two numbers often differ because of timing items such as unpresented checks, deposits in transit, pending bank charges, or uncleared transfers. A bank balance can look healthy while the organization’s books show a lower amount, or the books can show cash that has not yet cleared the bank. That is why the two figures should never be treated as identical without reconciliation. In NGO finance, understanding the difference matters because decisions are made from the books, but actual spendability depends on what is really available in the bank and what is already committed or restricted.
Send to the team:
- “Please reconcile book cash to bank cash regularly and explain every difference.”
Key point:
- Bank balance and book cash are not always the same.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
4. Q: What is liquidity in an NGO?
A: Liquidity is the NGO’s ability to meet short-term obligations when they fall due without creating operational distress. It is a measure of whether the organization has enough usable cash, near-cash resources, and payment capacity to keep moving through payroll cycles, vendor deadlines, and field commitments. A nonprofit may appear stable from a funding perspective and still be illiquid if money is locked in restricted grants, delayed reimbursements, or slow donor receipts. Liquidity therefore focuses on timing, not just total resources. It answers the practical question: can the NGO pay what it owes now, this week, and next month? Leaders need liquidity visibility because even a temporary gap can interrupt service delivery, create staff pressure, and weaken donor confidence.
Send to the team:
- “Please monitor liquidity every reporting cycle and flag any shortfall early.”
Key point:
- Liquidity is the ability to pay on time.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
5. Q: Why do NGOs need a liquidity plan?
A: A liquidity plan helps the NGO anticipate when money will be tight, when payments should be delayed, and when additional support may be needed. It turns cash management from a reactive exercise into a forward-looking discipline. This is especially important where grants reimburse expenses after the fact, where donor tranches arrive in stages, or where major program costs must be paid before related funding is received. A liquidity plan also supports decision-making about hiring, procurement, and field expansion because management can see whether current cash levels can sustain planned activity. In a nonprofit setting, this prevents the organization from confusing budget approval with actual cash capacity. The plan becomes a practical tool for matching ambition to available financial room.
Send to the team:
- “Please maintain a forward-looking liquidity plan for all major programs and funding streams.”
Key point:
- A liquidity plan reduces payment risk.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
6. Q: What is a cash forecast?
A: A cash forecast is a forward estimate of when cash will come in and when it will go out over a chosen period, such as a week, month, or quarter. It looks ahead rather than backward, combining expected donor receipts, grant disbursements, payroll dates, supplier schedules, tax deadlines, advances, and any known large commitments. A reliable forecast helps the NGO prepare for timing gaps before they become emergencies. It also gives managers a basis for sequencing payments, prioritizing urgent needs, and protecting core operations. In a nonprofit environment, the forecast is not just a treasury document; it is a management tool that connects funding reality with program delivery. It should be updated with actual receipts and actual payments, not left as a static spreadsheet.
Send to the team:
- “Please update the cash forecast using actual receipts, actual payments, and new commitments.”
Key point:
- Cash forecasts support forward planning.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
7. Q: How is a cash forecast different from a budget?
A: A budget shows what the NGO plans to earn and spend from a management perspective, while a cash forecast shows when money will actually move through the bank or cash box. Those are related but not the same. A budget can approve an activity months in advance, but the related cash may be received later or paid earlier depending on contracts, donor terms, or vendor schedules. That means a budget can look balanced even when a cash shortfall is approaching. The forecast therefore answers a timing question, not a policy question. In good NGO finance practice, budget and cash forecast should be linked but maintained separately so management can see both affordability and liquidity. This distinction is crucial when programs operate across different funding streams or currencies.
Send to the team:
- “Please separate budget planning from cash timing in all finance reviews.”
Key point:
- Budget is the plan; cash forecast is the timing view.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
8. Q: Why should an NGO forecast cash separately from expenses?
A: An NGO should forecast cash separately from expenses because accounting recognition and cash movement happen on different timelines. An expense may be incurred before payment, paid in advance, or settled long after the service or goods were received. If finance relies only on the expense budget, it can miss the actual pressure points that affect bank balances. Cash forecasting therefore focuses on when disbursement will happen, not just when the cost belongs in the accounts. This is especially important for payroll, rent, bulk procurement, and grant-funded projects where cash timing can be tight even if the accounting result looks fine. Keeping the two views separate gives management a clearer understanding of both financial performance and payment capacity.
Send to the team:
- “Please prepare a cash forecast that is separate from the budget and review both together.”
Key point:
- Cash timing is not the same as expense recognition.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
9. Q: What inputs are needed for a reliable cash forecast?
A: A reliable cash forecast needs complete and realistic timing inputs. At minimum, that includes opening cash balances, expected donor receipts, grant tranches, supplier payment schedules, payroll dates, taxes, planned advances, debt or settlement obligations, and any known large one-off transactions. The forecast becomes weak when one major category is missing or when assumptions are vague. For example, if grant receipts are delayed by two weeks or payroll grows after a hiring decision, the forecast must reflect that immediately. A practical forecast also benefits from program-side inputs because finance cannot predict every local or field commitment alone. The more complete the input set, the more useful the forecast becomes for decision-making.
Send to the team:
- “Please provide all expected receipts, commitments, and payment dates for the forecast period.”
Key point:
- Good forecasts need complete timing inputs.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
10. Q: How often should a cash forecast be updated?
A: A cash forecast should be updated as often as the organization’s cash risk requires. For many NGOs, monthly updates are the minimum useful standard, while organizations with tight liquidity, high transaction volume, or rapid field spending often need weekly refreshes. The right frequency depends on volatility, not convenience. If major receipts are delayed, if a donor tranche is postponed, or if spending accelerates, the forecast should be revised immediately rather than waiting for the next scheduled cycle. The purpose is to keep management ahead of the problem, not to document a problem after it has already caused disruption. A good forecast becomes more valuable over time when it is treated as a living management tool rather than a static report.
Send to the team:
- “Please refresh the cash forecast whenever material receipts or payments change.”
Key point:
- Update frequency should match cash risk.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
11. Q: What is the purpose of a daily cash review?
A: A daily cash review gives the NGO an immediate picture of what is available, what is pending, and what needs attention today. It is especially useful in active treasury environments where payments, receipts, and bank movements happen frequently. The review helps finance detect bounced payments, unrecorded receipts, unapproved releases, or sudden dips in balances before they spread into larger issues. In a nonprofit, daily review is a practical safeguard because even small errors can affect staff salaries, petty cash, procurement, or donor-funded field work. When the daily cash position is visible, managers can make quicker and safer decisions. This does not replace monthly review; it complements it by capturing operational urgency.
Send to the team:
- “Please review daily balances, pending payments, and immediate exceptions each working day.”
Key point:
- Daily review catches problems early.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
12. Q: Who should review cash balances regularly?
A: Cash balances should be reviewed by finance staff and, where appropriate, a supervisor, finance manager, or treasury lead who can challenge unusual movements. The reviewer should not be the same person who controls every step of receipt, recording, and payment, because review loses value when the process is not independent. Regular review provides a second set of eyes for errors, unauthorized activity, bank issues, or timing gaps. In a nonprofit, this matters because cash is one of the most sensitive assets and the easiest to misuse if oversight is weak. A clear review assignment also improves accountability because someone is visibly responsible for checking the numbers. The control works best when the reviewer asks questions, not just signs a form.
Send to the team:
- “Please assign a designated reviewer for regular cash balances and exceptions.”
Key point:
- Regular review strengthens control.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
13. Q: What is the role of the finance team in treasury management?
A: The finance team manages treasury by keeping cash visible, safe, and available for approved use. That includes monitoring bank balances, preparing cash forecasts, controlling payment releases, reconciling bank accounts, reviewing signatory authority, and escalating shortages or unusual activity. Treasury management in an NGO is not only a technical function; it is a control function that protects the organization from avoidable operational disruption. The finance team also helps leadership understand whether spending plans are aligned with funding timing and whether restricted resources are being handled correctly. In effect, the finance team translates raw cash movements into usable decision information. Without that discipline, the organization may know how much money it raised but not whether it can actually pay its next obligations.
Send to the team:
- “Please keep treasury records current and escalate any cash risk immediately.”
Key point:
- Finance manages both control and availability.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
14. Q: What is treasury in a nonprofit context?
A: Treasury in a nonprofit context is the management of cash, bank accounts, liquidity, payments, and short-term financial risk. It is the operational layer of finance that keeps money moving safely and responsibly. For an NGO, treasury includes practical tasks such as maintaining bank relationships, timing disbursements, monitoring restricted balances, and ensuring signatory controls are followed. It also includes anticipating shortages and deciding when to preserve cash rather than spend it too quickly. Treasury is therefore both protective and enabling: it safeguards the organization from misuse and helps programs operate smoothly. In a well-run NGO, treasury is not just a payment desk. It is a structured discipline that connects operational needs with financial control.
Send to the team:
- “Please manage treasury as a control function, not only as a payment function.”
Key point:
- Treasury protects cash availability and control.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
15. Q: How should an NGO manage multiple bank accounts?
A: An NGO should manage multiple bank accounts through a clear register, defined purpose for each account, regular reconciliation, and controlled signatory authority. Each account should have a reason for existing, an owner who understands its use, and a monitoring rhythm that matches its activity level. Too many accounts without structure create confusion, hidden balances, and weak oversight. A clear bank account register should show which funds or programs use each account, who can authorize movements, and when it was last reconciled. This approach helps leadership see whether balances are active, idle, restricted, or potentially unnecessary. It also reduces the risk that accounts are opened informally and then forgotten. Strong management of multiple accounts is a basic treasury safeguard in NGOs with projects, countries, or field locations spread across different needs.
Send to the team:
- “Please maintain a bank account register showing purpose, signatories, balances, and reconciliation status.”
Key point:
- Every account needs ownership and review.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
16. Q: Why should bank balances be monitored by account and by fund?
A: Bank balances should be monitored by account and by fund because one bank account can hold money with different uses, restrictions, or donor conditions. Monitoring only the total balance can hide the fact that one portion is reserved for a specific project while another portion is available for general operations. Fund-level monitoring also helps finance see whether project cash is being held in the correct place and whether restricted resources are being consumed appropriately. In a nonprofit, this is more than a reporting preference; it is a control requirement linked to donor accountability and stewardship. When the organization can trace balances by bank account and by funding source, it is much easier to explain cash availability and avoid misuse. This also supports more accurate forecasting and cleaner reconciliations.
Send to the team:
- “Please track each bank balance by account and by funding source.”
Key point:
- Fund tracking supports donor compliance.
Official links:
- IRS Publication 557
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
17. Q: What is restricted cash?
A: Restricted cash is cash that can only be used for a specific donor purpose, project, time period, or other imposed condition. It is not freely available for any general expense just because the money is sitting in the bank. The restriction may come from the donor, from a grant agreement, or from a board decision if internal designations are being tracked separately. In a nonprofit, the key issue is not only whether cash exists, but whether it is permitted to be used for the intended purpose. Restricted cash therefore requires separate tracking, clear coding, and careful reporting. If staff treat it like free cash, the organization can create compliance issues, reporting errors, and trust problems with funders.
Send to the team:
- “Please separate restricted cash from general operating cash in the ledger and reports.”
Key point:
- Restricted cash has use limits.
Official links:
- IRS Publication 557
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
18. Q: What is unrestricted cash?
A: Unrestricted cash is cash the NGO can use for general operations or other approved purposes without donor-imposed limitations. It is usually the most flexible part of the organization’s liquidity because management can direct it to payroll, rent, operating costs, or strategic needs as appropriate. That flexibility does not mean it should be spent casually, but it does mean the NGO has more room to manage timing problems or urgent needs. Unrestricted cash is often the buffer that allows a nonprofit to absorb delays in donor receipts or unexpected operational costs. In financial reporting, showing unrestricted cash clearly helps leadership understand how much real operational room remains. It also helps the board judge whether the organization is underfunded, stable, or overly dependent on restricted grants.
Send to the team:
- “Please identify unrestricted cash separately for operating use and liquidity review.”
Key point:
- Unrestricted cash is available for general use.
Official links:
- IRS Publication 557
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
19. Q: Why must restricted and unrestricted cash be kept distinct?
A: Restricted and unrestricted cash must be kept distinct because they serve different purposes and carry different accountability obligations. If they are blended together, management may overestimate available resources and accidentally spend money that is not available for general use. Clear separation also makes donor reporting, budgeting, and cash forecasting much more reliable. In an NGO, this distinction is essential because misclassification can affect both compliance and mission delivery. Separate records also help the board see whether the organization has enough flexible cash to cover overhead, temporary shortfalls, or strategic investments. The rule is simple: if the money is not free to use, it should not be presented as if it is. Clear separation protects both control and trust.
Send to the team:
- “Please keep separate records for restricted and unrestricted balances and review them each period.”
Key point:
- Separation protects compliance and clarity.
Official links:
- IRS Publication 557
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
20. Q: How should donor-restricted cash be tracked?
A: Donor-restricted cash should be tracked by donor, grant, project, purpose, and any relevant time condition. The ledger should make it possible to trace the cash from receipt to approved use to reporting, so there is no doubt about how the funds were handled. In a well-controlled NGO, restricted cash tracking is not only a bank balance exercise; it is a full accountability chain. That means the source of the funds, the coding of the spending, the supporting documents, and the remaining balance all need to align. If the money belongs to a specific project or donor, the records should show that clearly at every stage. This discipline reduces the chance of misapplication and makes donor reporting much easier to defend.
Send to the team:
- “Please code donor-restricted cash to the correct grant, project, and purpose.”
Key point:
- Track restricted cash at the source and in the ledger.
Official links:
- IRS Publication 557
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
21. Q: What is the difference between available cash and committed cash?
A: Available cash is the portion of money the NGO can actually use now without violating restrictions or approved commitments. Committed cash has already been mentally or formally set aside for known obligations such as payroll, rent, procurement, field advances, or a donor-specific use. The distinction matters because a bank balance can look comfortable while very little of it is truly free. If committed amounts are ignored, managers may overstate the organization’s flexibility and take on new spending that the cash position cannot support. In a nonprofit, this distinction is especially important because program teams may request spending based on funding availability rather than actual liquidity. Finance has to translate those requests into a true spendable balance view.
Send to the team:
- “Please distinguish free cash from cash already committed to obligations.”
Key point:
- Committed cash is not truly free cash.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
22. Q: What is the difference between cash on hand and cash in bank?
A: Cash on hand is physical cash held by the organization, usually in the office, field, or petty cash box. Cash in bank is money deposited in financial institutions and accessed through banking channels. The control risks are different: cash on hand is exposed to physical loss, theft, and documentation weakness, while cash in bank is more exposed to unauthorized transfers, delayed reconciliations, and stale records if oversight is weak. A good NGO finance system tracks both separately because each requires different controls and review procedures. Treating them as one undifferentiated balance can hide shortages or make the organization think it has more ready cash than it actually does. Clear separation improves both accountability and day-to-day treasury management.
Send to the team:
- “Please record and reconcile cash on hand and cash in bank separately.”
Key point:
- Physical cash and bank cash are different controls.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
23. Q: What is a bank reconciliation?
A: A bank reconciliation is the process of comparing the NGO’s own cash records with the bank statement and explaining every difference between them. It confirms whether the organization’s books are complete, whether the bank has processed transactions correctly, and whether any errors or unusual items need follow-up. A reconciliation is not just a matching exercise; it is a control check that protects against hidden errors and unauthorized activity. In NGO finance, it should be done with discipline because reconciled cash is one of the strongest foundations for reliable reporting. The reconciliation should show opening balance, movements, reconciling items, and closing balance with clear support for each adjustment. If the process is late, incomplete, or unsigned, the control loses much of its value.
Send to the team:
- “Please reconcile the ledger to the bank statement each period and review all reconciling items.”
Key point:
- Reconciliation verifies cash accuracy.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
24. Q: Why is bank reconciliation essential in NGO finance?
A: Bank reconciliation is essential because it uncovers the gap between recorded cash and actual bank activity. Without it, the organization can carry errors forward for months, miss duplicate payments, fail to notice bank charges, or overlook unauthorized transactions. It also helps the NGO identify timing differences such as deposits in transit and uncleared checks so management can understand the true cash position. In practical terms, bank reconciliation is one of the clearest ways to prove that cash records are complete and reliable. It supports donor trust, audit readiness, and internal decision-making. In a nonprofit, where cash often links directly to restricted funding and program delivery, reconciliation is a core control rather than a back-office formality.
Send to the team:
- “Please complete bank reconciliations on time and review exceptions before closing the period.”
Key point:
- Reconciliation is a core cash control.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
25. Q: How often should bank reconciliations be done?
A: Bank reconciliations should normally be completed monthly, aligned with the accounting close. In organizations with higher transaction volume, multiple bank accounts, field operations, or weak cash conditions, more frequent review may be needed to keep the records accurate and catch problems early. The best practice is not simply to do the reconciliation but to finish it on time, investigate differences, and clear reconciling items promptly. A monthly reconciliation that sits unreconciled for weeks is far less useful than a quicker cycle with proper review. In NGO finance, timely reconciliation supports both liquidity visibility and period-end reporting. It also creates the discipline needed to close the books cleanly and defend the balances in audit or donor review.
Send to the team:
- “Please close and review each bank reconciliation every month, or more often where risk is high.”
Key point:
- Monthly reconciliation is the baseline control.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
26. Q: Who should prepare bank reconciliations?
A: Bank reconciliations should be prepared by finance staff who understand the cash process but are not the same people fully controlling the disbursement and receipt flow. Separation of duties is important because the person reconciling should be able to detect errors, omissions, or unusual activity without having created every transaction themselves. In a small NGO, perfect separation may not always be possible, but the organization should still build review by another person wherever it can. The goal is independent thinking, not just mechanical matching. A good preparer should be organized, disciplined, and careful with support documents and timing items. The reconciliation becomes stronger when the preparer knows that another person will actually challenge the work.
Send to the team:
- “Please assign reconciliation preparation to staff independent from payment processing where possible.”
Key point:
- The preparer should not fully control the cash process.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
27. Q: Who should review bank reconciliations?
A: A supervisor, finance manager, or another independent reviewer should review bank reconciliations after they are prepared. The reviewer should look beyond arithmetic accuracy and assess whether reconciling items make sense, whether anything is stale, whether follow-up was completed, and whether unusual balances need escalation. This review is important because many errors are not in the matching itself but in the explanation of differences. In a nonprofit, review should be documented clearly so the control evidence is visible to audit and management. The reviewer’s role is to challenge assumptions, not simply to sign the page. A strong review often catches issues that the preparer, because of familiarity, may overlook.
Send to the team:
- “Please ensure every bank reconciliation has an independent review.”
Key point:
- Review adds a second layer of control.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
28. Q: What documents support a bank reconciliation?
A: A bank reconciliation is usually supported by the bank statement, cashbook or ledger, deposit records, payment vouchers, bank advices, and explanations for each reconciling item. If there are unusual entries, the supporting documents should show what happened, when it happened, and why the timing difference exists. The reconciliation should never rely on memory alone because reconciliation is supposed to create an auditable trail. In an NGO environment, proper support is especially important when funds are restricted or when there are multiple bank accounts and field locations. Good documentation also helps when different staff members review the same account over time. The easier it is to trace each item, the stronger the control and the cleaner the close.
Send to the team:
- “Please attach the bank statement and supporting details to each reconciliation file.”
Key point:
- Reconciliation needs documentary support.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
29. Q: What should be checked when reconciling bank statements?
A: The reviewer should check opening and closing balances, cleared receipts and payments, unmatched items, duplicate entries, unusual timing gaps, fees, returned payments, and any unauthorized movement. The purpose is to understand why the bank record and the book record differ and whether the difference is legitimate. A good reconciliation also tests whether older items are being followed up or simply carried forward month after month. In a nonprofit, stale reconciling items can hide errors for a long time if nobody pushes for resolution. The checking process should therefore be investigative, not passive. The more regular the review, the easier it becomes to see patterns that may signal control problems.
Send to the team:
- “Please check all reconciling items until they are fully explained or cleared.”
Key point:
- Reconciliation is a detail check, not a formality.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
30. Q: What causes differences between book cash and bank cash?
A: Differences can be caused by outstanding checks, deposits in transit, bank fees, timing gaps, returned payments, reversals, duplicate entries, or unauthorized transactions. Some of these are normal timing differences, while others point to recording problems or control weaknesses. The important thing is not merely to identify that the balances differ, but to explain the reason and determine whether action is needed. In an NGO, unexplained differences can distort donor reports, cash forecasts, and budget decisions. Regular reconciliations should therefore separate harmless timing items from items that need investigation or correction. When that discipline is followed, the difference between book cash and bank cash becomes a manageable control issue rather than a hidden risk.
Send to the team:
- “Please classify each difference and clear it promptly.”
Key point:
- Differences should be explained, not ignored.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
31. Q: How should outstanding checks be handled in reconciliation?
A: Outstanding checks should be listed as reconciling items until they clear the bank or are investigated and reversed if they remain stale. They are a normal timing difference, but only for a limited period. If a check remains outstanding too long, the organization should follow up with the payee, confirm whether it was lost or returned, and decide whether a replacement or reversal is needed. In an NGO, stale checks can mask old liabilities, duplicate payment risk, or missed receipt of the original instrument. The reconciliation file should show how long each item has been open and who is responsible for following it up. That level of detail makes the control useful and prevents old items from becoming permanent clutter in the books.
Send to the team:
- “Please review all stale outstanding checks each reconciliation cycle.”
Key point:
- Old checks need follow-up.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
32. Q: How should deposits in transit be handled in reconciliation?
A: Deposits in transit should be recorded as reconciling items when the NGO has already recorded the cash but the bank has not yet cleared it. They are common where deposits are made late in the day, across branch locations, or during bank processing delays. The key is to verify that the deposit really exists and is simply waiting to clear, rather than treating every unmatched amount as automatically valid.
