If a deposit sits in transit for too long, it should be investigated because the money may not actually be available or may have been misposted. Proper monitoring helps management avoid relying on balances that are not yet fully accessible. In practice, deposits in transit should disappear in the next reconciliation unless there is a genuine operational delay.
Send to the team:
- “Please verify deposits in transit against bank clearing status and follow up old items.”
Key point:
- Deposits in transit are timing items.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
33. Q: What is a cash count?
A: A cash count is a physical verification of cash held on site, such as petty cash, field cash, or any other small cash float. It confirms whether the actual physical cash matches the recorded balance and whether the custodian can account for the money on hand. Cash counts can be scheduled or surprise-based, and surprise counts are especially useful where leakage risk is higher. In an NGO setting, cash counts matter because small cash holdings are often used quickly and can be handled by staff under pressure. A count should be documented, signed, and compared to the ledger or imprest amount. When done well, the process deters misuse and identifies weak documentation before the problem spreads.
Send to the team:
- “Please count physical cash and compare it to the recorded balance.”
Key point:
- Cash counts verify physical cash.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
34. Q: Why is a cash count important for petty cash control?
A: A cash count is important for petty cash control because petty cash is one of the easiest balances to misstate, under-support, or misuse. The fund is small enough that people sometimes treat it casually, but that is exactly why control matters. Regular counts confirm whether the actual cash still matches the imprest amount and whether receipts have been properly filed. They also show whether staff are using petty cash only for approved small expenses or bypassing normal approval channels. In NGOs, petty cash often sits close to the field or front office, where access is broad and recordkeeping pressure is high. A surprise count helps remind staff that the money is still organizational cash and must be accounted for carefully.
Send to the team:
- “Please perform surprise petty cash counts periodically and document any differences immediately.”
Key point:
- Counting detects petty cash problems early.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
35. Q: Who should count petty cash?
A: Petty cash should be counted by someone independent from the petty cash custodian whenever possible. Independence is important because the custodian is responsible for handling the fund and should not be the only person verifying the balance. An independent count adds credibility, reduces the chance of concealment, and makes it easier to detect errors or unusual patterns. In a small NGO, the person doing the count may still be from finance, but should not be the same person who routinely controls the cash box. The count should be brief, documented, and signed by both the counter and the custodian if possible. This simple separation helps preserve trust in a control that is often underestimated because of the fund’s small size.
Send to the team:
- “Please assign petty cash counts to an independent reviewer.”
Key point:
- Independent counting strengthens control.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
36. Q: How should cash shortages be handled?
A: Cash shortages should be investigated immediately, documented clearly, and escalated according to policy. The organization should determine whether the shortage came from a counting error, a posting error, an unsupported disbursement, a theft risk, or some other control failure. If the shortage is real, management should decide whether recovery, disciplinary action, or write-off is appropriate under the NGO’s rules. Repeated shortages are especially serious because they indicate a control weakness rather than an isolated mistake. In the nonprofit context, the issue is not only the amount lost but the credibility of the control environment. Prompt handling helps prevent the same problem from being repeated and shows that even small cash exceptions are taken seriously.
Send to the team:
- “Please report and investigate any cash shortage on the same day.”
Key point:
- Shortages are control exceptions.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
37. Q: How should cash overages be handled?
A: Cash overages should also be documented and investigated because they can signal a counting mistake, incorrect change handling, omitted receipt, or earlier under-recording. An overage should not be treated as harmless just because the fund is in surplus. Instead, finance should determine the source and decide whether to adjust the ledger or keep the amount in suspense pending resolution. In a nonprofit, unexplained overages matter because they can hide deeper control problems, especially where petty cash or field cash moves quickly. A disciplined process treats overages and shortages with the same seriousness: both are exceptions that deserve explanation. This helps ensure the cash balance is accurate rather than merely approximately right.
Send to the team:
- “Please report cash overages and explain the source before closing the cash count.”
Key point:
- Overages still need explanation.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
38. Q: What is petty cash used for in NGOs?
A: Petty cash is used for small, urgent, low-value expenses where normal payment channels would be inefficient or impractical. Typical examples include minor local transport, emergency office supplies, small repairs, or limited field purchases when a formal bank payment would take too long. The purpose is convenience, not control bypass. In a well-run NGO, petty cash should have a narrow approved purpose, a clear threshold, and receipt support for every disbursement. If the fund is used too broadly, it becomes a weak substitute for the main payment system and can quickly accumulate undocumented spending. A narrow use case helps finance keep the control tight while still allowing staff to respond to minor operational needs.
Send to the team:
- “Please use petty cash only for approved small expenses and keep receipts for every use.”
Key point:
- Petty cash is for minor, immediate needs.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
39. Q: Why should petty cash be controlled tightly?
A: Petty cash should be controlled tightly because small amounts are often handled informally, and informality creates risk. Missing receipts, incomplete approvals, late replenishment, and casual access can all turn a small fund into a recurring leakage point. Tight control does not mean making petty cash impossible to use; it means making sure every use is traceable and authorized. In NGO finance, that traceability is essential because petty cash often exists in offices or field locations where supervision is lighter. A good control framework requires receipts, limits, approval rules, regular counts, and prompt replenishment review. When those pieces are in place, petty cash remains a useful tool rather than a recurring audit issue.
Send to the team:
- “Please require receipts and approvals for every petty cash use.”
Key point:
- Small cash still needs strong control.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
40. Q: What is a cash advance?
A: A cash advance is money given to a staff member, partner, or agent before the final expense is fully supported. It is meant to allow someone to carry out an approved activity and later account for the actual use of the funds with receipts and a settlement report. In NGO finance, advances should be treated as temporary balances, not as completed expenses. That means the organization must track who holds the money, why it was issued, when it is due back or due for liquidation, and what support is still missing. If the advance is not cleared on time, it can distort balances and hide misuse. Cash advances are useful tools, but only when the organization controls them from issue to settlement.
Send to the team:
- “Please issue cash advances only for approved purposes and track settlement dates carefully.”
Key point:
- Advances are temporary, not final expense recognition.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
41. Q: How should cash advances be approved?
A: Cash advances should be approved based on purpose, amount, budget availability, recipient, expected timing, and settlement deadline. Approval should happen before the money is released, and the request should clearly show why the advance is needed rather than using it as a default way to move cash around. The approval step should also confirm that the recipient is eligible, understands the liquidation rules, and has not left older advances unresolved. In an NGO, this process matters because advances can easily become hidden working capital if they are not controlled. A clear approval record supports accountability and makes later follow-up much easier. The approval should therefore be specific, dated, and tied to the exact activity being funded.
Send to the team:
- “Please approve advances before disbursement and set a clear settlement date.”
Key point:
- Advance approval should be specific and documented.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
42. Q: How should cash advances be liquidated?
A: Cash advances should be liquidated by submitting receipts, a statement of use, and any unspent balance back to the organization. Finance should review the support, confirm the spending is allowable, and clear the advance from the register only when the file is complete. If part of the amount was not used, the balance should be returned or otherwise accounted for according to policy. Liquidation is not just paperwork; it is the process that converts temporary custody of cash into a properly supported expense. In NGO finance, timely liquidation is important because old advances distort cash, receivables, and expense reporting all at once. A well-run advance process therefore tracks issue, use, settlement, and any exception in one clear flow.
Send to the team:
- “Please liquidate advances promptly with supporting documents and return any unspent balance.”
Key point:
- Liquidation closes the advance properly.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
43. Q: What is the risk of uncleared cash advances?
A: Uncleared cash advances can hide misuse, delay expense recognition, distort cash and receivable balances, and make it difficult to see whether money was used properly. When advances remain open too long, they can also become informal loans or forgotten balances that management no longer actively tracks. In a nonprofit, this is particularly risky because the money often belongs to a donor-funded activity and should be accounted for with precision. Old advances can also weaken trust in staff accountability if controls are not enforced. Finance should therefore monitor age, send reminders, escalate overdue items, and block new advances where necessary until old ones are settled. The longer the delay, the more difficult it becomes to recover the true story of the spending.
Send to the team:
- “Please follow up all overdue cash advances immediately and escalate unresolved items.”
Key point:
- Old advances are a control risk.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
44. Q: What is imprest cash?
A: Imprest cash is a fixed petty cash fund that is maintained at a predetermined amount and replenished only after actual spending is reviewed. The fund amount is kept constant, so the organization can clearly see how much was spent and whether receipts support the uses. If the fund starts at a set level and is replenished only after documentation is checked, control becomes much easier. In NGO finance, imprest cash is useful because it creates a simple, repeatable structure for small cash spending. The fund acts like a controlled envelope: money goes out, receipts come in, and the fund is restored to the original level. That pattern makes shortages, overages, and missing support easier to detect than with an open-ended cash box.
Send to the team:
- “Please keep petty cash on an imprest basis and replenish only with approved support.”
Key point:
- Imprest cash keeps the fund at a fixed level.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
45. Q: How does imprest cash control work?
A: Imprest cash control works by establishing a fixed fund, recording disbursements, and replenishing the fund only for the amount actually spent after support review. Because the fund should return to the original total after replenishment, any difference immediately points to a shortage, overage, or documentation issue. This makes error detection much easier than in a system where the cash balance can drift without structure. For NGOs, this is especially useful in small offices and field locations where petty cash activity is frequent. The control is simple but effective: count the fund, check the receipts, replenish what was approved, and keep the total constant. That discipline gives finance a clear and auditable picture of small cash use.
Send to the team:
- “Please replenish petty cash only after checking all support documents.”
Key point:
- Fixed-fund control makes errors visible.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
46. Q: What is the purpose of a payment approval matrix?
A: A payment approval matrix sets out who can approve which kinds of payments and at what thresholds. It ties authority to amount, risk, and role so the organization does not rely on vague personal discretion. In an NGO, the matrix is important because different payment types carry different risk levels: a small petty cash reimbursement should not be treated the same as a large vendor transfer or payroll run. The matrix should be practical enough to use in daily operations but strong enough to prevent unauthorized spending. It also helps staff know when escalation is required and who the secondary approver should be. When properly designed, the matrix makes spending control predictable instead of ad hoc.
Send to the team:
- “Please use an approval matrix for every payment type and threshold.”
Key point:
- Approval authority should be defined by rule.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
47. Q: Why should payment authority be limited?
A: Payment authority should be limited because concentrated power increases the risk of fraud, error, and unauthorized spending. If one person can request, approve, process, and release funds without oversight, there is little real control even if the records appear complete. Limiting authority does not mean slowing the organization down; it means making sure the right people are accountable at the right steps. In a nonprofit, this is particularly important because payments often relate directly to donor funds, program budgets, or staff compensation. Clear limits make it easier to trace responsibility if something goes wrong and easier to defend the process when reviewed. Well-defined authority also helps staff understand the boundary between operational needs and finance control.
Send to the team:
- “Please restrict payment approval to authorized staff only and keep the limits documented.”
Key point:
- Limited authority reduces misuse risk.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
48. Q: What is a signatory control on bank accounts?
A: Signatory control means only designated people can authorize bank transactions, sign checks, or approve transfers according to the bank mandate. It is a fundamental safeguard because bank access is one of the most direct ways money can move out of the organization. In an NGO, signatory control should reflect the same logic as payment approval: authority should be limited, documented, and reviewed periodically. A bank signatory list that is outdated, too broad, or shared casually weakens the whole treasury environment. The organization should know who can release money, under what conditions, and whether dual authorization is required. Strong signatory control helps prevent unauthorized transactions and supports board-level oversight of cash governance.
Send to the team:
- “Please maintain an updated bank signatory list and review it regularly.”
Key point:
- Signatory control restricts bank access.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
49. Q: Why should bank signatories be segregated from preparers?
A: Bank signatories should be segregated from preparers so that one person cannot create, authorize, and release a payment alone. If the same person can do everything, the organization loses one of its most important protections against error and misuse. Separation of duties makes it harder to hide problems and easier for another person to question unusual transactions before money leaves the bank. In a nonprofit, this matters because many payments are mission-sensitive and may be funded by restricted grants or donor commitments. Segregation also supports cleaner audit trails because the chain of responsibility becomes visible. Even in smaller teams, where perfect separation is difficult, the organization should still try to place review or sign-off elsewhere whenever possible.
Send to the team:
- “Please separate payment preparation from bank authorization wherever possible.”
Key point:
- Segregation of duties protects bank payments.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
50. Q: What is dual authorization for payments?
A: Dual authorization means two approved people must authorize a payment before it is released. It is one of the strongest practical controls over disbursements because it adds a check between initiation and final release. In an NGO, dual authorization is especially valuable for larger payments, sensitive categories, and transactions involving bank transfers or supplier commitments. It helps ensure that the payment was reviewed for legitimacy, completeness, and alignment with budget or funding rules. Dual authorization should not be treated as a formality; the second approver should understand what they are approving. When used consistently, this control reduces unauthorized disbursement risk and strengthens confidence in the payment process.
Send to the team:
- “Please require two approvals for payments above the set threshold.”
Key point:
- Two-step approval strengthens payment control.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
51. Q: When should dual authorization be required?
A: Dual authorization should be required whenever a transaction carries higher financial, operational, or donor risk and when a single person should not be trusted to initiate, approve, and release value on their own. In practice, this usually includes bank transfers, cash withdrawals, payroll release files, vendor payments, journal entries affecting cash or donor balances, and any unusual override of normal controls. The purpose is not to slow work for the sake of formality; it is to create an independent checkpoint before funds move. In an NGO setting, dual authorization is especially important because many payments are tied to restricted funds, donor commitments, and time-sensitive program activities. A strong practice is to define amount thresholds, payment types, and exception cases in policy so staff do not apply the rule inconsistently. For emergency situations, the policy should still require after-the-fact review and documented explanation rather than silent bypassing of the control.
Send to the team:
- “Please require two authorized approvers for high-risk or high-value cash and payment transactions.”
Key point:
- Dual authorization protects cash, reduces unauthorized spending, and creates accountability for sensitive transactions.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
- IRS Publication 557
52. Q: What is the difference between cash control and bank control?
A: Cash control and bank control are related, but they protect different parts of the same treasury process. Cash control covers the physical or digital handling of money before it is deposited or disbursed, including counting cash, securing petty cash, documenting receipts, controlling safe access, and ensuring that cash is recorded on time. Bank control covers what happens inside the bank accounts themselves, including signatory authority, online banking access, statement review, bank reconciliations, transfer approvals, and monitoring of bank fees or suspicious movements. An NGO can have strong bank controls and still lose track of physical cash, or it can control petty cash well and still have weak bank access discipline. That is why the two controls should be designed separately and reviewed separately. A good control environment also makes clear who handles cash, who records it, who approves transfers, and who performs the bank reconciliation, so the same person is not able to conceal problems across both sides of the process.
Send to the team:
- “Please manage cash handling and bank account access as separate control areas with different review points.”
Key point:
- Cash control protects what is physically handled; bank control protects what is held in accounts.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
- IRS Publication 557
53. Q: What is a disbursement forecast?
A: A disbursement forecast is a forward-looking schedule of cash payments that the organization expects to make over a defined period, such as a week, a month, or a quarter. It turns known obligations into a practical cash plan. Instead of looking only at what has already been spent, the forecast asks when actual money will leave the bank for payroll, supplier invoices, rent, grants, taxes, travel, utilities, and other recurring obligations. This matters because a nonprofit can appear healthy in the ledger and still miss payment deadlines if the timing of outflows is not visible. A useful disbursement forecast includes due dates, amount estimates, owner of the payment, funding source, and approval status. It should be updated often enough to reflect new invoices, delayed approvals, or shifting program activity. The forecast is also a management tool: it tells leadership when to slow nonessential spending, when to release reserves, and when to escalate funding gaps before they turn into missed obligations.
Send to the team:
- “Please prepare a forward-looking payment schedule for all expected outflows, with dates and priorities.”
Key point:
- A disbursement forecast predicts when cash will go out and helps prevent avoidable shortfalls.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- IRS Publication 557
- IMF Financial Soundness Indicators
54. Q: How does a disbursement forecast support liquidity?
A: A disbursement forecast supports liquidity by showing cash pressure before the pressure actually arrives. That advance visibility gives management time to act instead of reacting in crisis mode. If payroll, supplier invoices, rent, or grant transfers are expected before the next inflow, the organization can decide whether to delay discretionary spending, reschedule lower-priority payments, accelerate collections, or use approved reserves. The forecast also helps avoid overdrafts, failed bank transfers, late-payment penalties, and disruption to programs that depend on uninterrupted operations. In an NGO, liquidity management is especially important because funding often arrives in tranches, reimbursements may lag behind expenditure, and donor conditions can delay use of funds even when the budget exists. A strong forecast therefore compares timing, not just totals. It should be reviewed alongside actual bank balances and any committed payments that are not yet reflected in the ledger. The best liquidity decisions are made before the bank balance becomes critical, not after the organization has already run short.
Send to the team:
- “Please use the payment forecast to test whether cash will be sufficient each week or month.”
Key point:
- Forecasting outflows helps prevent cash shortages and forced spending cuts.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
- IMF Financial Soundness Indicators
55. Q: What is a cash inflow forecast?
A: A cash inflow forecast is a projection of how much cash the organization expects to receive and when it expects to receive it. It focuses on timing, source, and certainty of receipts rather than only on budgeted revenue. In a nonprofit, inflows may come from donor grants, contributions, reimbursements, service fees, membership income, investment income, government reimbursements, or other operating receipts. A good inflow forecast distinguishes between confirmed receipts, likely receipts, and optimistic assumptions so management can see how fragile or secure the position really is. It should also separate unrestricted cash from restricted cash where applicable, because cash in the bank is not always fully available for immediate use. The forecast becomes more useful when it includes the contractual basis for each inflow, the expected receipt date, the person responsible for follow-up, and any conditions that might delay payment. That way, treasury can compare the forecast to actual receipts and identify patterns such as chronic delays, slow donor processing, or gaps in collection follow-up.
Send to the team:
- “Please map all expected receipts by date, source, and certainty level.”
Key point:
- A cash inflow forecast predicts when funds will arrive and how reliable they are.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- IRS Publication 557
- IMF Financial Soundness Indicators
56. Q: What are the main cash inflows in an NGO?
A: The main cash inflows in an NGO usually come from donor grants, contributions, project reimbursements, service income, membership fees, investment income, and in some cases government support or partnership transfers. The exact pattern depends on the organization’s funding model and mission. A humanitarian NGO may rely heavily on donor tranches and reimbursements, while a service delivery organization may also collect fees or contractual income. The important finance point is that each inflow should be identified by source, purpose, and restriction so the organization can trace where the money came from and how it can be used. It is also useful to separate recurring inflows from one-time inflows, because recurring receipts support planning while one-time receipts may not be reliable enough to fund permanent costs. When finance teams understand the true mix of inflows, they can assess concentration risk, forecast continuity, and see whether the organization is overdependent on one donor or funding channel.
Send to the team:
- “Please classify all cash receipts by source, restriction, and expected timing.”
Key point:
- NGO inflows usually come from donors, services, reimbursements, and other operating receipts.
Official links:
- IRS Publication 557
- GAO Green Book
- FASB Not-for-Profits
57. Q: What are the main cash outflows in an NGO?
A: The main cash outflows in an NGO are payroll, supplier payments, program spending, rent, utilities, grants or sub-awards to partners, taxes where applicable, debt service, travel, and other operating costs. These outflows should be organized by timing, responsibility, and funding source rather than treated as one large pool of spending. The finance team needs to know which outflows are fixed, which are variable, which are urgent, and which can be delayed if cash tightens. Payroll and rent are usually non-discretionary. Program spending may be committed by donor workplan or activity schedule. Supplier payments may depend on invoice due dates and procurement terms. Grants to partners may depend on milestones or compliance documentation. By mapping outflows in this way, management can identify which costs protect mission delivery and which can be phased without damaging the program. The forecast then becomes a practical cash management tool rather than a static list of invoices.
Send to the team:
- “Please list all expected payments with dates, owners, and approval status.”
Key point:
- NGO outflows are driven by people, programs, vendors, and obligations.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- IRS Publication 557
- IMF Financial Soundness Indicators
58. Q: How should payroll be planned in cash forecasting?
A: Payroll should be treated as one of the most time-sensitive and non-discretionary items in the forecast. It is rarely a cost that can be ignored or delayed without serious consequences. The forecast should include gross salaries, employer contributions, benefits, allowances, overtime, statutory deductions, and the exact payment date. It should also include any processing lag between payroll approval and actual bank release, because a finance team that looks only at the pay date may miss the preparation window. In NGOs, payroll often represents the largest predictable monthly cash outflow, so it can become the anchor point for all other liquidity planning. A well-run forecast separates routine payroll from special items such as back pay, bonuses, severance, or salary adjustments. Those exceptional items should be flagged early so they do not surprise the treasury team. Payroll planning is strongest when HR, finance, and operations share one calendar and one approved staff list, so the forecast reflects real headcount rather than assumptions from a prior month.
Send to the team:
- “Please include gross payroll, employer costs, and payment dates in the cash forecast.”
Key point:
- Payroll is a priority cash outflow that must be forecast precisely.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- IRS Publication 557
- FASB Accounting Standards Updates
59. Q: How should supplier payments be planned in cash forecasting?
A: Supplier payments should be planned from the approved invoice and procurement schedule, not from guesswork or the date finance happens to see the bill. The forecast should reflect due dates, contract terms, purchase order commitments, receipt confirmation, and business priority. A supplier who supports mission-critical activity, field delivery, or essential utilities may need to be paid before a lower-priority administrative vendor. At the same time, the organization should avoid paying too early unless there is a business case, because early payments can reduce liquidity unnecessarily. A solid forecast also checks for duplicate invoices, disputed amounts, incomplete delivery, and pending approvals before the payment date is entered as final. This matters because vendor relationships can be damaged by late payment, but cash can also be wasted by paying before goods or services are properly received. Good treasury practice therefore combines payment discipline with operational judgment: pay the right supplier, at the right time, for the right amount, with the right evidence.
Send to the team:
- “Please schedule supplier payments by due date, contract terms, and business priority.”
Key point:
- Supplier payment timing affects continuity, vendor confidence, and liquidity.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
- IRS Publication 557
60. Q: How should grant receipts be planned in cash forecasting?
A: Grant receipts should be forecast based on the actual payment mechanics in the grant agreement, not on the total budget or the grant award value alone. The finance team should read whether the donor pays in advance, in milestones, on reimbursement, or after reporting and approval. The forecast should then reflect the most realistic receipt date for each tranche. If the donor requires narrative reports, expenditure evidence, or compliance clearance before payment, those steps should be built into the forecast because they affect timing. This is especially important when a grant appears “fully funded” on paper but the cash has not yet arrived in the bank. A grant can support activity in principle while still creating a short-term liquidity problem in practice. Good forecasting therefore distinguishes budget timing from cash timing. It also tracks expected claim dates, approval dates, and actual receipt dates so finance can measure collection delays and improve follow-up with donors or grant administrators.
Send to the team:
- “Please forecast grant cash by expected receipt date, not by budget date.”
Key point:
- Grant budget timing is not the same as cash timing.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- IRS Publication 557
- FASB Accounting Standards Updates
61. Q: What is timing risk in cash management?
A: Timing risk is the risk that cash will arrive later than expected or leave earlier than expected. It is one of the most practical treasury risks because it can create stress even when the overall budget looks healthy. For example, an NGO may expect a donor tranche in the same week that payroll falls due, but if the tranche arrives several days late, the organization may face an immediate payment problem. Timing risk also shows up when invoice approvals take longer than expected, bank transfers settle slowly, or field advances are liquidated too late to support accurate forecasting. The critical idea is that accounting balances do not always tell the full treasury story. A ledger can show expected income and expense while the bank account still needs actual cash today. That is why cash management must focus on dates, not only totals. The better the forecast granularity, the smaller the timing surprises.
Send to the team:
- “Please review cash timing separately from accounting balances and budget totals.”
Key point:
- Timing risk is one of the biggest treasury risks in NGO operations.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- IMF Financial Soundness Indicators
62. Q: Why does timing matter more than accounting in treasury?
A: Timing matters more in treasury because bank payments and receipts are settled by actual cash availability, not by whether income or expense has already been recorded. An NGO can be technically solvent in the ledger and still fail to pay staff if the bank balance is temporarily short. Treasury therefore asks a different question from accounting: not “What has been recognized?” but “What can actually be paid today, this week, or this month?” That distinction matters in donor-funded organizations, where budget approvals, grant restrictions, and reimbursement cycles can all create gaps between recorded activity and real cash. Treasury staff should therefore work from updated payment dates, expected receipt dates, and confirmed bank balances, then compare them with priority obligations. The more uncertain the cash timing, the stronger the need for reserves, rolling forecasts, and escalation procedures. Accounting supports the record; treasury protects the organization’s ability to continue operating.
Send to the team:
- “Please manage treasury based on actual cash dates, not only ledger balances.”
Key point:
- Treasury depends on real cash timing, not only accounting entries.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- IMF Financial Soundness Indicators
- FASB Accounting Standards Updates
63. Q: What happens when donor funds are delayed?
A: When donor funds are delayed, the organization may have to slow activity, adjust the spending plan, renegotiate payment dates, or use reserves until the expected funds arrive. In a nonprofit, donor delays do not just affect bookkeeping; they can affect service delivery, staff morale, supplier confidence, and field continuity. A delay may come from the donor’s own approval process, a missing report, an unmet milestone, or a mismatch between claim documentation and donor expectations. Finance should therefore treat delay as both a cash issue and a governance issue. The first response is to identify whether the delay is temporary or structural. The second is to separate urgent obligations such as payroll and essential vendor payments from lower-priority commitments. The third is to inform management early enough that there is room to act. Delayed donor cash often creates the biggest problems when nobody flags it until the payment date has already passed.
Send to the team:
- “Please escalate donor delays as soon as they affect cash availability or program continuity.”
Key point:
- Delayed donor cash can disrupt operations quickly and should be flagged early.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- IRS Publication 557
- IMF Financial Soundness Indicators
64. Q: How should an NGO respond to cash shortfalls?
A: An NGO should respond to a cash shortfall quickly and in a structured way rather than waiting for the bank balance to recover by itself. The immediate steps are to confirm the size and timing of the shortfall, review the next payroll and supplier commitments, identify any receivables or donor payments that can be accelerated, and freeze nonessential spending until the position is understood. Management should then decide whether to use reserves, adjust the program schedule, renegotiate with suppliers, or escalate to the board. A good response plan also makes room for communication: staff, vendors, and donors may need carefully managed explanations if payment timing changes. The goal is not panic but prioritization. A shortfall is manageable when it is identified early and handled with discipline. It becomes much more damaging when it is hidden inside the month-end close and discovered only after commitments have already been made.
Send to the team:
- “Please activate the shortfall response plan when forecast cash turns negative or too tight.”
Key point:
- Cash shortfalls require immediate prioritization, communication, and escalation.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
- IRS Publication 557
65. Q: What is a liquidity gap?
A: A liquidity gap is the period or amount by which expected cash outflows exceed available cash inflows and cash balances. It is not just a theoretical accounting problem; it is the point at which the organization may fail to meet obligations on time. The gap can be short, such as a few days between payroll and donor receipt, or longer, such as several weeks of slow reimbursement. What makes the concept important is that it looks forward. A liquidity gap tells management when the pressure will hit, how large the pressure will be, and what kind of action may be needed. In practice, finance teams may map the gap by week or month and compare it with reserve coverage, committed inflows, and nonessential spending that can be delayed. The earlier the gap is identified, the more options the organization has. The later it is identified, the fewer choices remain.
Send to the team:
- “Please identify any week or month where payments exceed available cash.”
Key point:
- A liquidity gap is a cash timing shortage that can threaten operations.
Official links:
- IMF Financial Soundness Indicators
- GAO Green Book
66. Q: How can a nonprofit identify a liquidity gap early?
A: A nonprofit can identify a liquidity gap early by using a rolling forecast, updating bank balances frequently, comparing expected inflows and outflows by week or month, and reviewing committed payments before they are released. It should also compare forecast assumptions against real collection patterns so it does not overstate future cash. Early warning signs often include repeated delays from donors, growing unpaid supplier balances, payroll pressure near month-end, or increasing reliance on manual treasury workarounds. The finance team should watch for these indicators before the bank balance becomes critical. A useful practice is to maintain a forward cash view covering at least the next several payment cycles, not just the current month. That way, management can see whether the gap is temporary or systemic. Early identification is important because it gives the organization options such as payment sequencing, reserve use, or funding escalation before the gap turns into crisis.
Send to the team:
- “Please review future cash position before approving new spending.”
Key point:
- Early forecasting is the best way to spot liquidity problems before they become emergencies.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- IMF Financial Soundness Indicators
- GAO internal control standards
67. Q: What is a cash reserve?
A: A cash reserve is a set amount of money held back to absorb timing delays, emergencies, and unexpected costs. It is a cushion that allows the organization to continue operating when normal inflows are delayed or when spending is higher than expected. A reserve is not the same as idle cash. It should have a purpose, a target level, and a rule for when it may be used. In a nonprofit, reserves are often used to protect payroll, core service delivery, and urgent obligations during periods of funding uncertainty. They can also help the organization avoid making poor short-term decisions simply because one donor payment was late. A reserve becomes especially valuable when revenue is concentrated, when field activity is expensive to interrupt, or when the organization must keep promises to staff or beneficiaries regardless of collection timing. The key discipline is that reserves should be managed deliberately and replenished after use rather than treated as open spending money.
Send to the team:
- “Please maintain an approved reserve for cash timing shocks and emergencies.”
Key point:
- A reserve is a buffer that protects operations during uncertainty.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- IMF Financial Soundness Indicators
68. Q: Why should an NGO maintain a cash reserve?
A: An NGO should maintain a cash reserve because donor timing is rarely perfectly smooth, program obligations do not stop just because cash is late, and operational credibility can be damaged quickly by missed payroll or unpaid suppliers. A reserve protects the organization from one-off shocks and also buys time for management to make better decisions under pressure. It can reduce the need for emergency borrowing, rushed spending cuts, or uncontrolled payment delays. In the nonprofit context, reserve discipline also strengthens stewardship: it shows that management is not treating every received dollar as immediately spendable. Instead, some cash is set aside to preserve continuity, protect beneficiaries, and maintain basic stability. The reserve should be sized according to risk, concentration of funding, seasonal spending, and the speed at which alternative funding can be mobilized. A reserve policy makes the practice clear and prevents ad hoc use of protected cash when the organization comes under pressure.
Send to the team:
- “Please protect a minimum reserve level unless management formally approves its use.”
Key point:
- Reserves help the NGO survive timing shocks and continue core operations.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- IMF Financial Soundness Indicators
69. Q: How should a reserve policy be used in treasury management?
A: A reserve policy should be used as an operational rulebook for deciding when protected cash can be drawn down, who can authorize the draw, how the release is documented, and how the reserve is replenished. Treasury should not treat reserve money as merely another bank balance with no boundaries. Instead, the policy should define the reserve purpose, the target amount, the minimum floor, escalation triggers, permitted emergencies, and reporting requirements after any use. In practice, this means that when cash pressure appears, treasury first checks whether the shortfall can be solved through timing changes or payment sequencing before touching the reserve. If the reserve must be used, the event should be reported clearly to management so that replenishment becomes a priority. That discipline prevents silent erosion of the organization’s safety buffer and keeps leadership aware of how much protection remains.
Send to the team:
- “Please follow the reserve policy before using any protected balance.”
Key point:
- Reserve policy turns cash buffers into controlled resources rather than discretionary cash.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
70. Q: What is cash concentration?
A: Cash concentration is the process of moving available balances from multiple accounts into a smaller number of central accounts so that treasury can see and control liquidity more clearly. The practice is common when an organization has separate project accounts, field accounts, or country accounts that each hold small balances that are not efficiently managed on their own. By concentrating cash, the finance team reduces fragmentation, improves visibility, and makes forecasting easier. It also lowers the chance that idle money will be overlooked in one account while another account is short. In a nonprofit setting, concentration must still respect donor restrictions, local banking requirements, and legal or operational constraints. The goal is not to move every cent into one place blindly, but to make sure surplus balances are visible and managed centrally. This approach improves oversight, simplifies reconciliation, and reduces the risk that money is left stranded in low-use accounts.
Send to the team:
- “Please sweep idle balances into the main operating account where appropriate and permitted.”
Key point:
- Cash concentration improves visibility, control, and liquidity planning.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- IMF Financial Soundness Indicators
71. Q: Why should cash not be left idle in too many accounts?
A: Cash should not be left idle in too many accounts because scattered balances are harder to monitor, easier to overlook, and less efficient to use for organization-wide needs. Too many accounts also create extra reconciliation work, weaken the treasury team’s view of total available cash, and can hide small balances that are never swept back into productive use. In some cases, unnecessary accounts also create fee leakage or access-control risk. The more accounts there are, the more signatories, online banking profiles, passwords, and statement reviews the organization must manage. That complexity increases the chance of error. For NGOs that operate across locations or projects, some account separation may be necessary. Even so, finance should regularly ask whether each account still serves a real purpose. If the answer is no, the account may be better closed or consolidated. Cleaner banking structures usually improve control and reduce administrative burden.
Send to the team:
- “Please reduce unnecessary bank accounts and consolidate idle balances where possible.”
Key point:
- Too many accounts weaken visibility, control, and efficiency.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
72. Q: What is bank transfer control?
A: Bank transfer control is the collection of steps used to make sure transfers between bank accounts are legitimate, approved, documented, and recorded correctly. It covers the full path from request to execution to reconciliation. A strong transfer control process checks the reason for the transfer, the source and destination accounts, the approved amount, the signatory authority, the beneficiary details, and the ledger entry that follows. Internal transfers are easy to treat casually because no outside vendor is involved, but that is exactly why they need discipline. A poorly controlled internal transfer can hide misclassification, temporary shortages, or unauthorized movements of restricted cash. In an NGO with multiple accounts, transfer control also helps distinguish real income from simple movement of funds. Without that discipline, finance reports can become misleading even when the bank statements look active. Good control means every transfer leaves a trace that can be reviewed later without guessing.
Send to the team:
- “Please document and review every inter-account transfer from request through reconciliation.”
Key point:
- Internal transfers need the same discipline as external payments.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
73. Q: Why should internal transfers be documented?
A: Internal transfers should be documented so that the organization can trace what moved, why it moved, who approved it, and how it was recorded. This prevents confusion between true revenue and mere movement of cash from one bank account to another. Documentation also supports bank reconciliation, because the finance team can match transfers from one account against deposits in another and identify delays, errors, or missing entries. In a nonprofit, documentation is even more important when transfers involve restricted funds, field accounts, or donor-specific balances. A transfer may be operationally necessary, but it can still affect reporting if it is not explained. Good documentation typically includes the request, approval, transfer confirmation, dates, amounts, account references, and the accounting entry. The point is not paperwork for its own sake. The point is to leave a defensible trail that any reviewer can follow months later.
Send to the team:
- “Please attach support for every internal transfer and retain the trail in the finance file.”
Key point:
- Documentation prevents transfer errors, misclassification, and reconciliation problems.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
74. Q: What is an unauthorized withdrawal risk?
A: An unauthorized withdrawal risk is the possibility that money will leave a bank account without valid approval, valid purpose, or valid supporting evidence. It can happen through weak signatory control, shared login credentials, forged instructions, social engineering, or poor review of online banking activity. In an NGO, the risk is particularly serious because withdrawals may be made from accounts holding donor funds or balances reserved for payroll and operations. Once money leaves the account, recovery can be difficult. That is why prevention matters so much. Preventive controls include restricted access, dual authorization, approved payment limits, transaction alerts, and regular review of bank activity. Detective controls include statement checks and reconciliation. Corrective controls include blocking access, investigating the incident, and tightening procedures. An effective treasury function assumes unauthorized withdrawal is possible and designs controls accordingly, rather than relying on trust alone.
Send to the team:
- “Please enforce approval and access controls on every withdrawal channel.”
Key point:
- Unauthorized withdrawals are a direct cash loss and control failure risk.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
75. Q: What are common fraud risks in cash handling?
A: Common fraud risks in cash handling include skimming receipts before they are recorded, creating fake expense claims, making duplicate payments, forging approvals, altering beneficiary bank details, hiding shortages through false entries, and delaying deposits to misuse cash temporarily. These risks are not limited to large organizations; they can also appear in small NGOs where one person handles several steps of the process. Cash is risky because it is liquid, easy to move, and hard to recover once lost. The stronger the separation of duties, the less room there is for one person to manipulate the cycle from start to finish. Management should therefore watch for warning signs such as missing support, repeated manual adjustments, unusual timing patterns, or unexplained reconciliations. Cash fraud is rarely successful in a well-controlled process unless several controls fail together. That is why the process should be designed as layers, not as a single gatekeeper.
Send to the team:
- “Please watch for duplicate, missing, altered, or unexplained cash movements.”
Key point:
- Cash is highly exposed to fraud if controls are weak or concentrated in one person.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
76. Q: What is the role of segregation of duties in cash control?
A: Segregation of duties is one of the most important cash controls because it prevents any single person from controlling every major step of a cash transaction. The person who handles cash should not also be the only person who records it, approves it, reconciles it, and releases it. Splitting these responsibilities creates independent checks that make fraud harder and errors easier to find. In an NGO, where field teams, finance staff, and project staff may all touch the cash process, segregation can be difficult but still necessary. Even when staffing is small, the organization should separate at least the highest-risk duties and use compensating controls where full separation is not possible. Examples include manager review, periodic spot checks, and independent bank reconciliation. The principle is simple: if one person can take cash, hide the evidence, and approve the record, the control environment is too weak.
Send to the team:
- “Please separate cash custody, recording, approval, and reconciliation duties wherever possible.”
Key point:
- Segregation of duties reduces error, concealment, and misuse risk.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
77. Q: Why should the person approving payment be different from the person preparing it?
A: The approver and preparer should be different because the approval step is meant to be an independent review, not a rubber stamp. If the same person prepares the payment and authorizes it, there is no real check on whether the payment is valid, complete, correctly coded, or consistent with policy. In a nonprofit environment, this separation also matters because funds may be tied to donor restrictions, budget limits, procurement rules, or program approvals. Independent approval helps ensure that spending is not only technically accurate but also mission-appropriate. The approver should be able to ask whether the payment has proper support, whether the vendor is approved, whether the cost belongs to the right project, and whether the amount is within the delegated limit. Preparing a payment is a transaction task; approving it is a control task. Those roles should not be merged unless there is a very clear and documented reason in a controlled exception process.
Send to the team:
- “Please keep payment preparation and approval separate.”
Key point:
- Independent approval protects payment integrity and reduces self-approval risk.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
78. Q: Why should the person recording cash be different from the person handling cash?
A: The person recording cash should be different from the person handling it because recordkeeping is supposed to provide an independent picture of what actually happened. If the same person both holds the money and writes the books, it becomes much easier to hide shortages, delay deposits, alter receipts, or invent supporting entries after the fact. Separation creates a check between what the custodian says happened and what the records show. That is why even small NGOs should try to separate cash custody from accounting wherever possible. If full separation is impossible, management should add compensating controls such as supervisor review, surprise counts, and frequent reconciliations. In practice, the custodian should collect and secure the cash, while another person records the transaction and confirms that the deposit or disbursement matches the source document. That division gives the organization a chance to detect problems before they spread into the ledger and the donor report.
Send to the team:
- “Please separate cash custody from cash recording.”
Key point:
- Separate custody from recordkeeping so the record can be independently checked.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
79. Q: What is a cash receipt process?
A: A cash receipt process is the full sequence used to receive, count, document, record, deposit, and reconcile cash and cash-like inflows. It applies to physical cash, checks, transfers, mobile money, and other receipt channels. The purpose is to make sure every receipt is captured promptly and accurately, then tied to the correct source and fund. A good process starts with the source document or incoming notice, continues through acknowledgment or counting, then moves to recording, deposit, and reconciliation. The process should also define who handles cash, who issues receipts, who confirms deposits, and who reviews discrepancies. In an NGO, receipt discipline matters because some inflows may come from field offices, project sites, or decentralized locations where money is more exposed. The stronger the receipt process, the less room there is for unrecorded collections or delayed banking. Timeliness matters as much as accuracy because money sitting off-book is money that can be lost, misplaced, or misused.
Send to the team:
- “Please record and deposit cash receipts promptly and traceably.”
Key point:
- Receipt processing must be timely, complete, and documented from source to bank.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
80. Q: How should receipts be recorded on the day they are received?
A: Receipts should be recorded as soon as possible on the day they are received, or at the latest according to the organization’s formal close and banking rules. Same-day recording reduces the time between custody and accounting, which lowers the risk of misplacement, theft, duplicate handling, or later disputes about what was received. This is especially useful for field collections, small cash receipts, and donor-related receipts where staff may otherwise hold money overnight or wait until the end of a trip to record it. A proper same-day process should include a count, a receipt or acknowledgment, a log entry, and a deposit or secure storage action. If exact same-day recording is not possible, the reason should be documented and the delay should be short and reviewable. The bigger principle is that the finance record should follow the money quickly, not slowly. When recording is delayed, evidence becomes weaker and reconciliation becomes harder.
Send to the team:
- “Please log cash receipts immediately and match them to deposits or secure custody.”
Key point:
- Same-day recording strengthens control, transparency, and reconciliation.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
81. Q: What is a cash disbursement process?
A: A cash disbursement process is the sequence used to review, approve, pay, record, and reconcile outgoing money. It begins with a valid business trigger, such as an invoice, approved request, or contracted obligation, and ends only when the payment is posted and matched against the bank. A strong disbursement process verifies that the payment is legitimate, accurate, budgeted, approved, and supported. It also checks the payee, amount, bank details, coding, and timing. In nonprofits, disbursements must often align with donor restrictions and procurement rules, so the process cannot focus only on whether a bill exists. It must also ask whether the cost belongs in the right project and whether payment is allowed now. Disbursement control protects cash, but it also protects reporting quality and donor accountability because the same transaction affects the ledger, the bank, and the supporting file. The process should therefore be designed as a chain of checks, not just as a payment button.
Send to the team:
- “Please pay only after review, approval, and support are complete.”
Key point:
- Disbursement processing should link approval, payment, documentation, and posting.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
82. Q: How should disbursements be matched to approved documentation?
A: Disbursements should be matched to approved documentation before any money is released. The supporting file should show what was ordered, what was received, what was approved, and what amount is due. Depending on the transaction, that may include an invoice, purchase order, contract, service completion note, goods-received note, approval email, or payment request. Matching is important because it prevents overpayment, duplicate payment, or payment for goods and services that were never properly received. In an NGO, matching also helps confirm that the expense belongs to the right program, donor, or cost category. The review should not be mechanical only; it should ask whether the payment makes sense in context. If the documentation is incomplete, the payment should either be held or formally escalated. Good matching is what turns an invoice into a defensible disbursement rather than a risky cash movement.
Send to the team:
- “Please match each payment to approved support before releasing funds.”
Key point:
- Payment should follow proof, approval, and a clear business basis.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
83. Q: What is the role of supporting documents in treasury?
A: Supporting documents are the evidence behind treasury actions. They show why a cash movement happened, who approved it, what it related to, and how it should be recorded. In a treasury context, documents support payments, transfers, receipts, bank reconciliations, exception handling, and any follow-up on unusual items. They are what allows a reviewer to confirm that the bank activity was valid rather than accidental or suspicious. In many NGOs, treasury documents are also the bridge between finance and program operations, because a payment may relate to procurement, payroll, travel, field advances, or donor reporting. Without the documents, treasury becomes a set of numbers with no context. With the documents, the organization can reconstruct events and defend its decisions later. The best practice is to keep the documents complete, readable, dated, approved, and filed in a way that allows easy retrieval during internal review, donor review, or audit.
Send to the team:
- “Please file every payment, transfer, and receipt with its support.”
Key point:
- Treasury decisions should always be supported by evidence that can be reviewed later.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
84. Q: Why is physical security important for cash and bank materials?
A: Physical security is important because cash, cheque books, tokens, signed instruments, cards, and other payment materials can be misused if they are left unprotected. Even in organizations with strong policy on paper, weak physical custody can create an easy path for fraud or error. Secure storage matters for both cash and the tools used to move money. That includes safes, locked cabinets, restricted rooms, access logs, and limited distribution of bank materials. In an NGO, physical security also applies to field offices, sub-offices, and temporary project locations where controls may be weaker than at headquarters. The objective is to make unauthorized access visibly difficult and formally traceable. If a payment instrument is lost or stolen, the organization should be able to identify who had custody and when. Strong physical security does not replace good accounting, but it closes an important gap that accounting alone cannot fix.
Send to the team:
- “Please store all cash and bank instruments in secure, access-controlled locations.”
Key point:
- Physical security protects the tools used to move money and prevents avoidable loss.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
85. Q: How should cheque books be controlled?
A: Cheque books should be controlled as sensitive payment instruments. They should be numbered, logged, stored securely, issued only to authorized persons, and periodically reconciled against use. Every used, voided, spoiled, or unused cheque should remain traceable. In organizations that still use cheques, poor cheque control creates a direct fraud and error risk because cheques can be removed, altered, or misused before the finance team notices. The control record should show who received the cheque book, when it was issued, who used each cheque, which cheques were voided, and where the supporting documents are kept. Even if cheque usage is declining, the organization should not assume the risk has disappeared. Older banking channels are often the least reviewed and sometimes the least controlled. A simple register and periodic review can prevent major problems later.
Send to the team:
- “Please log cheque book custody and every cheque issued, voided, or returned.”
Key point:
- Cheque control depends on custody, numbering, and traceability.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
86. Q: How should unused cash be stored safely?
A: Unused cash should be stored in a locked, access-controlled, and regularly checked location such as a safe or secure cash box system. The goal is to reduce the chance of theft, loss, tampering, or unauthorized access. Safe storage is not only about the physical container; it also includes who has access, who holds the key or code, how access is logged, and how often the cash is counted. In a nonprofit, this matters in headquarters, field offices, project sites, and transit situations where cash may temporarily be held before banking or disbursement. The cash count should be independent where possible, or at least periodically reviewed by a supervisor. The storage practice should also reflect whether the money is restricted, petty cash, or operational float. Good custody rules make it clear that the cash is never just “lying around.” It is held under a formal responsibility chain.
Send to the team:
- “Please keep idle cash locked and count it regularly.”
Key point:
- Safe custody prevents physical loss and misuse.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
87. Q: What is a cash policy?
A: A cash policy is the formal set of rules that governs how cash is received, held, approved, disbursed, reconciled, and safeguarded. It tells staff what must happen, who is responsible, which approvals are required, what records must be kept, and what exceptions need escalation. In an NGO, a cash policy is especially useful because cash may move through headquarters, field offices, mobile teams, or temporary project locations. Without a written policy, people tend to improvise. Improvisation may be acceptable once or twice, but it usually leads to inconsistent treatment, weak evidence, and control gaps. A good cash policy covers receipts, disbursements, petty cash, bank transfers, signatory authority, bank reconciliations, reserve handling, emergency use, and filing requirements. It should also say what is prohibited, such as personal use of funds, unofficial loans, or uncontrolled handoffs. In short, the policy creates the rules of the road for treasury work.
Send to the team:
- “Please use the cash policy as the rulebook for all cash handling and bank transactions.”
Key point:
- A cash policy defines how cash must be controlled from receipt to reconciliation.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
- IRS Publication 557
88. Q: What should a cash policy cover?
A: A cash policy should cover the entire cash lifecycle, not just one small section of it. It should define who can receive cash, how receipts are recorded, how bank deposits are made, which approvals are required for payments, how petty cash is replenished, how reserves are protected, how bank accounts are accessed, how reconciliations are completed, and how exceptions are handled. It should also state documentation rules, spending limits, safe custody expectations, review frequency, and escalation lines when something looks unusual. For NGOs, the policy should be practical enough for field teams and strict enough for donor-funded activity. It should distinguish between routine payments and emergency exceptions so people know what happens when normal procedures cannot be followed. The best cash policy is one that staff can actually use because it matches the way the organization works while still preserving control. A policy that exists only for audit shelves is not useful.
Send to the team:
- “Please ensure the cash policy covers routine, field, and emergency cash actions.”
Key point:
- A useful cash policy covers the full cash lifecycle and the key exception paths.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
89. Q: What is a bank account opening procedure?
A: A bank account opening procedure is the approved process used to create a new bank account, define who can operate it, collect required documents, and record the account in the finance system. It protects the organization from informal account creation and hidden banking activity. In a nonprofit, a new bank account should never be opened casually by one staff member without a proper business reason. The procedure should require a purpose statement, approval by the right authority, signatory designations, ownership information, a coding structure, and a plan for bank reconciliation from the first day. It should also ask whether the new account is truly needed or whether an existing account could be used instead. Account proliferation creates control burden, so opening an account should be treated as a managed decision rather than an administrative shortcut. Once opened, the account should be tracked in the bank register and included in the reconciliation calendar immediately.
Send to the team:
- “Please open bank accounts only through an approved process with documented signatories and a stated business reason.”
Key point:
- New bank accounts should never be opened informally or outside the finance register.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
90. Q: Why should bank account access be tightly controlled?
A: Bank account access should be tightly controlled because online banking credentials, signatory rights, tokens, and mobile approvals can move money immediately. Once access exists, the wrong person can pay, transfer, change beneficiary details, or review confidential balances. Tightly controlling access means limiting who can log in, who can approve, who can release, who can amend beneficiary data, and who can see statement information. In an NGO, this is especially important because field staff, finance staff, and management may all need different levels of access. Access should be assigned by role and reviewed regularly so that employees who leave, change jobs, or no longer need the account cannot still use it. Strong access control also supports segregation of duties, because the same person should not be able to create, approve, and release the same transfer. The account may be digital, but the risk is very real.
Send to the team:
- “Please restrict bank access to authorized users only and review it regularly.”
Key point:
- Bank access is a high-risk control point that must be role-based and reviewed.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
91. Q: What is a payment batch?
A: A payment batch is a grouped set of approved payments prepared for release together through a bank transfer system or payment platform. Batching helps finance review several items as one package before the release step. That creates an opportunity to check payees, amounts, bank details, coding, approval status, duplicates, and budget alignment before anything leaves the account. In a nonprofit, batching is common for payroll, vendor settlements, or regular operating payments. The batch should have a clear cut-off time, preparer, reviewer, and approver. It should also be locked once reviewed so items are not changed without recheck. Batching can improve efficiency, but only if it does not hide risk. If the batch is very large or sensitive, a secondary review is wise. The key question is whether the group of payments has been tested for correctness before the final click or bank release is made.
Send to the team:
- “Please compile all payments into a reviewed batch before release.”
Key point:
- Batching supports review before payment execution, but it must still be controlled.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
92. Q: How should payment batches be reviewed before release?
A: Payment batches should be reviewed for the basics first and the exceptions second. The reviewer should check that each payee is valid, each amount is correct, each invoice or support file exists, each transaction has the right coding, and each payment has the required approval. Then the reviewer should scan for duplicates, unusual bank details, missing attachments, spending outside budget, and any item that appears out of place in the batch. If the batch includes payroll or donor-related payments, special care is needed because the consequences of error are higher. A good review process also confirms that the batch is being released by the right person at the right time and that any emergency or override item is explicitly documented. The objective is to catch errors before money leaves the account, not after the bank statement arrives. A second reviewer is valuable because it adds an independent checkpoint on a high-risk action.
Send to the team:
- “Please verify payee, amount, coding, and approval before releasing a batch.”
Key point:
- Batch review prevents payment errors and fraud before funds move.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
93. Q: What is the risk of unrecorded bank charges?
A: The risk of unrecorded bank charges is that the bank balance and the ledger balance will drift apart, fees will accumulate unnoticed, and management will not see the true cost of banking. Even small charges can create meaningful discrepancies over time if they are not posted and reconciled. Unrecorded charges can also distort donor reports, project cost allocations, and management views of liquidity because the organization thinks it has more cash than it really does. They may also conceal unauthorized account activity or unusual transaction patterns. A strong finance team therefore reviews bank statements line by line, posts known bank fees promptly, and investigates any unfamiliar charge. This is not just bookkeeping accuracy; it is part of treasury control. If the organization is paying for services it did not approve or no longer uses, the bank charge review is often where that first becomes visible.
Send to the team:
- “Please review bank statements for fees and post them promptly.”
Key point:
- Small bank fees can create real reporting, reconciliation, and control problems.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
94. Q: How should bank charges be monitored?
A: Bank charges should be monitored through regular statement review, reconciliation, and comparison against the organization’s expected fee structure or banking agreement. Monitoring should ask whether the fee is legitimate, whether it was expected, whether it was duplicated, and whether the bank service still makes sense for the organization. If a charge looks unusual, it should be investigated quickly while the evidence is still easy to retrieve. In a nonprofit, fee monitoring matters because unnecessary banking costs reduce resources available for mission work. It also matters because some charges may indicate overdrafts, failed transfers, cross-border costs, dormant account charges, or service levels that no longer fit the organization’s needs. Regular fee analysis can support bank account rationalization and negotiation with providers. Good monitoring therefore has a financial benefit and a control benefit at the same time.
Send to the team:
- “Please track all bank fees and investigate any unusual charge.”
Key point:
- Fee monitoring protects cash and supports better banking decisions.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
95. Q: What is foreign currency cash risk?
A: Foreign currency cash risk is the risk that the value of cash held in another currency will change because exchange rates move. If an NGO holds dollars, euros, pounds, shillings, or another foreign currency, the local-value equivalent can rise or fall even if the foreign amount itself does not change. This matters when the organization must pay local expenses, report in a functional currency, or convert balances at a later date. Foreign currency cash risk is therefore both a treasury issue and a reporting issue. A balance may look stable in the foreign currency ledger but become worth less in local terms by the time it is used. Conversely, a favorable movement can create a gain. Good management tracks the currency of the balance, the planned use date, the conversion expectation, and the policy for revaluation or translation. The organization should also distinguish between actual cash available and any accounting effect created by exchange movement.
Send to the team:
- “Please monitor foreign currency balances and exchange exposure regularly.”
Key point:
- Exchange movements can change the real value of cash held in another currency.
Official links:
- IMF Financial Soundness Indicators
- GAO Green Book
96. Q: How should foreign currency bank balances be monitored?
A: Foreign currency bank balances should be monitored by account, by currency, by exchange rate assumption, and by expected use date. Treasury should know not only how many foreign currency units are on hand but also what they mean in the reporting or operating currency. Monitoring should therefore include the local-currency equivalent, recent exchange movement, any restrictions on conversion, and the timing of planned payments. If the organization holds foreign currency for a donor project, it should also consider whether the currency is needed later for the same obligation or whether it should be converted earlier to reduce exposure. Monthly or weekly review may be enough for some organizations, but volatile balances may require more frequent attention. The key is to avoid treating foreign currency as if it were static cash with no valuation risk. It is cash, but it is also a moving target when exchange rates change.
Send to the team:
- “Please review foreign currency balances and revalue them where required by policy.”
Key point:
- Currency balances need both operational and valuation monitoring.
Official links:
- IMF Financial Soundness Indicators
- GAO Green Book
- FASB Accounting Standards Updates
97. Q: What is cash reporting to management?
A: Cash reporting to management is the regular presentation of current balances, expected inflows, expected outflows, reserve position, and key exceptions so leadership can make informed decisions. It is not just a finance report for recordkeeping; it is a decision support tool. Management needs to know whether cash is stable, tightening, improving, or at risk, and it needs enough detail to act on that information. A strong report will show actual bank balances, the forecast for the coming weeks or months, major payments due, major receipts expected, restricted versus unrestricted cash where relevant, and any exceptions that could affect continuity. In an NGO, this report is especially useful because leaders often need to balance service delivery, donor commitments, and staffing decisions at the same time. Clear cash reporting helps avoid surprises and supports transparent communication among finance, program, and leadership teams.
Send to the team:
- “Please provide management with a clear cash position report on a regular schedule.”
Key point:
