- Management needs timely cash information to make practical operating decisions.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- IMF Financial Soundness Indicators
98. Q: What should a liquidity dashboard include?
A: A liquidity dashboard should give a short, clear picture of the organization’s near-term cash health. It should include current bank balances, forecast inflows and outflows, upcoming payroll, major supplier payments, reserve status, restricted and unrestricted cash where relevant, and any warning flags for negative coverage or delayed funding. A good dashboard is not overloaded with detail; it shows the few pieces of information leadership needs most urgently. It should also be updated often enough that the information still feels current when a decision is made. In an NGO, the dashboard can help management answer practical questions such as whether a new activity can start, whether an invoice can be paid, or whether a donor follow-up is needed. If the organization works across multiple accounts or entities, the dashboard should either consolidate them or clearly separate them so the real liquidity position is not hidden. The best dashboard makes future pressure visible, not just current bank balance.
Send to the team:
- “Please build the dashboard around the next 4 to 12 weeks of cash needs.”
Key point:
- A good liquidity dashboard shows current position and future pressure in one view.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- IMF Financial Soundness Indicators
99. Q: How can strong cash controls improve nonprofit resilience?
A: Strong cash controls improve nonprofit resilience because they make it harder for money to be lost, harder for risk to remain hidden, and easier for management to respond to funding shocks. A resilient NGO can see its cash position early, protect its reserves, sequence its payments, and communicate clearly with donors, staff, and suppliers when pressure appears. Strong controls also improve credibility, because funders and boards are more likely to trust an organization that can show where cash is, how it moved, and what risks are emerging. Resilience is not only about having more money; it is also about knowing how much money is available, when it will arrive, and how long the organization can continue if something goes wrong. Good control builds that knowledge. It also makes the organization less dependent on last-minute heroics by one finance person because the process is visible, repeatable, and reviewable.
Send to the team:
- “Please treat cash control as a resilience tool, not just a bookkeeping task.”
Key point:
- Good cash control strengthens the organization’s ability to absorb shocks and continue operations.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- IMF Financial Soundness Indicators
- GAO internal control standards
100. Q: What does good cash, treasury, banking, and liquidity management look like in practice?
A: Good cash, treasury, banking, and liquidity management is disciplined, forward-looking, and documented from end to end. It begins with accurate cash forecasting, both for inflows and outflows, and continues through controlled bank access, secure custody of instruments, prompt posting of receipts, properly approved disbursements, and regular bank reconciliation. It also includes reserve management, payment prioritization, review of batch payments, monitoring of foreign currency exposure, and regular reporting to management. In a nonprofit, the treasury function must balance control and mission delivery. It should be strict enough to protect donor funds and flexible enough to keep programs moving. Good practice means the organization knows what cash it has, what cash it expects, what cash it owes, what cash is restricted, and what risks could affect the next payment cycle. When all of that is visible, management can make decisions with confidence instead of guesswork.
Send to the team:
- “Please manage cash through forecast, control, reconciliation, and reporting every period.”
Key point:
- Good treasury practice combines planning, control, and timely reporting into one operating system.
Official links:
- GAO Green Book
- GAO internal control standards
- IRS Publication 557
- IMF Financial Soundness Indicators
